باب ما يجوز للمكاتب أن يفعله وما لا يجوز
للمكاتب البيع والشراء والسفر لأن مقصود السيد من العقد الوصول إلى بدل الكتابة ومقصود العبد به الوصول إلى الحرية وذلك إنما يحصل بالبيع والشراء وقد لا يتفقان في الحضر فاحتاج إلى السفر ويملك البيع بالمحاباة لأن عادة التجار يفعلونه إظهاراً للمسامحة واستجلاباً لقلوب الناس وقد يخابي في صفقة ليربح في أخرى. وأفاد إطلاقه أنه يملك أن يبيع بالنقد والنسيئة الغبن الفاحش واليسير عند الإمام، وعندهما لا يملك بالغبن الفاحش كالعبد المأذون له، ولو زاد في الثمن أو حط بسبب عيب جاز، ولو حط من غير عيب لا يجوز، وشراء المكاتب وبيعه من مولاه جائز. وإذا اشترى شيئاً من مال المضاربة ولا ربح فيه جاز ولا المولى ما يبيع اشترى من مكاتبه مرابحة ما لم يبين لقيام شبهة الملك له فيه. ولو أوصى بعين من ماله عتق فأجاز الوصية جازت؛ كذا في المحيط. وفي المبسوط ولو باع من مكاتبه درهماً بدرهمين لا تجوز لأن هذا صريح الربا والمكاتب في كسبه بمنزلة الحر، والمكاتب في حق الشفعة فيما يستحقه أو استحق عليه كالحر ا هـ . ولا يقال هذه الأحكام علمت من قوله خرج من يده دون ملكه فيكون تكراراً لأنا نقول : علمت هناك وإن رهن أو ارتهن أو أجر أو استأجر فهو جائز ليس له أن يقرض ضمناً لا تصريحاً ما علم ضمناً لا يكون مكرراً فتأمله . وفي المبسوط . ولو زنى المكاتب أو سرق منه يجب القطع لأنه يخاطب ا هـ. 
قال رحمه الله : (وإن شرط أن لا يخرج من المصر) ((أن) هذه وصلية وهذا الكلام متصل بما قبله يعني له أن يسافر وإن شرط المولى عليه أن لا يخرج من البلد كما لو خص له نوعاً من التصرف دون غيره كان ذلك باطلاً لأن هذه الشروط مخالفة لما اقتضى عقد الكتابة لأن مقتضاها فك الحجر على وجه الاستمداد والاختصاص بنفسه ومنافع نفسه واكتسابه وأن لا يتحكم عليه أحد ويحصل المال بأي وجه شاء فكانت هذه الشروط باطلة والسفر مظنة تحصيل المال . قال الله تعالى وآخرون يضربون في الأرض يبتغون من فضل الله ﴾ [المزمل: ٢٠] والكتابة لا تبطل بالشروط الفاسدة كما تقدم إلا إذا كان داخلاً في صلب العقد وهو أن يكون في البدل مثل أن يشترط خدمته أو مكاتبته على خمر أو خنزير فيفسد العقد لأن الكتابة تشبه البيع من حيث إنها تحتمل الفسخ قبل أداء البدل فيفسد العقد إذا وجد الشرط في صلب العقد، وتشبه النكاح من حيث إنها لا تحتمل الفسخ بعد الأداء لأنها مبادلة مال بمال في حق المولى ومبادلة مال بغير مال في حق العبد لأنه لا يملك نفسه فلا يفسد العقد بالشروط إذ لم يكن في صلب العقد كما هنا. قال في العتابية : والتمكن في صلب العقد أن هو يدخل في أحد البدلين والذي ليس في صلب العقد هو الذي ليس في بدل الكتابة ولا فيما يقابله، وقد رد عليه بعض العلماء بأن قوله ولا فيما يقابله» ممنوع فإن مقابلة فك الحجر وحرمة المنع من الخروج تخصيص للفك والحرية فتأمل. أقول : ليس ذلك بشيء لأن كون المنع من الخروج تخصيصاً للفك والحرية لا يقتضي كونه داخلاً فيها فإن تخصيص الشيء قد يكون بأمر خارج عنه أخص منه كما إذا عرفنا الإنسان بالحيوان الضاحك فتأمل ا هـ . 
رحمه الله : ( وتزويج أمته) للمكاتب أن يعني الأمة لأنه يزوج الاكتساب فيملكه ضرورة بخلاف تزويج المكاتبة نفسها حيث لا يجوز لها وأن كان فيه اكتساب لأن ملك المولى باق فيها فمنعها من الاستبداد بنفسها وفيه تعيبها، وربما يعجز فيبقى هذا العيب فيكون على المولى ..ضرر وليس مقصودها بتزويج نفسها المال وإنما هو التحصين والإعفاف بخلاف تزويج أمتها فإن المقصود منه كسب المال فيجوز لها كما يجوز للأب والوصي بخلاف العبد المأذون له في التجارة والمضارب والشريك لأنهم لا يملكون إلا ما يكون من باب التجارة والتزويج ليس منها فلا يملكونه. وبهذا التقرير ظهر الفرق بين تزويج المكاتبة ن مها حيث لا يجوز وإن كان فيه اكتساب المهر ودفع النفقة كما في تزويج المكاتب أمة نفسه لأن العلة في تزويج المكاتبة نفسها مركبة بما ذكرناه فتأمل. قيد بالأمة لأن المكاتب لا يملك أن يزوج نفسه وولده لأنه ليس من التجارة ولا فيه اكتساب مال بل فيه شغل رقبته بالمهر والنفقة. وفي المحيط : زوج امرأة فأعتق فأجاز لم يجز لأن هذا العقد لا له حال وقوعه لأن الكتابة توجب فك الحجر في الاكتساب وهذا ليس منها بخلاف ما لو كفل مالاً ثم أعتق نفذت كفالته، وكذا لو وكل فعتق جاز وكذا لو أوصى لعبد فأعتق فأجاز لأن هذه العقود لها مجيز حال وقوعها وإنما يمنع ظهورها في حق غيره فسقط حق الغير بالعتق فظهر النفاذ مطلقاً. ولا تجوز هبة المكاتب وصدقته ووصيته وكفالته في الحال ولو أعتق ترد له الهبة والصدقة لأنها وقعت فاسدة ولو دفع مضاربة أو أخذ مالاً مضاربة جاز، ويجوز له شركة العنان لا قال
Chapter of what is permissible for the mukatab to do and what is not. The mukatab has the right of sale, purchase, and travel, because the master's purpose from the contract is to obtain the kitabah payment, and the slave's purpose is to attain freedom, and that is only achieved through sale and purchase, and these may not both occur in the city, so he needed to travel. He has the right to sell at a loss (muhabah) because it is the custom of merchants to do so, showing leniency and attracting people's hearts, and he may lose in one transaction to profit in another. Its unrestricted expression indicates that he may sell for cash or credit, with gross or slight fraud according to Imam Abu Hanifa; according to the two companions, he does not have the right to gross fraud, like the authorized slave. If he increases the price or reduces due to a defect, it is permissible; if he reduces without a defect, it is not permissible. The purchase and sale of the mukatab from his master are permissible. If he buys something from mudarabah capital with no profit, it is permissible. The master may not sell and buy from his mukatab on a profit basis unless he discloses, due to the semblance of ownership he has over it. If he bequeaths a specific item from his property and then becomes free and accepts the bequest, it is permissible; as in al-Muhit. In al-Mabsut: if he sells a dirham to his mukatab for two dirhams, it is not permissible because this is clear riba, and the mukatab in his earning is like a free person; the mukatab in the right of pre-emption regarding what he acquires or what is acquired from him is like a free person. It cannot be said that these rulings are known from his statement 'it left his hand without his ownership', thus it would be repetition, because we say: they are known there implicitly. If he pawns or takes a pawn, leases or hires, it is permissible. He does not have the right to lend, implicitly not explicitly; what is known implicitly is not repetition, so reflect. In al-Mabsut: if the mukatab commits zina or steals, the hadd punishment is obligatory because he is addressed. He (author) said: (and if he stipulates that he not leave the city) - this 'an' is a conjunction, and this speech is connected to what preceded, meaning he may travel even if the master stipulated that he not leave the city, just as if he specified a type of transaction for him to the exclusion of others, that would be void, because these conditions contradict what the kitabah contract necessitates, for its requirements are the removal of restriction for the purpose of seeking sustenance and specialization in himself and his own usufructs and earnings, and that no one has authority over him, and that he may obtain wealth by any means he wishes. Thus these conditions are void, and travel is a likely means of obtaining wealth. Allah said: 'And others traveling in the land seeking the bounty of Allah' [Quran 73:20]. Kitabah is not invalidated by void conditions, as previously mentioned, unless it enters into the core of the contract, such as stipulating his service or making the kitabah for wine or pork, then the contract becomes void because kitabah resembles sale in that it can be rescinded before payment of the substitute, so the contract becomes void if the condition is in the core of the contract; and it resembles marriage in that it cannot be rescinded after payment, because it is an exchange of property for property from the master's side, and an exchange of property for non-property from the slave's side because he does not own himself, so the contract is not invalidated by conditions if they are not in the core, as here. He said in al-Atabiyyah: 'Being in the core of the contract is that it enters into one of the two substitutes, and what is not in the core is that which is not in the substitute of kitabah nor in its counterpart.' Some scholars objected to his statement 'nor in its counterpart', saying it is unacceptable, for the counterpart of removal of restriction and prohibition of exit is a specification of the removal and freedom, so reflect. I say: that is nothing, because the prohibition of exit being a specification of removal and freedom does not necessitate its being included in them; for specification of something may be by an external matter more specific than it, as when we define man as the laughing animal, so reflect. He (author) said: (and marrying his slave-girl) - the mukatab may marry off his slave-girl because it involves earning, so he owns it out of necessity, unlike the marriage of the mukatabah herself, which is not permissible for her even though it involves earning, because the master's ownership remains in her, so she is prevented from acting independently regarding herself, and it involves demeaning her, and perhaps she may become unable, so this defect remains, causing harm to the master. Her purpose in marrying herself is not wealth but rather seeking chastity and modesty, unlike marrying her slave-girl, for the purpose is earning wealth, so it is permissible for her, just as it is permissible for the father and executor, unlike the authorized slave in trade, the mudarib, and the partner, because they only own what is of the nature of trade, and marriage is not from it, so they do not own it. With this explanation, the difference between the mukatabah marrying off her slave-girl, which is permissible, and her marrying herself, which is not permissible even though it involves earning the dowry and paying maintenance, becomes clear, as in the case of a mukatab marrying off his own slave-girl, because the cause for the prohibition of the mukatabah marrying herself is composite as we mentioned, so reflect. He restricted it to 'his slave-girl' because the mukatab does not have the right to marry himself or his child, for it is not from trade and does not involve earning wealth; rather it involves burdening his neck with dowry and maintenance. In al-Muhit: if he married a woman and then became free and accepted, it is not permissible, because this contract had no validator at the time of its occurrence, for kitabah necessitates removal of restriction in earning, and this is not from it, unlike if he guaranteed a debt and then became free, his guarantee is effective; likewise if he appointed an agent and then became free, it is permissible; likewise if he was bequeathed a slave and then became free and accepted, because these contracts have a validator at the time of their occurrence; the prevention only appears in relation to others, so the right of others falls by freedom, and effect appears absolutely. The gift, charity, bequest, and guarantee of the mukatab are not valid at present; if he becomes free, the gift and charity are returned to him because they occurred void. If he gives or takes capital as mudarabah, it is permissible. He is allowed to have a partnership of equal shares ('inan), not...
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof passage from the chapter on what a mukatab is permitted to do and what not. The author expands on the matn by explaining the rationale and providing details. The passage covers several key points:

1. The mukatab has the right to sell, buy, and travel because the underlying purposes of the kitabah contract are for the master to receive payment and for the slave to earn money to become free. Trade and travel are necessary means to that end. The mukatab may also sell at a loss (muhabah) as it is customary among merchants to build goodwill. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the mukatab may sell with gross or slight fraud; but according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, he may not engage in gross fraud, akin to an authorized slave. Increasing or decreasing the price due to defect is allowed, but an unjustified reduction is not. Transactions between the mukatab and his master are valid. Special rules apply to riba and pre-emption.

2. If the master stipulates that the mukatab not leave the city, this condition is void because it contradicts the essence of kitabah, which requires freedom of movement for earning. The author cites Quran 73:20 as evidence that travel is a means of seeking Allah's bounty. The condition being void does not invalidate the kitabah contract itself, as long as it is not a core condition (e.g., stipulating service or payment in wine/pork), which would render the contract void. The distinction is that kitabah resembles sale in being rescindable before payment, but resembles marriage after payment, so only conditions in the core affect validity.

3. The mukatab may marry off his slave-girl because it is a means of earning (through the dowry). However, he cannot marry himself or his child because that is not a trade and burdens him with financial obligations. Similarly, a female mukatabah cannot marry herself off because it demeans her and may harm the master, though she can marry off her slave-girl. The distinction is based on the purpose: earning vs. chastity. Other contracts like gift, charity, bequest, and guarantee are invalid while he is a mukatab, though they may become effective after freedom under certain conditions. Mudarabah and partnership of equal shares ('inan) are permissible.

The commentary adheres strictly to the text, explaining each ruling with the author's reasoning, without importing external rulings.

Key Terms
تزويج الأمة (tazwij al-ama): The act of a master marrying off his female slave to a free man, receiving a dowry. For a mukatab, this is permissible as a means of earning.
المكاتبة (al-mukatabah): A female slave under a kitabah contract. She cannot marry herself but can marry off her own slave-girl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a female mukatabah prohibited from marrying herself, according to the text?
A) Because she does not have the financial means to pay the dowry.
B) Because her purpose is chastity, not wealth, and it may harm the master.
C) Because she can only marry with the master's permission.
D) Because marriage is not permissible for any slave.

Key Takeaway
The mukatab's earning capacity extends to marrying off his slave-girl, but not to his own marriage or other non-earning contracts.

Nikah — Marriage

Mukatab Giving His Slave Woman in Marriage
Ruling with rational proof on mukatab's marriage authority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a mukatab giving his slave in marriage, explain why a mukataba cannot marry herself, and distinguish the underlying causes of the difference.

Core Idea
A mukatab may give his slave in marriage to earn; a mukataba may not marry herself as it harms her master.

In this passage, the author of al-Bahr al-Raiq explains a key distinction in the Hanafi fiqh of kitaba. While a mukatab (male slave under kitaba) is permitted to give his female slave in marriage to generate earnings, a mukataba (female slave under kitaba) is strictly prohibited from marrying herself off. The reasoning turns on the purpose of the act and the harm it causes to the master. Let us unpack the analytical layers behind this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
First, note the two distinct rulings in the passage: (a) the mukatab may give his female slave in marriage (jāʾiz), and (b) the mukataba may not give herself in marriage (ghayr jāʾiz). Mark these as the central legal determinations.
The author explicitly contrasts the two cases.
2
Locate the proofs (dalīl ʿaqlī)
The proofs are rational, not textual. For the mukatab: the act is a means of earning (iktisāb), which is the very purpose of the kitaba contract. For the mukataba: even though there is potential earning through mahr and maintenance, the harm (ḍarar) to the master—due to ongoing ownership, defect (ʿayb) from marriage, and potential burden if she becomes unable—prohibits it.
Also note the contrasting intentions: the mukatab seeks wealth; the mukataba seeks protection (taḥṣīn) and chastity (iʿfāf).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalālah
The link between the proof and the ruling: The principle of kitaba is to enable the slave to earn and purchase freedom. Allowing the mukatab to marry off his slave generates income directly serving this purpose. For the mukataba, marrying herself does not primarily serve earning; it creates a permanent defect and liability for the master, contradicting the purpose of kitaba which is to benefit both parties without harm.
The passage explicitly says 'the cause is compounded by what we mentioned'.
4
Contrast with other parties
The author compares with an authorized slave (maʾdhūn), mudārib, and sharīk—they cannot perform marriages because marriage is not from the category of trade (tijāra). This sharpens the distinction: the mukatab's capacity is broader, encompassing any means of earning, not just trade.
This comparative step confirms the specific scope of the mukatab's authority.
5
Consider cross-madhhab ikhtilāf
Other schools (e.g., Shafiʿi) may permit the mukataba to marry herself if there is no harm. The Hanafi position is that the potential harm (ʿayb, burden) is inherent, so it is prohibited. The practical consequence (thamara) is that in the Hanafi school, a mukataba's marriage without master's permission is invalid and requires annulment.
This step highlights the school's caution based on the master's rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وتزويج أمته) للمكاتب أن يعني الأمة لأنه يزوج الاكتساب فيملكه ضرورة بخلاف تزويج المكاتبة نفسها حيث لا يجوز لها وأن كان فيه اكتساب لأن ملك المولى باق فيها فمنعها من الاستبداد بنفسها وفيه تعيبها، وربما يعجز فيبقى هذا العيب فيكون على المولى ..ضرر وليس مقصودها بتزويج نفسها المال وإنما هو التحصين والإعفاف بخلاف تزويج أمتها فإن المقصود منه كسب المال فيجوز لها كما يجوز للأب والوصي بخلاف العبد المأذون له في التجارة والمضارب والشريك لأنهم لا يملكون إلا ما يكون من باب التجارة والتزويج ليس منها فلا يملكونه. وبهذا التقرير ظهر الفرق بين تزويج المكاتبة نفسها حيث لا يجوز وإن كان فيه اكتساب المهر ودفع النفقة كما في تزويج المكاتب أمة نفسه لأن العلة في تزويج المكاتبة نفسها مركبة بما ذكرناه فتأمل.
(And giving his slave woman in marriage) The mukatab may give his slave woman in marriage because he marries for earning, so he owns it by necessity, unlike the mukataba giving herself in marriage, for it is not permissible for her, even though there is earning in it, because the ownership of the master remains upon her, so she is prevented from acting independently regarding herself, and there is a defect in her, and perhaps she will become unable, so this defect remains, causing harm to the master. And her intention in marrying herself is not wealth, but rather protection and chastity. Unlike giving her slave woman in marriage, for the intention is to earn money, so it is permissible for her, just as it is permissible for the father and the executor. Unlike the slave authorized for trade, the mudarib, and the partner, for they only own what is from the category of trade, and marriage is not from it, so they do not own it. By this exposition, the difference appears between the mukataba giving herself in marriage, which is not permissible even though there is earning of mahr and payment of maintenance, as in the case of the mukatab giving his own slave woman in marriage, because the cause in the mukataba giving herself in marriage is compounded by what we mentioned, so reflect.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling that a mukatab (male slave under kitaba) is permitted to give his female slave in marriage because this act generates earnings, which is the purpose of kitaba. In contrast, a mukataba (female slave under kitaba) is not permitted to marry herself off, even though there is potential earning through mahr and maintenance, because her master still owns her and marriage causes harm to the master (defect, potential burden). The author distinguishes between the two by noting that the mukataba's intention is protection, not earnings, while the mukatab's intention in giving his slave is earnings. The passage also contrasts with other parties like the authorized slave, mudarib, and partner who cannot engage in marriage because it is not a trade. The key distinction is based on the type of benefit: for the mukatab, it is pure financial gain; for the mukataba, it is personal and harmful to the master.

Key Terms
تزويج الأمة (tazwīj al-ama): The act of giving one's female slave in marriage to a man.
المكاتبة (al-mukātaba): A female slave who has entered into a kitaba contract with her master.
الاكتساب (al-iktisāb): Earning or acquiring wealth, which is the primary purpose of the kitaba contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is it permissible for a mukatab to give his slave woman in marriage but not for a mukataba to give herself?
A) Because the mukatab has more rights over his property.
B) Because the mukatab's act is for earning, while the mukataba's causes harm to her master and her intention is protection.
C) Because marriage is a form of trade only for men.
D) Because the mukataba does not own herself at all.

Key Takeaway
A mukatab may give his slave in marriage as a means of earning; a mukataba cannot marry herself because it harms the master and does not serve the earning purpose of kitaba.

The Mukatab's Incapacity to Marry: Ruling and Rationale
Restriction: mukatab's marriage incapacity and its reason

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a mukatab marrying himself or his child and explain the underlying reasoning based on the purpose of the kitaba contract.

Core Idea
The mukatab cannot marry because marriage burdens him financially, contrary to the kitaba's goal of earning freedom.

The previous passage restricted a discussion to a slave woman (al-ummah). This passage clarifies why: the mukatab — a slave who has contracted to earn his freedom — lacks the legal capacity to contract marriage for himself or his child. The reason is not that he is unfree per se, but that marriage is not a commercial activity and imposes financial obligations (mahr and maintenance) that hinder his ability to pay his kitaba. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal subject
The passage discusses a specific type of slave: al-mukatab. Confirm the meaning: a slave who has a kitaba contract, obligated to pay a sum to his master in exchange for freedom. He has limited rights but is not fully free.
The mukatab is in a transitional state between slavery and freedom.
2
Identify the ruling
The passage states that the mukatab 'does not have the capacity' (la yamlik) to marry himself or his child. This implies the marriage contract is invalid (batil) if performed without the master's permission, and even with permission it may be discouraged due to the burden.
The hukm level is batil: the marriage does not produce legal effects.
3
Analyze the rationale
The passage gives two reasons: (1) marriage is not from trade (tijara) and does not involve earning money (iktisab mal), (2) marriage occupies his neck (i.e., incurs liability) with mahr and maintenance (nafaqa). This burdens him, contradicting the purpose of kitaba, which is to enable him to earn and pay off his debt.
The reasoning is based on maslaha: protecting the mukatab from additional debt that would impede his freedom.
4
Locate the source authority
This ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah, the core transmitted corpus of the Hanafi school. There is no dispute among Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani on this point.
This makes the ruling strong and reliable within the school.
5
Apply to a new scenario
Consider a mukatab who marries without his master's permission. According to this passage, the marriage is invalid. What about if he marries with permission? The passage implies even with permission, the burden remains, so some Hanafi scholars consider it makruh or invalid if it would impair his ability to pay.
The operative fatwa (mufta bihi) is that such a marriage is invalid if it burdens him.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيد بالأمة لأن المكاتب لا يملك أن يزوج نفسه وولده لأنه ليس من التجارة ولا فيه اكتساب مال بل فيه شغل رقبته بالمهر والنفقة.
It is restricted to a slave woman because the mukatab does not have the capacity to marry himself or his child, for it is not from trade nor does it involve earning money, rather it involves occupying his neck with mahr and maintenance.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage provides the reasoning behind the previous restriction: the mukatab cannot marry himself or his child because marriage is not a commercial activity and does not bring in earnings; instead, it imposes financial obligations (mahr and maintenance) on him. Since the purpose of kitaba is to enable the slave to earn money for freedom, marriage goes against that purpose by burdening him. Therefore, the ruling is that the mukatab lacks the legal capacity to contract marriage for himself or his child, and any such marriage performed without permission is invalid.

Key Terms
المكاتب (al-mukātab): A male slave who has entered into a kitaba contract with his master, obligated to pay a sum for freedom.
تزويج النفس (tazwīj al-nafs): Marrying oneself (i.e., the mukatab entering into his own marriage contract).
شغل الرقبة (shughl al-raqaba): Occupying the neck; a metaphor for incurring financial liability or debt.
المهر والنفقة (al-mahr wa al-nafaqa): Dower (mahr) and maintenance (nafaqa) – financial obligations arising from marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Why does the mukatab lack capacity to marry himself according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because he is not free.
B) Because marriage is not a trade and burdens him with mahr and maintenance.
C) Because he cannot afford the mahr.
D) Because his master forbids it.

Key Takeaway
The mukatab's marriage is invalid because marriage imposes financial burdens that conflict with the goal of achieving freedom through kitaba.

Marriage of a Mukatab Cannot Be Ratified After Freedom
Ruling that marriage contracted by mukatab is not validated by later freedom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a marriage contracted by a mukatab is void, explain the rationale, and distinguish it from other contracts that become valid upon emancipation.

Core Idea
A marriage by a mukatab is void ab initio—no later ratification can cure it.

This passage from al-Muhit addresses a critical distinction in Hanafi fiqh: some contracts made by a mukatab can become valid upon freedom, but marriage is not one of them. The reason lies in the nature of the contract and the capacity at formation. We will explore the ruling, its rationale, and how it compares with guarantee, agency, and bequest.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the act is a marriage contract entered by a mukatab without permission. The ruling is that it is void (batil) and ratification after freedom does not make it valid.
The contract is completely invalid from the start.
2
Understand the legal basis
Kitaba only lifts the restriction (hajr) on earning (iktisab) to allow the mukatab to engage in commercial activities. Marriage is not a commercial activity, so the mukatab lacks capacity for marriage without the master's permission. Therefore, the contract had no potential for validity at its time of occurrence.
The key is that the contract is outside the scope of kitaba's permission.
3
Contrast with other contracts
Examine the exceptions: kafala (guarantee), wakala (agency), and wasiyya (bequest to a slave). In each, the contract had a potential validator (mujiz) at the time of occurrence; its effectiveness was merely prevented by the master's right. When that right is removed by emancipation, the contract becomes effective.
These contracts are of a financial nature and fall within the kitaba's permission for earning.
4
Apply the principle
Determine whether any contract entered by a mukatab has a potential for validity. If it is a financial contract related to earning, it may become valid upon freedom. If it is a personal status contract like marriage, it cannot.
This distinction governs all similar cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المحيط : زوج امرأة فأعتق فأجاز لم يجز لأن هذا العقد لا له حال وقوعه لأن الكتابة توجب فك الحجر في الاكتساب وهذا ليس منها بخلاف ما لو كفل مالاً ثم أعتق نفذت كفالته، وكذا لو وكل فعتق جاز وكذا لو أوصى لعبد فأعتق فأجاز لأن هذه العقود لها مجيز حال وقوعها وإنما يمنع ظهورها في حق غيره فسقط حق الغير بالعتق فظهر النفاذ مطلقاً.
And in al-Muhit: A man married a woman, then was freed, and he ratified (the marriage), it is not permissible, because this contract had no validity at the time of its occurrence, for kitaba requires lifting of restriction in earning, and this is not from it. Unlike if he guaranteed money, then was freed, his guarantee is effective. Similarly, if he appointed an agent, then was freed, it is permissible. Similarly, if he made a bequest to a slave, then was freed and ratified, because these contracts have a validator at the time of their occurrence, but its effectiveness is prevented in respect of others. When the right of others is removed by emancipation, the effectiveness appears absolutely.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage from al-Muhit discusses a scenario where a mukatab enters into a marriage contract, then becomes free, and then ratifies the marriage. The ruling is that the ratification is invalid because at the time of the marriage, the contract was void due to the mukatab's lack of capacity. The author contrasts this with other contracts like guarantee (kafala), agency (wakala), or bequest (wasiyya) which, although entered into during kitaba, can become valid upon freedom because they have a potential validator at the time. The difference is that marriage is not a commercial transaction and the mukatab had no capacity at all, whereas in the other contracts, there was a right of the master that prevented effectiveness, and when that right is removed by freedom, the contract becomes effective.

Key Terms
الإجازة (al-ijāza): Ratification or approval of a contract after its occurrence.
الكفالة (al-kafāla): Guarantee to pay a debt or produce a person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if a mukatab marries without permission and later becomes free?
A) The marriage becomes valid automatically.
B) The marriage can be ratified and becomes valid.
C) The marriage remains invalid even if ratified.
D) It depends on the master's consent at the time of marriage.

Key Takeaway
A contract that is void ab initio cannot be cured by subsequent ratification; this applies to a mukatab's marriage but not to financial contracts that had potential validity.

Mukatab cannot marry his male slave
Ruling that mukatab cannot marry off his slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a mukatab marrying his male slave is batil and explain the talil based on the limitation of the mukatab's authority to acts of acquisition.

Core Idea
The mukatab's authority is restricted to acquisition; marrying a male slave reduces his value and is invalid.

This lesson examines a specific restriction on the mukatab's authority over his own slaves. While a free master has broad powers, the mukatab's ownership is qualified by the purpose of kitabah—to earn his freedom. As we will see, marrying off a male slave is not an act of acquisition and harms the slave's earning potential, rendering the act batil.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Identify what act is being ruled upon (tazwij of male slave by mukatab) and what the hukm is (batil).
The act is clearly stated: 'لا يملك تزويج عبده' – he does not own the right to marry off his slave.
2
Understand the talil (reasoning)
Read the reason given: it causes a defect ('ayb) and reduces wealth because it encumbers the slave with mahr and nafaqah, and it is not from the category of acquisition (iktisab).
The talil is explicit: 'لأنه تعيب له ونقص في المال لكونه شاغلاً للرقبة بالمهر والنفقة'.
3
Recognize the exception for female slaves
Note that the ruling contrasts with marrying a female slave, which is allowed because it can be a form of acquisition (offspring).
The text says: 'بخلاف تزويج الأمة على ما بينا' – unlike marrying a female slave, as we explained.
4
Derive the underlying principle
From this, extract the principle: the mukatab's actions must be acts of acquisition that help him earn his freedom; actions that harm the slave's earning capacity are prohibited.
This principle governs many rulings on mukatab's dealings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( وتزويج عبده) يعني لا يملك تزويج عبده، وكذا لا يملك أن يوكل به لأنه تعيب له ونقص في المال لكونه شاغلاً للرقبة بالمهر والنفقة وليس هو من باب الاكتساب في شيء بخلاف تزويج الأمة على ما بينا.
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And marrying off his slave) meaning he does not own the right to marry off his slave, and likewise he does not own the right to appoint an agent for it because it is a defect and a reduction in wealth, as it encumbers the neck with dower and maintenance, and it is not from the category of acquisition at all, unlike marrying off a female slave as we explained.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage gives the ruling that the mukatab does not have the right to marry off his male slave, nor to appoint an agent for that. The reason is that marrying off a slave causes a defect ('ayb) and reduces the slave's value because it imposes obligations of mahr and nafaqah on the slave's neck, which hinders his ability to earn for the mukatab's kitabah. This is not an act of acquisition (iktisab), which is the only type of action the mukatab is permitted to perform. By contrast, marrying off a female slave is allowed because it can be a source of profit through her offspring, as mentioned earlier.

Key Terms
تزويج (tazwīj): The act of marrying off someone (here, a slave) by the owner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is a mukatab's act of marrying his male slave considered batil?
A) Because the slave does not consent.
B) Because it reduces the slave's value and is not acquisition.
C) Because the mukatab does not own the slave.
D) Because marriage is not allowed for slaves.

Key Takeaway
The mukatab may only perform acts of acquisition; marrying a male slave harms his earning capacity and is thus invalid.

Nikah — Marriage

Mukatab Marries a Free Claimant: Ruling on the Child
Commentary on intra-Hanafi dispute regarding child of mukatab marrying a free claimant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the three Hanafi positions on the child's status when a mukatab or ma'dhun marries a free claimant, and apply the ruling to determine liability for compensation.

Core Idea
A child born to a slave mother is a slave, even if she claimed freedom, but the deceived husband may recover compensation after emancipation.

In this passage, Al-Marghinani addresses a nuanced case within marriage law: a mukatab (a slave under a kitabah contract) or a ma'dhun (a slave permitted to trade) marries a woman who claims to be free. After the marriage, the woman is claimed as a slave. What is the status of their child? The Hanafi imams differ, and the commentator explores their reasoning, shedding light on principles of lineage, compensation, and the effect of deception (gharur). This lesson will guide you through the dispute and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the scenario
Determine who the husband is (mukatab or ma'dhun) and the conditions: he married with the master's permission, the woman claimed to be free, she gave birth, then she was claimed as a slave by a third party.
The master's permission is a key factor affecting liability for dower and compensation.
2
State the ruling of Imam Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf
According to Imam Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the 'two' or 'lahuma'), the child is a slave. The husband cannot take the child by paying its value. Their proof: the child follows the mother in freedom or slavery; since the mother is a slave, the child is a slave.
This is based on the general principle that lineage follows the mother's status.
3
State the ruling of Muhammad al-Shaybani
Muhammad holds that the child is free, but the husband must pay the value to the claimant immediately if the marriage was with the master's permission; if without permission, after emancipation. He analogizes to a free man deceived by a free claimant, relying on the consensus of the Companions.
Muhammad emphasizes the husband's deception (gharur) and his reliance on the woman's claim.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response to Muhammad's proof
The two (Imam and Abu Yusuf) counter that a slave is not analogous to a free man because in the free man's case, compensation is immediate, while for a slave it is deferred until after emancipation, breaking the analogy.
The commentator finds this response problematic because the debts of a slave with the master's permission are due immediately.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamar al-khilaf)
If the child is a slave, it belongs to the claimant; if free, the husband must pay its value to the claimant. The husband may recover the value from the deceptive woman after emancipation if she deceived him.
This difference affects whether the claimant gets the child or compensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

With her claim, she gave birth, then she was claimed, so her child is a slave; and if he has intercourse with a slave woman by purchase and she is claimed, or by a fasid (voidable) purchase and she is returned, then the 'aqr (compensation) is [due] in the state of kitabah; and if by marriage, it is taken after emancipation. Likewise, if a ma'dhun slave or a non-ma'dhun or a mukatab deceived him, he recovers from them after emancipation, and it is not enforceable against the master. Unlike the case of sale, because the master's permission in sale includes the sale even if fasid, so they differ. Likewise, a ma'dhun slave for trade, because this is from the chapter of trade, and transactions sometimes occur validly and sometimes invalidly, and kitabah and permission encompass sale and purchase of both types, so they are permitted in both like an agent for both, so it appears against the master and he is held liable immediately. He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (And if by marriage, it is taken after emancipation) meaning if the mukatab marries a woman without the master's permission and has intercourse with her, he is held liable for the 'aqr after emancipation. Commentary
This passage is the commentary on the third matn, which deals with a mukatab or a ma'dhun slave (permitted to trade) who marries a woman claiming to be free, with the master's permission. The woman gives birth, then is claimed as a slave by a third party. The ruling on the child is disputed within the Hanafi school. According to Imam Abu Hanifa and the second (likely Abu Yusuf), the child is a slave and the husband cannot take the child by paying its value. According to the third (Muhammad al-Shaybani), the child is free, but the husband must pay the value to the claimant immediately if the marriage was with the master's permission; if without permission, he pays after emancipation. The commentary then discusses the implications for the dower (mahr) and the concept of gharur (deception). Muhammad's view is that the husband is deceived (maghrur) and can recover the value from the woman after emancipation if she deceived him. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad? Actually the text says 'lahuma' but it might refer to the two companions against Muhammad? The commentary explains that according to them, the child is a slave because born to two slaves. The Hanafi response to the problem of whether the master is liable for the debt is that the mukatab and ma'dhun have a degree of freedom of action (hurriyat al-yad) so they are treated like free persons regarding these liabilities, and the master's permission for marriage does not make him liable. The passage then addresses the case of a fasid purchase: if a mukatab buys a slave woman with a fasid contract and has intercourse, then returns her, he must pay 'aqr immediately because it is a trade transaction and the master's permission covers such. Finally, if a mukatab marries without permission and has intercourse, the 'aqr is due after emancipation.

Key Terms
غرور (gharur): Deception; here, the husband is deceived by the woman's claim of freedom.
عقر ('aqr): Compensation for sexual intercourse with a slave woman; it is a financial liability.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has made a contract (kitabah) with his master to purchase his freedom in installments.
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave who has been given permission by his master to engage in trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the view of Imam Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (lahuma), what is the status of the child born to a mukatab who married a free claimant with the master's permission?
A) The child is free, and the husband must pay its value immediately.
B) The child is a slave, and the husband has no right to take it by paying value.
C) The child is a slave, but the husband may purchase it from the claimant.
D) The child is free, and the husband must pay its value after emancipation.

Key Takeaway
The status of a child born to a mukatab or ma'dhun married to a free claimant depends on the actual status of the mother, not her claim; Imam Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf rule the child a slave, while Muhammad rules it free with compensation, based on differing analogies.

intermediate
8 min
Deferral of 'Aqr for Mukatab's Marriage Without Permission
Explains the deferral of 'aqr for mukatab's marriage without permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on 'aqr for a mukatab who marries without permission and explain why it is deferred, applying the distinction between trade and non-trade acts.

Core Idea
A mukatab's 'aqr for marriage without permission is deferred because marriage is not trade, unlike purchase.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies a nuance in the law of the mukatab (indentured slave). When a mukatab marries without the master's permission and consummates, the compensation ('aqr) for the woman is not due immediately but is postponed until his emancipation. This contrasts with the case of purchasing a slave woman, where 'aqr is immediate. The distinction hinges on the nature of the act: marriage is not a trade or earning activity, so it falls outside the scope of the kitabah contract and the master's liability. We will walk through the reasoning and the exceptions in this lesson.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its condition
First, note the rule: if a mukatab marries without permission and has intercourse, the 'aqr is wajib but deferred until after emancipation. This applies also to a ma'dhun (slave permitted to trade).
The key condition here is the absence of the master's permission for marriage.
2
Trace the legal reasoning (ta'lil)
The reason given is that marriage is not from earnings (kasb) nor trade (tijarah). The kitabah contract and any permission for trade do not cover marriage. Therefore, the mukatab cannot be held immediately because the liability would affect the master's interest, which is not justified.
The principle: a mukatab's actions that affect the master are only valid if they are within the scope of permission or the purposes of kitabah.
3
Contrast with the earlier case (purchase of a slave woman)
The text contrasts: if a mukatab buys a slave woman and has intercourse, then she is reclaimed, the 'aqr is due immediately. Why? Because purchase is trade, which is within the mukatab's authority, so liability attaches immediately.
This distinction is crucial: trade acts incur immediate liability; non-trade acts like marriage are deferred.
4
Analyze the exception from al-Asl
The passage also cites a scenario from al-Asl: if a mukatab commits zina and claims a shubha (mistaken belief of lawful intercourse), the hadd is dropped and 'aqr becomes due immediately. The difference is that the shubha claim does not make the act a valid marriage; it only removes the criminal penalty. Thus, the liability is immediate because no valid marriage contract protected him.
In the case of a genuine but unpermitted marriage, the shubha is stronger because there is a valid contract; hence deferral.
5
Apply the rule to analogous cases (insane person)
The text mentions the insane person: if he forces a woman, mahr is due; if she is willing, no mahr. This parallels the distinction about coercion vs. willingness in the mukatab case, reinforcing that liability depends on the victim's consent and the nature of the act.
This helps see the broader principle: compensation for intercourse depends on the presence of shubha and the woman's consent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ولو بنكاح مذ عتق) يعني لو تزوج المكاتب امرأة بغير إذن المولى فوطئها يؤاخذ بالعقر بعد العتق، وكذا المأذون له في التجارة لأن التزوج له ليس من الاكتساب ولا من التجارة لأن الكتابة كالكفالة فلا يظهر في حق المولى فلا يؤاخذ به في الحال بخلاف الفصل الأول، وبخلاف ما إذا اشترى أمة فوطئها فاستحقت حيث يؤاخذ بالعقر في الحال وفيما نحن فيه وجب العقر باعتبار شبهة النكاح وذلك ليس من التجارة في شيء ولا من الكسب ولا يتناول الإذن ولا عقد الكتابة فيؤخر ما وجب فيه إلى ما بعد العتق لعدم ولاية التزامه بهذه الطريق. وفي الأصل : إذا وقع المكاتب على امرأة كان عليه الحد وهذا ظاهر، فإن ادعى شبهة فسقط عنه الحد فإذا سقط الحد وجب العقر كما في الحر ثم أخذ بهذا المهر في الحال ولا يتأخر إلى ما بعد العتق وإن كانت مطاوعة لا يؤاخذ بالمهر للحال. ونظير هذا ما قالوا في المجنون إذا وقع على امرأة فوطئها فإن كانت مكرهة فإنه يجب عليه المهر، وإن كانت مطاوعة لا يجب عليه المهر. هذا إذا ادعى نكاحاً وأنكرت فإن صدقته لا يؤاخذ بالمهر في الحال سواء كانت مكرهة أو مطاوعة.
He said (may Allah have mercy on him): ('And if by marriage, then after emancipation.') Meaning: If the mukatab married a woman without the master's permission and had intercourse with her, he is held for the 'aqr after emancipation. Likewise the slave permitted to trade, because marriage for him is not from earnings nor from trade, because the kitabah is like a suretyship, so it does not manifest against the master, so he is not held for it immediately, unlike the first section, and unlike when he bought a slave woman and had intercourse and she was reclaimed where he is held for 'aqr immediately. In our case here, the 'aqr becomes due due to the shubha of marriage, and that is not from trade at all nor from earnings, nor does the permission or the contract of kitabah include it, so what is due is delayed until after emancipation because he has no authority to undertake it in this way. In al-Asl: If a mukatab falls upon a woman, the hadd is upon him, and this is apparent. If he claims a shubha, the hadd is dropped, and when the hadd is dropped, the 'aqr becomes due as in the free man. Then he is held for this dower immediately and it is not delayed until after emancipation. But if she was willing, he is not held for the dower immediately. The like of this is what they said about the insane person: if he falls upon a woman and has intercourse, if she was coerced, the dower is obligatory upon him; if she was willing, the dower is not obligatory. This if he claims marriage and she denies; if she affirms him, he is not held for the dower immediately, whether she was coerced or willing.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage from the commentary explains the matn 'and if by marriage, then after emancipation.' The rule is that if a mukatab marries a woman without the master's permission and has intercourse, the 'aqr (compensation) is due only after his emancipation, not immediately. This applies also to a ma'dhun slave. The reason is that marriage is not an earning activity nor trade; it is not included in the permission for trade or the kitabah contract. Therefore, the liability does not bind the master immediately. The text contrasts this with the earlier case of purchasing a slave woman, where 'aqr is immediate because purchase is trade. Additionally, the text discusses a scenario from al-Asl: if a mukatab commits zina with a woman, the hadd penalty applies. If he claims a shubha (e.g., mistaken belief of marriage), the hadd is dropped and 'aqr becomes due as with a free man. In that case, the 'aqr (or mahr) is due immediately, not deferred. The difference is that in the case of genuine marriage without permission, the 'aqr is deferred, but in the case of zina with a shubha claim, it is immediate because the shubha does not create the same protection. The text also parallels the case of an insane person, where 'aqr depends on whether the woman was coerced or willing.

Key Terms
عقر ('aqr): Compensation paid to a woman for intercourse, often equivalent to the mahr al-mithl.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered a contract of manumission (kitabah) to earn his freedom by paying installments.
شبهة (shubha): A mistaken belief that the intercourse is lawful, e.g., claiming a valid marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the 'aqr for a mukatab who marries and has intercourse without the master's permission deferred until after emancipation?
A) Because the master did not permit marriage, so the mukatab must pay after he becomes free.
B) Because marriage is not trade, and the kitabah does not authorize it.
C) Because the mukatab cannot afford to pay immediately.
D) Because the 'aqr is only due if the woman is a slave.

Key Takeaway
The 'aqr for a mukatab's marriage without permission is deferred because marriage is not trade; only trade-related acts create immediate liability.

Ruling on a Female Mukataba Giving Birth from Her Master
States the ruling and initial commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling regarding a female mukataba who gives birth from her master and explain the reasoning behind the delay of the dower.

Core Idea
A female mukataba who bears her master's child may choose to continue her kitaba or become umm walad; the dower is delayed because kitaba is akin to a surety.

This lesson examines a specific ruling on a female mukataba (a slave under contract of manumission) who gives birth to a child from her master. The Hanafi school gives her a unique choice: she may either continue her kitaba or become an umm walad (mother of her master's child). The commentary explains why the master is not immediately liable for the dower, drawing on the nature of the kitaba contract and the limitations of the permission to trade.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
What is the legal scenario? A female mukataba gives birth to a child from her master. What is her ruling? She has a choice: continue on her kitaba or become unable to pay and be considered umm walad.
2
Locate the Dalil (Proof)
The proof is the reasoning that kitaba is like kafala (surety). The master is not immediately liable because the kitaba contract does not encompass marriage or trade, and the 'aqr is due from semblance of marriage, not from trade.
The dalil is a qiyas (analogy) based on the nature of the kitaba contract.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
How does the analogy prove the ruling? Since the mukataba is not fully free, her contracts (like marriage) do not bind the master immediately. The 'aqr is delayed until after emancipation because the kitaba contract is akin to a guarantee, not a direct liability.
Compare to the case of a purchased slave-woman who is claimed—there the 'aqr is immediate because the sale was part of trade.
4
Distinguish from Similar Cases
The text distinguishes this from two other cases: (1) buying a slave-girl and having intercourse, then she is claimed—here the 'aqr is immediate; (2) a mukatab permitted to trade who marries—marriage is not included in the permission, so the master is not liable.
The key distinction: in our case, the kitaba is like kafala, so obligations from semblance of marriage are delayed.
5
Understand the Practical Consequence
The female mukataba has a choice: if she continues kitaba, she remains a slave paying installments; if she becomes umm walad, she is freed upon master's death but may have to pay the 'aqr after emancipation.
The master is not immediately required to pay the 'aqr; it is deferred until she is free.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب المكاتب باب ما يجوز للمكاتب أن يفعله وما لا يجوز فصل ولدت مكاتبة من سيدها مضت على كتابتها أو عجزت وهي أم ولد وإن كاتب أم وكذا المأذون له في التجارة لأن التزوج له ليس من الاكتساب ولا من التجارة لأن الكتابة كالكفالة فلا يظهر في حق المولى فلا يؤاخذ به في الحال بخلاف الفصل الأول، وبخلاف ما إذا اشترى أمة فوطئها فاستحقت حيث يؤاخذ بالعقر في الحال وفيما نحن فيه وجب العقر باعتبار شبهة النكاح وذلك ليس من التجارة في شيء ولا من الكسب ولا يتناول الإذن ولا عقد الكتابة فيؤخر ما وجب فيه إلى ما بعد العتق لعدم ولاية التزامه بهذه الطريق.
Kitab al-Mukatab: Chapter on what the mukatab is allowed to do and what not. Section: If a female mukataba gives birth from her master, she continues on her kitaba or becomes incapacitated and she is umm walad. And likewise the one permitted [by the master] to trade, because marriage is not from earning nor from trade, because kitaba is like kafala, so it does not become apparent in the right of the master, so he is not held liable for it immediately, unlike the first section, and unlike the case where he buys a slave-girl and has intercourse with her then she is claimed [by another], where he is held liable for the marriage dower ('aqr) immediately. And in our case here, the 'aqr becomes obligatory due to the semblance of marriage, and that is not from trade at all nor from earning, and the permission and the kitaba contract do not include it, so what becomes obligatory is delayed until after emancipation because he has no authority to undertake it in this way.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
Passage type: This is a ruling statement followed by an explanatory commentary from the sharh. The matn states the ruling: if a female mukataba gives birth to a child from her master (the master claims paternity), she has a choice: she may either continue on her kitaba contract or become incapacitated (by being unable to pay) and thereby become umm walad (mother of her master's child). The commentator then draws an analogy: a mukatab permitted to trade by his master is similar; marriage is not part of trade, so the master is not immediately liable for the dower. The sharh distinguishes this from the case of a slave-woman who is married and has intercourse, where the dower is due immediately. The key point is that because the kitaba is like a surety (kafala), it does not become apparent in the master's right, so the dower is delayed until after emancipation. The ruling is based on the principle that the kitaba contract does not cover such obligations.

Key Terms
مكاتبة (mukātaba): A female slave who has entered into a kitaba contract with her master to purchase her freedom by paying installments.
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who bears a child from her master; upon the master's death, she is automatically freed.
عقر ('aqr): The marriage dower (mahr) given to a woman when there is intercourse under a semblance of marriage; here it refers to the compensatory dower.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A female mukataba who bears her master's child has a choice between continuing her kitaba or becoming umm walad; the master's dower liability is deferred because the kitaba contract does not encompass such obligations.

The Female Mukataba as Umm Walad: Two Aspects and the Debate
Expounds the dual nature of the female mukataba's freedom when she becomes umm walad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the core ruling for a female mukataba who bears her master's child, explain the two aspects (kitaba and umm walad) that give her a choice, and analyse the intra-Hanafi debate regarding the survival of the kitaba payment.

Core Idea
A female mukataba who becomes umm walad gains two routes to freedom; the kitaba payment drops on the master's death.

This lesson examines the foundational ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding a female slave who is both a mukataba and becomes umm walad. The author presents two simultaneous aspects: immediate freedom with compensation (kitaba) and deferred freedom without compensation (umm walad). Taju al-Shari'a raises an objection about the consistency of retaining earnings while dropping the payment, which the commentator resolves by explaining that the kitaba is dissolved only as to the payment but remains for earnings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its two rulings
The act is a female mukataba giving birth from her master. Two rulings apply: she continues on kitaba (immediate freedom with payment) or becomes umm walad (deferred freedom without payment). She can choose.
The master's claim establishes lineage, making her umm walad.
2
Understand the two aspects
She is met by two aspects: immediate freedom with compensation (kitaba) and deferred freedom without compensation (umm walad). She may choose whichever she wishes.
Her choice determines the immediate consequences.
3
Follow the objection from Taju al-Shari'a
Taju al-Shari'a argues: if earnings and children remain with her, the kitaba payment should not drop. He claims this inconsistency shows the kitaba must fully survive.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
4
Analyse the resolution
The commentator resolves by saying the kitaba resembles both an exchange (mu'awada) and a condition (shart). Acting on the exchange aspect, earnings remain; acting on the condition aspect, payment drops. The correct answer is that the kitaba is dissolved regarding the payment but remains for earnings and children.
This resolution protects both parties' interests.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل ذكر هذه المسائل في فصل على حدة لاختصاصها بأحكام تخالف ما سبق. قال رحمه الله : (ولدت مكاتبة من سيدها مضت على كتابتها أو عجزت وهي أم ولد) لأن المولى لما ادعاه صارت أم ولد منه فتلقاها جهتا حرية عاجلة ببدل وهي الكتابة وآجلة بغير بدل وهي أمومية الولد فتختار رأيهما شاءت، ولا يحتاج إلى تصديقها لأنها مملوكة له رقبة بخلاف ما إذا ادعى ولد جارية المكاتبة حيث لا يثبت نسبه من المولى إلا بتصديق المكاتبة لأنه لا ملك له حقيقة في ملك المكاتبة وإنما له حق الملك فيحتاج فيه إلى تصديقها، فإذا مضت على الكتابة أخذت عقرها من سيدها وإذا مات المولى عتقت بالاستيلاد وسقط عنها مال الكتابة لأن العتق حصل لها بغير بدل بالاستيلاد وقال تاج الشريعة فإن قلت ينبغي أن لا يسقط عنها لأن الاكتساب تسلم لها وكذا أولادها التي اشتراها بعد الكتابة وهذا آية بقاء الكتابة قلنا : الكتابة تشبه المعاوضة وبالنظر إلى ذلك لا يسقط البدل وتشبه الشرط وبالنظر إليه يسقط . قلنا بسلامة الاكتساب عملاً بجهة المعاوضة وقلنا بسقوط البدل عملاً بجهة الشرط، ورد بأنه قد تقرر مراراً أن العمل بالشبهين إنما يتصور فيما يمكن الجمع بين الجهتين وهنا ليس كذلك لأن جهة كون الكتابة معاوضة تستلزم عدم سقوط البدل وجهة كونه شرطاً يستلزم السقوط والسقوط وعدمه متنافيان قطعاً لا يمكن اجتماعهما في محل واحد وتنافي اللازمين يوجب تنافي الملزومين فلا يمكن اجتماعهما. والصواب في الجواب أنه إنما سلم لها البدل لأن الكتابة انفسخت في حق البدل وبقيت في حق الاكتساب والأولاد لأن الفسخ للنظر لهما والنظر فيما ذكرناه وإن ماتت وتركت مالاً يؤدي كتابتها منه وما بقي لولدها ميراثاً لأنه ثبت عتقها في آخر جزء من أجزاء حياتها، وإن لم تترك مالاً فلا سعاية على الولد لأنه حر. وإن ولدت ولداً آخر لم يثبت نسبه من غير دعوى لحرمة وطئها عليه وولد أم الولد إنما يثبت نسبه من غير دعوى إذا كان وطؤها حلالاً، وإذا عجزت نفسها وولدت بعد ذلك ولداً في مدة يمكن العلوق بعد التعجيز يثبت نسبه من غير دعوى إلا إذا انفاه صريحاً. ولو لم يدع الولد الثاني وماتت من غير وفاء سعى هذا الولد في بدل الكتابة لأنه مكاتب تبعاً لها، ولو مات المولى بعد ذلك عتق وبطل عنه السعاية لأنه بمنزلة أم الولد وأطلق في قوله «مكاتبة» فشمل ما إذا كانت مفردة بالعقد أو مكاتبة مع أخرى وما ذكره خاص بالأولى. قال في المحيط : رجل كاتب جاريتين مكاتبة واحدة ثم استولد أحدهما فالولد حر والأم مكاتبة كما كانت ولا خيار لها لأن الاستيلاد حصل في ملكه فعلق حراً. وإنما قلنا لا خيار لها لأنه لا يمكن ردها إلى الرق بدون الأخرى، ولو ولدت إحداهما بنتاً فاستولد المولى البنت صارت أم ولد له والولد حر بغير القيمة ، وليس لها أن تعجز نفسها وتبطل الكتابة لأنها تابعة لأمها وإذا تعذر فسخ الكتابة تصير أم ولد له .اهـ. فلو قال بعقد مفرد لسلم. وفي المبسوط : إذا ادعى المولى حبل المكاتبة فضرب إنسان بطنها بعد ذلك فألقت جنيناً ميتاً فإن في الولد غرة لأبيه لأنه عتق بيوم بدعوته فكان ميراثاً له ولا ترث شيئاً ولكنها تأخذ العقر إن اختارت المضي على المكاتبة اهـ . فلو قال ولو ادعى جبلها فضرب آخر بطنها فألقت جنيناً ميتاً مضت إلى آخره. لكان أولى لأنه يعلم حكم ما إذا ولدته فادعاه بالأولى. وفي المبسوط أيضاً : ولدت مكاتبة من مولاها ثم أقر المولى أنها أمة لفلان لم يصدق وإن صدقته في ذلك لأن حق أمومية الولد قد ثبت لها فلا يصدقان في إبطالها. فإن قال المدعي بعتها منك بألف ولم ينقد الثمن وقال المولى زوجتني والأمة معروفة للمدعي فعلى المولى المهر لمستوفيه قصاصاً من الثمن، وليس عليه قيمة في الأم ولا في الولد وإن لم تكن معروفة أنها للمدعي ضمن القيمة ألا ترى أنه لو أنكر البيع لم يتمكن من استردادها فيضمن قيمتها بعد أن يحلف بالله ما اشتراها منه بما يدعيه من الثمن اهـ. وقيد بقوله «مكاتبة من سيدها ليحترز عن أمة المكاتب فإن صدقه ثبت النسب ويضمن قيمة الولد وتعتبر قيمته يوم الولادة هذا إذا جاءت به لستة أشهر من حين اشتراها، فلو جاءت به لأقل فادعاه المولى لا تصح دعوته، وكذا إذا اشترى المكاتب غلاماً من السوق لا تصح دعوته إلا بتصديق المكاتب عبد كاتبه وكاتب العبد أمة ثم ولدت المكاتبة ولداً فادعاه مولى المكاتب فالمسألة على وجوه إما إن صدقاه في ذلك أو كذباه أو صدقه أحدهما وكذبه الآخر، فإن جاءت بالولد لستة أشهر فأكثر فصدقاه في ذلك أو صدقه المكاتب ثبت النسب منه، وإن كذباه في ذلك أو كذبته المكاتبة لا يثبت النسب والعبرة هنا بتصديق المكاتبة دون
Section: He mentioned these matters in a separate section because they are distinguished by rulings that differ from what preceded. He (the author of Kanz) said: (If a female mukataba gives birth from her master, she continues on her kitaba or becomes incapacitated and she is umm walad.) Because when the master claimed it, she becomes umm walad from him. So she is met by two aspects: immediate freedom with compensation, which is the kitaba, and deferred freedom without compensation, which is motherhood of the child. So she may choose whichever she wishes. And her attestation is not needed because she is his property by her person, unlike the case where he claims a child from a slave-girl of the mukatab, where the lineage is not established from the master except with the attestation of the mukataba, because he has no ownership in reality over the mukataba's property, he only has a right of ownership, so her attestation is needed. So if she continues on the kitaba, she takes her 'aqr from her master. If the master dies, she becomes free by istilad and the kitaba payment is dropped because freedom came to her without compensation by istilad. And Taju al-Shari'a said: If you say: it should not be dropped because the earnings remain hers, and likewise her children that she bought after the kitaba, and this is a sign of the kitaba's continuation, we say: The kitaba resembles a mu'awada (exchange) and with respect to that, the payment does not drop, and it resembles a shart (condition) and with respect to that, it drops. We say: the earnings remain safe by acting on the aspect of exchange, and we say the payment drops by acting on the aspect of condition. It was objected that it has been established repeatedly that acting upon two similarities is only conceivable when both aspects can be combined, but here it is not so, because the aspect of being an exchange necessitates non-dropping of the payment, and the aspect of being a condition necessitates dropping, and dropping and non-dropping are absolutely contradictory and cannot coexist in one place, and the contradiction of the necessitateds necessitates the contradiction of the necessitators, so they cannot be combined. The correct answer is that the payment is indeed given to her because the kitaba is dissolved regarding the payment and remains regarding earnings and children because the dissolution is for their benefit. And if she dies and leaves property, her kitaba is paid from it, and what remains is inheritance for her child because her freedom is established in the last part of her life. And if she does not leave property, no earning (sa'aya) is required of the child because he is free. And if she gives birth to another child, his lineage is not established without a claim, because intercourse with her is forbidden. And the child of umm walad only has his lineage established without a claim if her intercourse was lawful. And if she becomes incapacitated and then gives birth after that within a period that can accommodate conception after the incapacitation, his lineage is established without a claim unless he explicitly denies him. And if the second child is not claimed and she dies without paying, this child earns for the kitaba payment because he is a mukatab subordinately to her. And if the master dies after that, he becomes free and the earning is dropped because he is in the position of umm walad. And the wording 'mukataba' is general, including whether she is alone in the contract or with another. And what he mentioned is specific to the first. It says in al-Muhit: A man has two slave-girls in one kitaba, then he has a child from one of them: the child is free and the mother remains a mukataba as before, and she has no choice because the istilad occurred in his property, so the child is free. And we say she has no choice because she cannot be returned to slavery without the other. And if one of them gives birth to a daughter and the master has a child from the daughter, she becomes umm walad for him, and the child is free without value, and she does not have the right to incapacitate herself and invalidate the kitaba because she is subordinate to her mother, and if the kitaba cannot be dissolved, she becomes umm walad for him. End. If he said with a single contract, it is sound. And in al-Mabsut: If the master claims the pregnancy of the female mukataba, then a person strikes her belly and she miscarries a dead fetus, then the fetus is subject to a bloodwit (ghurra) for the father because it became free on the day of his claim, so it was his inheritance, and she does not inherit anything, but she takes the 'aqr if she chooses to continue on the kitaba. End. If he said: and if he claims her pregnancy, and another strikes her belly and she miscarries a dead fetus, to the end, it would be more appropriate because the ruling if she gives birth and he claims it is known by priority. And in al-Mabsut also: A female mukataba gives birth from her master, then the master declares that she is a slave of so-and-so; he is not believed, even if she affirms him in that, because the right of motherhood of the child is established for her, so they are not believed in invalidating it. If the claimant says: I sold her to you for a thousand but you did not pay the price, and the master says: you married her to me, and the slave is known to belong to the claimant, then upon the master is the mahr to the one who was entitled to it, set off against the price, and there is no value upon him for the mother nor the child. But if she was not known to belong to the claimant, then he guarantees the value. Do you not see that if he denied the sale, he could not recover her, so he guarantees her value after swearing by Allah that he did not buy her from him with the price he claims. End. And the restriction with his saying 'mukataba from her master' is to guard against the slave of the mukatab. For if he affirms him, the lineage is established and he guarantees the value of the child, and its value is considered on the day of birth. This is if she delivers the child six months from the time he purchased her. If she delivers it in less than that, then if the master claims him, his claim is not valid. Likewise, if the mukatab buys a male slave from the market, his claim is not valid except with the attestation of the mukatab. A slave's mukatab and a slave's mukataba, then the female mukataba gives birth, and the master of the mukatab claims the child: the matter has several aspects: either they both affirm him, or they both deny him, or one affirms and the other denies. If she delivers the child after six months or more, and they both affirm him, or the mukatab affirms him, the lineage is established from him. If they both deny him, or the female mukataba denies him, the lineage is not established. And the consideration here is the attestation of the female mukataba, not...
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
Passage type: This is a detailed commentary (sharh) that expands on the ruling of the female mukataba. The commentator explains the two aspects of her freedom: immediate with compensation (kitaba) and deferred without compensation (umm walad). She chooses. A debate from Taju al-Shari'a is presented: some argued the kitaba payment should not drop because she retains earnings and children; the commentator resolves that the kitaba is dissolved only regarding the payment but remains for earnings and children, as this benefits both parties. Then several subcases are given: if she dies, her estate pays the kitaba; if she has a second child, its lineage requires a claim; if she becomes unable to pay and gives birth after, the child is mukatab subordinately; if the master dies, that child is freed. Quotes from al-Muhit and al-Mabsut provide additional rulings: cases of two slave-girls in one kitaba, a third party striking the pregnant mother causing miscarriage, and the master admitting she belongs to another. Finally, the restriction 'mukataba from her master' distinguishes the case of a slave of a mukatab, where different rules apply based on attestation and the timing of birth.

Key Terms
كتابة (kitāba): A contract between a slave and master for the slave to purchase freedom by paying installments.
استيلاد (istīlād): The act of a master fathering a child on his slave-woman, which makes her umm walad.
عقر ('aqr): A compensation paid to the slave woman for the master's intercourse; here, a sum she receives if she chooses to continue the kitaba.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to the kitaba payment of a female mukataba who becomes umm walad when the master dies?
A) It must still be paid in full by her or her child.
B) It is dropped completely because she becomes free by istilad.
C) It is reduced by half.
D) It is transferred to the child as a debt.

Key Takeaway
The kitaba payment drops on the master's death for the female mukataba who is umm walad, but her earnings and property remain hers.

Subsidiary Cases: Death, Inheritance, and Claims
Presents numerous subsidiary cases from al-Muhit and al-Mabsut extending the female mukataba's rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rulings for the female mukataba's death, the status of her children, the effect of incapacitation, and claims of ownership by third parties as detailed in al-Muhit and al-Mabsut.

Core Idea
When a female mukataba dies, her kitaba is paid from her estate; her free child owes nothing.

This lesson continues from the core ruling by exploring several subsidiary cases that arise in practice. The text from al-Muhit and al-Mabsut provides detailed rulings on death, inheritance, the obligation of children to earn (sa'aya), cases of multiple slave-girls in one kitaba, miscarriage due to a third party, and the master's admission that the female mukataba belongs to another. These cases illustrate the comprehensive nature of Hanafi fiqh and the interplay between kitaba, umm walad, and property rights.

Guided Path
1
Case of death: inheritance and sa'aya
If she dies leaving property, the kitaba is paid from it; the remainder goes to her free child as inheritance. If she leaves nothing, no earning is required from the child because he is free.
Her freedom is established at the last moment of life, so the child inherits.
2
Second child and lineage
If she gives birth to another child, its lineage is not established without a claim because intercourse with her is forbidden. After incapacitation, if she gives birth within a period that can accommodate conception, lineage is established without a claim unless denied.
The second child's status depends on whether the mother was still mukataba or umm walad.
3
Second child after incapacitation and sa'aya
If she becomes incapacitated and then gives birth, that child is mukatab subordinately and must earn to pay her kitaba if she dies without paying. But if the master dies, that child is freed and the earning obligation drops.
The child's obligation is derivative of the mother's status.
4
Multiple slave-girls in one kitaba
From al-Muhit: if two slave-girls are in a single kitaba and one is istilad, the child is free, the mother remains a mukataba, and she has no choice because she cannot be separated from the other.
The joint kitaba restricts the individual choice.
5
Miscarriage and third-party liability
From al-Mabsut: if the master claims the pregnancy and a third party strikes her causing miscarriage, a bloodwit (ghurra) is due for the fetus (inherited by the father), and she takes 'aqr if she continues the kitaba.
The fetus is considered a free person because the master's claim establishes lineage.
6
Master's admission and disputed ownership
If the master admits that the female mukataba belongs to another, not believed because umm walad right is established. If the claimant says he sold her to the master, the master must pay mahr (set off) but not value of mother and child.
The established right of motherhood overrides admission.

Section: He mentioned these matters in a separate section because they are distinguished by rulings that differ from what preceded. He (the author of Kanz) said: (If a female mukataba gives birth from her master, she continues on her kitaba or becomes incapacitated and she is umm walad.) Because when the master claimed it, she becomes umm walad from him. So she is met by two aspects: immediate freedom with compensation, which is the kitaba, and deferred freedom without compensation, which is motherhood of the child. So she may choose whichever she wishes. And her attestation is not needed because she is his property by her person, unlike the case where he claims a child from a slave-girl of the mukatab, where the lineage is not established from the master except with the attestation of the mukataba, because he has no ownership in reality over the mukataba's property, he only has a right of ownership, so her attestation is needed. So if she continues on the kitaba, she takes her 'aqr from her master. If the master dies, she becomes free by istilad and the kitaba payment is dropped because freedom came to her without compensation by istilad. And Taju al-Shari'a said: If you say: it should not be dropped because the earnings remain hers, and likewise her children that she bought after the kitaba, and this is a sign of the kitaba's continuation, we say: The kitaba resembles a mu'awada (exchange) and with respect to that, the payment does not drop, and it resembles a shart (condition) and with respect to that, it drops. We say: the earnings remain safe by acting on the aspect of exchange, and we say the payment drops by acting on the aspect of condition. Commentary
Passage type: This is a detailed commentary (sharh) that expands on the ruling of the female mukataba. The commentator explains the two aspects of her freedom: immediate with compensation (kitaba) and deferred without compensation (umm walad). She chooses. A debate from Taju al-Shari'a is presented: some argued the kitaba payment should not drop because she retains earnings and children; the commentator resolves that the kitaba is dissolved only regarding the payment but remains for earnings and children, as this benefits both parties. Then several subcases are given: if she dies, her estate pays the kitaba; if she has a second child, its lineage requires a claim; if she becomes unable to pay and gives birth after, the child is mukatab subordinately; if the master dies, that child is freed. Finally, the restriction 'mukataba from her master' distinguishes the case of a slave of a mukatab, where different rules apply based on attestation and the timing of birth.

Key Terms
سعاية (sa'āya): Earning or forced labor to pay off a debt; here, the child's obligation to earn to pay the mother's kitaba.
عجز ('ajz): Incapacity of the mukataba to pay, leading to termination of kitaba and her becoming umm walad.
غرة (ghurra): A bloodwit (diya) paid for a fetus; here, 1/10 of diya (value of a slave) for a miscarried fetus.

Checkpoint
A female mukataba dies leaving no property. Her child was born after she became incapacitated. What is the child's obligation regarding the kitaba payment?
A) The child is free and owes nothing.
B) The child must earn (sa'aya) to pay the kitaba.
C) The child must pay half the kitaba.
D) The child is enslaved to pay the debt.

Key Takeaway
The female mukataba's child from the master is free and inherits; if she dies with property, it pays the kitaba first; otherwise the child owes nothing.
