Estate Distribution When a Mukatab Dies with Wealth: Hanafi Furu'
Presents furu' rules for estate distribution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for distributing a mukatab's estate upon his death, including priority of debts, treatment of bequests, and inheritance of children.

Core Idea
When a mukatab dies with wealth, outsider debts take priority over the kitabah price, and children born during kitabah inherit only if the kitabah is paid.

The Hanafi school provides detailed guidance on how a mukatab's estate is handled when he dies with sufficient wealth. This lesson examines the rules from al-Asl and other authoritative works, focusing on the priority of debts, the fate of bequests, and the inheritance rights of children born during the kitabah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the obligations
List all claims on the estate: outsider debts, kitabah price, bequests.
2
Apply the priority rule
From al-Asl: first outsider debt, then kitabah price; bequests are void if insufficient.
3
Handle cases where only a debt is owed to the mukatab
The child works to pay the kitabah; if unable, returns to slavery.
4
Analyze inheritance scenarios
From Nawadir Bishr: children born during kitabah inherit from father only after kitabah paid; a child who dies before payment does not inherit.
5
Apply the rules to a mixed scenario
Free sons and children born during kitabah: they inherit differently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وإن مات وله مال لم تفسخ) وهذا قول ابن مسعود وبه أخذ علماؤنا . وقال زيد بن ثابت تنفسخ الكتابة بموته وبه أخذ الإمام الشافعي له أن العقد لو بقي لبقي لتحصيل العتق بالأداء وقد تعذر إثباته ،فبطل، وهذا لأن العتق لا يخلوا إما أن يثبت العتق قبل الموت أو بعده مقتصراً أو مستنداً لا وجه إلى الأول لعدم شرطه وهو الأداء والشيء لا يسبق شرطه، ولا إلى الثاني لأن الميت ليس بمحل لنزول العتق عليه لأن العتق إثبات قوة الميت يتصور في المالكية بخلاف ما إذا مات المولى لأنه ليس بمعقود عليه بل عاقد والعقد يبطل بموت المعقود عليه لا بموت العاقد، ولأن المولى يصلح أن يكون معتقاً بعد الموت كما هو. تفسخ وتؤدي كتابته من ماله وحكم بعتقه في آخر حياته وإن ترك ولداً ولد في كتابته إذا قال أنت حر بعد موتي. ولنا أن الكتابة عقد معاوضة لا تنفسخ بموت أحد المتعاقدين وهو المولى فلا ينفسخ بموت الآخر وهو العبد كالبيع ولأن قضية المعاوضة المساواة فإذا بقي العقد بعد موت المولى لحاجته إلى الولاء وغيره جاز أن يبقى بعد موت العبد لحاجته إلى الحرية ليتوصل إلى حرية أولاده، ولو مات عاجزاً تفسخ الكتابة، ولو قذفه إنسان بعد الأداء يلزمه الحد، وقيل الأداء لا يلزمه الحد لأن العتق ثبت مستنداً إلى آخر جزء من أجزاء حياته فلا يظهر الاستناد في حق هذا الحكم. قال رحمه الله : (وتؤدي كتابته من ماله) يعني يؤدي من خلفه فيكون أداء الخليفة كأدائه بنفسه. فإن قيل : الأداء فعل والاستناد يكون في أحكام الشريعة قلنا : نعم لكن فعل الثابت مضافاً إلى حسي الثبوت وهذه الإضافة شرعية ألا ترى أن من رمى صيداً فمات قبل أن يصيبه ثم أصابه صار مالكاً له حتى يورث عنه والمالك ليس بأهل لكن لما صح السبب والملك يثبت بعد تمام السبب وتمامه بالإضافة إليه وهو ليس أهلاً له ثبت الملك من حين الإمكان وهو آخر جزء من أجزاء حياته فكذا هنا. وفي الأصل: إذا مات المكاتب عن وفاء وعليه ديون لأجنبي سوى بدل الكتابة وله مال يوفي وله وصايا يبدأ من تركته بدين الأجنبي ثم بدل الكتابة وتبطل وصاياه وما بقي يقسم بين ورثته، وإن لم يبق بعد قضاء الدين شيء يبدأ ببدل الكتابة ولا يبدأ بالدين، وإن لم يترك مالاً إلا ديناً على الناس فاستسعى المولود في الكتابة فعجز يرد في الرق فإذا خرج الدين بعد ذلك فذلك للمولى ا هـ . قال رحمه الله : (وحكم بعتقه في آخر حياته) بأن يقام الترك الموجود منه في آخر حياته مقام التخلية بين المال والمولى وهو الأداء المستحق عليه وما بقي فهو لورثته. قال في نوادر بشر عن الثاني : مات مكاتب عن وفاء وله أولاد أمته فمات بعض قبل الأداء فأدى ما عليه وبقي مال فهو ميراث ولا يرث الابن الميت وما تركه الابن الميت فهو لأمه وإخوته، ولو كان الولد معه في عقد الكتابة ثم مات بعد أبيه ثم أديت الكتابة لم يرث أباه . وفي نواد ابن سماعة محمد مكاتب مات وترك ابناً ولد له في الكتابة وترك ألفي درهم ديناً على الناس فاكتسب ألف درهم وأداها في كتابة أبيه ثم خرج دين الأب وله أخ فإن الألفين ميراث بينهما ولا يرجع الابن بما أدى في الألفين، وإن لم يؤد الابن ذلك من ماله فله أن يأخذ من مال الأب. وفي المنتقى : مكاتب مات وله دين على الناس وله مولود في الكتابة يسعى في الكتابة على نجومها وله ابنان حران أيضاً ثم مات أحد الابنين الحرين ثم خرج ما للمكاتب على الناس فأديت من ذلك بدل الكتابة فالفاضل بين الولد الحر والمولود في الكتابة ويرث الابن الحر أخاه الذي مات بعد موت الأب والابن المولود في الكتابة لا يرث من أخيه الذي مات بعد موت الأب وفي التتارخانية : مات الرجل عن مكاتبه وله ورثه ذكور وأناث ثم مات المكاتب عن وفاء يؤدي من ذلك بدل الكتابة ويكون بين الورثة الذكور والإناث.
He said (may Allah have mercy on him): (And if he dies and has wealth, it is not rescinded) — this is the saying of Ibn Mas'ud, and our scholars adopted it. Zayd ibn Thabit said: The kitabah is rescinded by his death, and Imam al-Shafi'i adopted this. His proof: if the contract remained, it would remain for the acquisition of freedom through payment, but its realization is impossible, so it is void. This is because freedom must either be established before death or after death as a restricted or retroactive effect. The first is impossible because its condition (payment) is absent, and a thing cannot precede its condition. The second is also impossible because the dead is not a locus for the descent of freedom — freedom is the establishment of strength, which can be imagined in the living, unlike the case of the master's death because he is not the contracted subject but the contractor, and the contract is voided by the death of the contracted subject, not the contractor. And because the master is fit to be an emancipator after death as well. It is rescinded, and his kitabah is paid from his wealth, and a ruling is made for his freedom at the last moment of his life, even if he left a child born during his kitabah and he had said 'you are free after my death'. Our proof: the kitabah is a contract of exchange (mu'awadah) that is not rescinded by the death of one of the two contractors — the master — so it is not rescinded by the death of the other — the slave — like a sale. And because the nature of exchange is equivalence; if the contract remains after the master's death due to the need for wala' and other things, it is permissible for it to remain after the slave's death due to the need for freedom to facilitate the freedom of his children. If he dies unable, the kitabah is rescinded. If someone accuses him of fornication after payment, the prescribed hadd (80 lashes) is obligatory upon the accuser; and some said payment does not require the hadd because the freedom is established retroactively to the last part of his life, so the retroactivity does not appear regarding this ruling. He said (may Allah have mercy on him): (And his kitabah is paid from his wealth) meaning his successor pays, so the payment of the successor is like his own payment. If it is said: payment is an act, and retroactivity applies in shariah rulings, we say: yes, but the act of the one established is attributed to sensory existence, and this attribution is shariah-based. Do you not see that if someone shoots at game and dies before it hits, then it hits, he becomes its owner so that it is inherited from him — even though the owner is not eligible (for ownership) — but because the cause is sound, and ownership is established after the completion of the cause and its completion is attributed to him, and he is not eligible for it, ownership is established from the time of possibility, which is the last part of his life. So here similarly. In al-Asl: if a mukatab dies with sufficient wealth and owes debts to outsiders besides the kitabah price, and has wealth to pay, and has bequests, then his estate begins with the outsider's debt, then the kitabah price, and his bequests are void; what remains is divided among his heirs. If nothing remains after paying the debt, he begins with the kitabah price and does not begin with the debt. If he leaves no wealth except a debt owed by people, then the child (born during kitabah) works to pay the kitabah installments, and if unable, he is returned to slavery; if the debt is collected afterward, it goes to the master. End. He said (may Allah have mercy on him): (And a ruling is made for his freedom at the last moment of his life) meaning the wealth left by him at the last moment of his life is considered as handing over the wealth to the master, i.e., the payment due from him, and what remains is for his heirs. He said in Nawadir Bishr from the second: a mukatab died with sufficient wealth and had children from his slave-wife; some died before payment; he paid what was due and wealth remained; it is inheritance, and the deceased son does not inherit, and what the deceased son left is for his mother and siblings. If the child was included in the same kitabah contract and then died after his father, and then the kitabah was paid, he does not inherit from his father. In Nawadir Ibn Sam'ah: Muhammad said: a mukatab died leaving a son born during his kitabah and leaving 2000 dirhams as a debt owed by people; the son earned 1000 dirhams and paid it for his father's kitabah; then the father's debt was collected and he has a (free) brother; then the 2000 are inheritance between them, and the son does not take back what he paid from the 2000. If the son did not pay that from his own wealth, he may take from the father's wealth. In al-Muntaqa: a mukatab died, owed debt to people, and had a child born during his kitabah who works to pay the kitabah installments, and he also has two free sons; then one of the free sons died; then the mukatab's debt owed by people was collected, and from it the kitabah price was paid; the surplus is between the free son and the child born during kitabah; the free son inherits from his brother who died after the father's death, but the child born during kitabah does not inherit from that brother. In al-Tatar Khaniyyah: a man died leaving a mukatab, and his heirs are males and females; then the mukatab died with sufficient wealth; the kitabah price is paid from that and distributed among the male and female heirs.

Commentary
This section presents a significant inter-madhhab dispute regarding the fate of a mukatab who dies with sufficient wealth to pay the kitabah price. The Hanafi position, attributed to Ibn Mas'ud (ra), is that the kitabah is NOT rescinded by his death; rather, the kitabah price is paid from his estate, and he is considered to have become free at the last moment of his life. This allows his children born during the kitabah to become free as well. The opposing position, attributed to Zayd ibn Thabit (ra) and adopted by Imam al-Shafi'i, holds that the kitabah is rescinded upon the mukatab's death, and his estate goes to his heirs as a free man's estate (i.e., no kitabah price is due) or returns to the master. The Hanafi proof (dalil) is that the kitabah is a contract of exchange (mu'awadah) like a sale — it is not voided by the death of either party. The master's death does not void it because the contract is on the slave, not the master; similarly, the slave's death should not void it. The master can still receive the payment from the estate, and the slave's freedom is established retroactively upon payment. To address the objection that a dead person cannot be emancipated, the Hanafis use the principle of istinad (retroactive attribution) drawing an analogy with hunting: a hunter who shoots and dies before the arrow hits can still become the owner of the game at the moment of impact because the cause was sound; similarly, the payment by the estate is attributed to the mukatab's life. The commentary then provides detailed furu' (branches) from al-Asl, al-Nawadir, al-Muntaqa, and al-Tatar Khaniyyah about how the estate is distributed, priority of debts, and inheritance shares for children born during kitabah versus free children. Key points: (1) Outsider debts take priority over the kitabah price; (2) Bequests are void if the estate is insufficient; (3) Children born during kitabah who work to pay the price and become unable are returned to slavery; (4) Complex inheritance cases show that a child born during kitabah inherits from the father only if the kitabah is paid, but does not inherit from a free half-brother who died after the father.

Key Terms
استناد (istinād): Retroactive attribution of a legal effect to a prior moment in time, used here to establish the mukatab's freedom at the last moment of his life upon payment from his estate.
ولاء (walāʾ): Patronage bond between the emancipator and the freed slave, which remains with the master's heirs after the master's death.
معاوضة (muʿāwaḍah): Contract of exchange where each party gives something in return for something else, like sale; kitabah is considered such despite its kindness aspect.

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi rules, what is the priority of claims on a mukatab's estate?
A) Kitabah price first, then outsider debts, then bequests.
B) Outsider debts first, then kitabah price, then bequests.
C) Bequests first, then outsider debts, then kitabah.
D) All claims are paid proportionally.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school establishes a clear priority: outsider debts, then kitabah price; bequests are void if insufficient, ensuring the mukatab's freedom and fair treatment of creditors.

Effect of Child's Crime on Mukatab's Kitaba
Explains that the judgment on the child's crime does not indicate the mukatab's incapacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a judge's ruling on a child's crime does not prove the mukatab's incapacity and explain the reasoning using the legal status of the mukatab.

Core Idea
A judgment on a child's crime does not invalidate the father's kitaba while payment remains pending.

The mukatab is a slave who has contracted to earn his freedom by paying a sum. His status is suspended until full payment. This lesson examines a scenario where the mukatab dies, leaving a child from a free woman and a sufficient debt, and the child commits a crime. Does a judge's ruling against the mother's ʻāqila imply the mukatab's incapacity? The passage clarifies it does not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
Understand the setup: mukatab dies, leaves a free child, a debt covering kitaba, child commits a crime. Judge rules against mother's ʻāqila for blood money.
The child is from a free woman, so the ʻāqila is the mother's side.
2
State the legal question
Does the judgment constitute a ruling on the mukatab's incapacity (ʻajz), i.e., inability to pay kitaba? If it did, the kitaba would dissolve and the child's wala' would revert differently.
ʻAjz invalidates kitaba according to Hanafi law.
3
Extract the Hanafi ruling and proof
The judgment does not prove ʻajz because the mukatab is not yet free; the debt covering kitaba is not paid. The crime is against the mother's mawali, confirming the kitaba.
The judgment confirms the kitaba remains; it does not rule on the mukatab's ability to pay.
4
Understand the practical consequence
After judgment, if kitaba is later paid (by the child or others), the mukatab becomes free, child's wala' attaches to father's side, and mother's mawali can recoup from father's mawali.
The ruling preserves the potential for freedom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن ترك ولداً من حرة وديناً فيه وفاء بكاتبته فجنى الولد فقضى به على عاقلة الأم لم يكن ذلك قضاء بعجز المكاتب لأن القضاء بموجب الجناية على موالي الأم لأن المكاتب وإن ترك مالاً وهو الدين لا يحكم بعتقه إلا عند أداء بدل الكتابة فكانت الجناية عليهم، فإذا قضى به القاضي عليهم كان القضاء تقريراً للكتابة فتبقى الكتابة على حالها، فإذا بعد ذلك بدل الكتابة عتق الكاتب وظهر للابن ولاء في جانب الأب فينجر إليه ولاؤه. ولأنه فرع ظهور العتق وكانوا مضطرين فيما علقوا فلهم الرجوع بذلك على موالي الأب ولا يرجعون بذلك على ولي الجناية.
If he leaves a child from a free woman and a debt covering his kitaba, and the child commits a crime, and the judge gives judgment against the mother's 'aqila, that is not a judgment of the mukatab's incapacity, because the judgment is based on the crime against the mother's mawali. The mukatab, even though he leaves property—the debt—he is not judged to be free until payment of the kitaba. So the crime is against them (the mawali). When the judge rules against them, the ruling confirms the kitaba, so the kitaba remains as it was. Then if he pays the kitaba later, the writer becomes free, and the son's wala' appears on the father's side, and his wala' joins to him. And because it is a branch of the appearance of freedom, and they were forced in what they claimed, they may recourse to the father's mawali, but they do not recourse to the guardian of the crime.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the chapter on the mukatab. It addresses a scenario where a mukatab dies leaving a free child (from a free wife) and a debt that suffices to pay the kitaba. The child then commits a crime. The judge rules that the blood money is on the 'aqila of the mother (i.e., her mawali). This ruling does not establish the mukatab's incapacity because the debt is not yet paid; the mukatab is still considered a slave until payment. Thus, the kitaba remains intact. If later the kitaba is paid (e.g., by the child or someone else), the mukatab becomes free, and the child's wala' transfers to the father's side. The mother's mawali, who paid the blood money, can then recoup from the father's mawali, but not from the guardian of the crime.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukātab): A slave who has contracted with his master to earn and pay a specified sum for his freedom.
عاقلة (ʿāqila): The group of paternal relatives (ʻaṣaba) liable for blood money (diyah) on behalf of a killer.
ولاء (walāʾ): The right of inheritance and patronage that belongs to the manumitter (or his heirs) from a freed slave.
عجز (ʿajz): Incapacity, especially the inability of the mukatab to pay the kitaba, which dissolves the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the judge's ruling against the mother's ʻāqila not constitute a judgment of the mukatab's incapacity?
A) Because the mukatab is already free.
B) Because the debt covering the kitaba has not yet been paid, so the kitaba remains.
C) Because the child's crime is unrelated to the mukatab's ability.
D) Because the mother's ʻāqila are responsible for the crime.

Key Takeaway
A judgment on a child's crime does not imply the mukatab's incapacity; the kitaba remains pending payment, preserving the potential for freedom.

Judgment for Mother's Mawali as Proof of Mukatab's Incapacity
Explains that a dispute over wala' resulting in judgment for mother's mawali is a judgment of incapacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a judgment awarding wala' to the mother's mawali constitutes a judgment of the mukatab's incapacity, and distinguish the Hanafi position on rescission of kitaba from the intra-madhhab dispute.

Core Idea
A judgment giving wala' to the mother's side necessarily rescinds the kitaba because wala' from the father becomes impossible.

This lesson examines a specific ruling: if after a mukatab's death a dispute over wala' (patronage rights) arises between the mawali of the mother and father, and the judge rules for the mother's mawali, that judgment is considered a ruling of the mukatab's incapacity (ʿajz). This has direct consequences for the kitaba contract—it is rescinded. We'll walk through the reasoning, the supporting dalil (consensus on protecting judicial rulings), and an intra-Hanafi difference on whether rescission requires a judge's ruling or occurs automatically upon death without payment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the dispute
The scenario involves a mukatab who dies with a child, and the mawali of the mother and father dispute the child's wala'. The judge rules that wala' belongs to the mother's mawali.
Wala' follows the asaba; mother's side only inherits if father's side cannot.
2
Understand the consequence of the judgment
The judge's award to the mother's mawali necessarily implies the mukatab's incapacity. Why? Because if the kitaba were still intact, the father's mawali could claim wala' upon eventual payment. Since the mother's wala' only becomes possible when the father's claim is definitively blocked (by rescission of kitaba), the judgment effectively rescinds the kitaba.
This is a logical necessity (luzum) – the judgment cannot be valid without the kitaba being void.
3
Analyze the support from consensus
The text invokes the consensus rule: 'Protecting a judicial ruling from being overturned is obligatory.' Thus the judgment stands, and the resulting consequence (incapacity) is also firmly established.
This is a dalil aqli (rational proof) based on consensus.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on rescission after death
Even after the judgment, there is a dispute: al-Isfaji holds that the kitaba is automatically rescinded upon death without payment (so no later payment can free the mukatab), while Abu al-Layth holds it only rescinds by the judge's ruling of incapacity.
The text does not explicitly give a tarjih, but the commentary leans toward Abu al-Layth's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله: (وإن اختصم موالي الأم وموالي الأب في ولائه فقضى به الموالي الأم فهو قضاء بالعجز) لأنه إذا كانت الخصومة في نفس الولاء بأن مات الولد بعد موت الأب قبل خروج الدين وقضى بميراثه لمولى الأم بطلت الكتابة لأن القاضي يقضي بكون الولاء لمولى الأم لأن الخصومة وقعت في الولاء ومن ضرورة القضاء فسخ الكتابة لأن الولاء من جانب الأم لا يثبت إلا إذا تعذر إثباته من جانب الأب، وإنما يتعذر بفسخ الكتابة لأنها لو كانت باقية يمكن أن يثبت من جانبه بالأداء، ولو خرج الدين بعد ذلك يكون لمولى المكاتب ميراثاً عن عبده لأن صيانة القضاء عن الانتقاض واجب بالإجماع، وفسخ الكتابة بعد موت المكاتب مختلف فيه فكان فسخ الكتابة أولى من نقض القضاء لأن القضاء بالفعل لا ينفسخ وبالقضاء ظهر العجز مطلقاً حتى لو ظهر مال مقدار البدل وأخذه المولى لا يكون بدلاً . عن الكناية بخلاف ما قبل القضاء قال في المحيط : وإذامات المكاتب عاجزاً وترك ولداً حراً فظهر للمكاتب وديعة أديت منها كتابته ولا يتحول ولاء الولد إلى مولى الأب لأن المودع أقر بشيئين؛ أقر بأنه ملك المكاتب وأقر أن ولاءه تحول، فإقراره على نفسه صحيح فيصدق فيه وإقراره بتحول الولاء إلى غيره لا يصدق فيه، ألا ترى أن المولى لو أقر أنه استوفى منه بدل الكتابة قبل موته لا يصدق في حق تحول الولاء إلى موالي الأب فكذا هنا . وأما إذا مات لا عن وفاء ولا ولد فاختلفوا في بقاء الكتابة ; قال الاسكافي: تنفسخ حتى لو تطوع له إنسان بأداء بدل الكتابة عنه لا تقبل منه وقال أبو الليث : لا تنفسخ ما لم يقض القاضي بعجزه حتى لو تطوع إنسان عنه قبل القضاء بالفسخ جاز ويحكم بعتقه في آخر . أجزاء حياته.
He (author) said: (And if the mawali of the mother and the mawali of the father dispute over his wala', and he judges for the mother's mawali, then it is a judgment of incapacity) because if the dispute is over the wala' itself—such as the child died after the father's death before the debt is paid, and he judges his inheritance for the mother's mawali—then the kitaba is void. Because the judge rules that the wala' belongs to the mother's mawali, since the dispute occurred over wala', and it is a necessary consequence of the judgment to rescind the kitaba. Because wala' from the mother's side is only established if it is impossible to prove it from the father's side. And it is impossible only by rescinding the kitaba, because if it remained, it could be proven from his side through payment. And if the debt is later paid, it would be for the mukatab's master as inheritance from his slave, because protecting the judgment from being overturned is obligatory by consensus. And rescinding the kitaba after the mukatab's death is disputed, so rescinding the kitaba is preferable to overturning the judgment, because the judgment in effect is not rescinded. And by the judgment, incapacity appears absolutely, such that if property equivalent to the kitaba appears and the master takes it, it does not count as payment. Unlike before the judgment. He said in al-Muhit: And if the mukatab dies incapacitated and leaves a free child, and then a deposit appears for the mukatab from which the kitaba is paid, the child's wala' does not transfer to the father's mawali, because the depositor admitted two things: he admitted that it was the property of the mukatab, and he admitted that his wala' had transferred. So his admission against himself is valid, so he is believed regarding it; but his admission that the wala' transferred to another is not believed against that other. Do you not see that if the master admitted that he had received the kitaba before his death, he is not believed regarding the transfer of wala' to the father's mawali? So likewise here. And as for if he dies not after payment and without a child, they differed over the continuance of the kitaba. Al-Isfaji said: It is rescinded, such that even if someone volunteers to pay the kitaba on his behalf, it is not accepted. And Abu al-Layth said: It is not rescinded until the judge rules his incapacity, so that if someone volunteers before the ruling of rescission, it is permissible and he is ruled to be free in the last part of his life.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and commentary on the second matn. It discusses a scenario where after the mukatab's death, a dispute arises between the mawali (patrons) of the mother and father over the wala' of the child. If the judge rules that the wala' belongs to the mother's mawali, that judgment necessarily implies the mukatab's incapacity, because wala' from the mother's side only becomes possible if the kitaba is rescinded (since if the kitaba were intact, the father's side could eventually claim wala' upon payment). Therefore, the judgment effectively rescinds the kitaba. This is a strong indication of incapacity. The text further discusses a case from al-Muhit: if after the mukatab's death a deposit is found that covers the kitaba, the child's wala' does not revert to the father's mawali, because the depositor's admission that the deposit belonged to the mukatab and that his wala' had transferred is self-serving and cannot affect the rights of others. The passage ends with a difference of opinion: if the mukatab dies without paying and without a child, some (al-Isfaji) say the kitaba is automatically rescinded, while others (Abu al-Layth) say it continues until a judge rules incapacity.

Key Terms
ولاء (walāʾ): The right of inheritance and clientage that belongs to the manumitter (or his ʻaṣaba) from a freed slave.
عجز (ʿajz): Incapacity of the mukatab to pay the kitaba, leading to rescission of the contract.
كتابة (kitābah): Contract of manumission by which a slave purchases his freedom in installments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The mukatab is free.
B) The kitaba continues.
C) The mukatab is incapacitated and the kitaba is rescinded.
D) The child is a slave.

Key Takeaway
A judicial award of wala' to the mother's mawali after the mukatab's death is a definitive proof of his incapacity and rescind the kitaba, protected by consensus.

The Child's Wala Following the Mother's Wala
Ruling with expansion and dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a child following the mother's wala when the father is unknown, and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
A child of unknown paternity enters the mother's wala upon her contract, but the imams differ on whether the child follows in all cases.

Wala al-muwalat is a contract of clientage between a free person and a patron. When a woman enters such a contract and has a child whose father is unknown, does the child automatically fall under the same wala? The matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq states that the child follows, but the commentary reveals a subtle dispute between the founding imams. This lesson examines the ruling and its application in two scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling
Look at the matn: if a woman contracts wala and gives birth to a child without a known father, the child follows her in the wala. Note that the ruling is stated generally.
The translation clarifies 'without a known father' as a key condition.
2
Distinguish the two scenarios
The commentary expands the matn into two situations: (1) the child is born after the wala contract, and (2) the mother already has a small child of unknown father when she declares her wala. Understand each.
The first scenario is directly from the matn; the second is added by the commentator.
3
Understand the ikhtilaf
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the child follows only in the first scenario (newborn after contract). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the child follows in both scenarios. Identify the difference.
The text signals the ikhtilaf with 'عند الإمام' and 'وقالا'.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The commentary references an earlier explanation ('وقد تقدم بيان ذلك'), indicating that the relied-upon view is that of the Sahibayn. Conclude that in both scenarios the child follows the mother's wala.
This is the operative position for fatwa today.
5
Apply the ruling
Use a concrete example: a woman enters wala, then gives birth to a child with unknown father. The child becomes a mawla of the same patron. Also consider a pre-existing child.
Practical impact: inheritance and manumission rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ولو والت امرأة فولدت تبعها فيه) يعني ولدت ولداً لا يعرف له أب وكذا لو أقرت أنها مولاة فلان ومعها ولد صغير لا يعرف له أب صح إقرارها على نفسها ويتبعها ولدها فيه، وهذا عند الإمام. وقالا : يتبعها ولدها فيه في الصورتين وقد تقدم بيان ذلك .
He, may God have mercy on him, said: (And if a woman contracts wala and gives birth, the child follows her in it) meaning she gives birth to a child whose father is not known. Likewise, if she declares that she is the mawla of so-and-so and she has a small child whose father is not known, her declaration is valid concerning herself, and her child follows her in it. This is the view of Imam [Abu Hanifa]. The two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] say: The child follows her in it in both cases, and this has been explained previously.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage addresses the wala of a woman. The matn states that if a woman enters into a wala al-muwalat contract and then gives birth to a child without a known father, the child is included in her wala. The commentary clarifies two situations: (1) she gives birth to a child with unknown paternity, (2) she already has a small child of unknown paternity and declares herself as a mawla. In both, Abu Hanifa holds that her declaration is valid for herself and the child follows; the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the child follows in both cases. The text notes this has been explained earlier, likely in the chapter on acknowledgment.

Key Terms
تبعها فيه (tabi'aha fihi): The child follows the mother in the wala contract, i.e., shares the same patron.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A child of unknown father follows the mother's wala in both scenarios, and the relied-upon Hanafi view is that of the Sahibayn.

The Validity of Coerced Divorce and Manumission
Matn ruling on coerced manumission and divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on divorce and manumission under coercion and explain the coerced party's right to recourse (rujūʿ).

Core Idea
Coerced divorce or freeing a slave is valid but the coercer must compensate.

In Hanafi fiqh, verbal acts like divorce (talāq) and manumission (ʿitq) are effective by utterance alone, regardless of willingness. Therefore, even under coercion, these acts take effect. However, the coercer is liable for the financial loss incurred by the coerced party.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The matn states: 'وعلى إعتاق وطلاق ففعل وقع ورجع'. Recognize that the act is either freeing a slave or divorce, and the ruling is that it occurs (valid) and the coerced has recourse.
2
Understand the basis
Why does coercion not invalidate these acts? Because they are utterances; coercion does not affect the legal efficacy of the pronouncement.
The Shari'ah considers the utterance itself, not the inner intent.
3
Recognize the recourse
The coerced can claim compensation from the coercer for the value lost (e.g., the value of the slave or the mahr).
This is called rujūʿ.
4
Distinguish from other coerced acts
Contrast with murder: killing under coercion is not permitted and the killer is executed because permission is invalid. The difference is that divorce/manumission are pure verbal acts.
The passage explicitly contrasts with the murder case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى إعتاق وطلاق ففعل وقع ورجع فقتله عمداً يقتل القاتل لأن الإذن بالقتل لم يصح مع الإكراه ولأنه قول لا يؤثر فيه عدم الرضا فيكون التلف مضافاً إلى القتل دون الإذن بخلاف المأمور بالعتق حيث لا يضمن لأن المأمور لا يملك الإعتاق إلا بالإذن فصار المعتق متلقياً بسبب الإذن فيصير التلف محالاً إلى الإذن. ولو أكره المولى بحبس أو قتل فقتله يضمن قيمته استحساناً ويقتص القاتل قياساً. وجه الاستحسان أن الإذن إذا فسد بالإكراه لفوات الرضا معتبر من وجه وفعل المأذون كفعل الآذن فأورث شبهة فلم يجب القصاص فأوجبنا الدية صوناً لدمه عن الهدر، ولو أكره المولى بقتل على بيع عبده وتسليمه والمشتري بالقتل على الشراء والقبض ثم أكره المشتري من على قتله بقتل فللمولى أن يقتل المكره قياساً لأن المشتري مكره على القتل فصار فعله منقولاً إلى المكره، ويضمن قيمته استحساناً لأن العبد مملوك للمشتري وللبائع فيه حق الاسترداد فكان القصاص للبائع من وجه وللمشتري من وجه فكان المستحق للقصاص مجهولاً فلا يكون لأحدهما حق استيفاء القصاص فأوجبنا القيمة على المكره في ماله للبائع لأن للبائع حق الاسترداد وقد أبطل المشتري هذا الحق عليه بالقتل بغير رضاه، فلو أكره أو قيد على بحبس البيع والقبض والمشتري على الشراء بقتل ثم أكره المشتري على قتله بقتل فقتله يضمن قيمته لمولاه ثم يقتل المكره بالعبد قصاصاً لأن المشتري طائع في القبض مكره في الشراء فملك المشتري العبد بعقد فاسد فكان مضموناً عليه بالقيمة وقتله صار منقولاً إلى المكره فصار المكره قاتلاً عبداً عمداً فيجب القصاص. ولو أكره المشتري على الشراء بحبس وللبائع بقتل ثم أكره المشتري على القتل بقتل فقتله فالولي بالخيار إن شاء ضمن المكره قيمة عبده، وإن شاء ضمن لأنه طائع في القبض وقد قتله المكره بقتل المشتري فيجب القصاص اهـ.
And regarding freeing (a slave) and divorce: if he does it, it takes effect, and he (the coerced) has recourse (against the coercer). So if he kills him intentionally, the killer is executed because permission for killing is not valid with coercion, and because it is an utterance in which lack of consent does not affect it; so the destruction is attributed to the killing, not the permission, unlike the one commanded to free (a slave) where he is not liable, because the one commanded does not own the act of freeing except by permission, so the manumitter derives the act from the permission, and thus destruction is referred to the permission. And if the owner is coerced by imprisonment or killing and he kills (his slave), he is liable for its value by istihsan, and the killer is subject to retaliation by qiyas. The reason for istihsan is that if the permission is invalidated by coercion due to loss of consent, which is considered in some sense, and the act of the permitted person is like the act of the permitter, so it creates doubt, and thus retaliation is not required; so we impose blood money to preserve his blood from being wasted. And if the owner is coerced by killing to sell his slave and hand him over, and the buyer is coerced by killing to buy and take possession, then the buyer is coerced to kill him by killing, then the owner may kill the coercer by qiyas because the buyer is coerced to kill, so his act is transferred to the coercer, and he is liable for its value by istihsan because the slave is owned by the buyer and the seller has a right of recovery, so the right to retaliation belongs partly to the seller and partly to the buyer, and thus the one entitled to retaliation is unknown, so neither has the right to execute retaliation, so we impose the value on the coercer from his property for the seller because the seller has the right of recovery and the buyer has nullified this right against him by killing without his consent. If he is coerced or shackled for imprisonment? for sale and possession, and the buyer is coerced by killing to buy, then the buyer is coerced to kill him by killing, and he kills him, he is liable for his value to his owner, then the coercer is killed for the slave as retaliation because the buyer is voluntary in possession and coerced in purchase, so the buyer owns the slave by a defective contract, so he is liable for the value, and his killing is transferred to the coercer, so the coercer becomes a deliberate killer of a slave, so retaliation is obligatory. And if the buyer is coerced to buy by imprisonment, and the seller by killing, then the buyer is coerced to kill by killing, and he kills him, then the guardian has the option: if he wishes he can claim the value of his slave from the coercer, and if he wishes he can claim because he is voluntary in possession and the coercer killed him by killing the buyer, so retaliation is obligatory.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage begins with the matn ruling that if a person is coerced to free a slave or divorce his wife, the act is valid and takes effect, and the coerced person can seek compensation from the coercer. The commentary then discusses ancillary issues: if a person is coerced to kill, the permission is invalid, so the killer is executed. In contrast, if one is commanded to free a slave, the act is valid and the commander is liable. Then cases of coerced sale and purchase are explored, particularly when both seller and buyer are coerced, leading to complex questions of liability for killing the slave. The Hanafi jurists use istihsan to avoid retaliation in cases of doubt and impose blood money instead. The final case involves a buyer coerced by imprisonment, leading to options for the guardian.

Key Terms
رجوع (rujūʿ): Recourse; the coerced person can claim from the coercer the value of what he lost.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference; departing from qiyas to a more equitable ruling.
إكراه (ikrāh): Coercion; forcing someone to act against their will under threat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man is coerced under threat of death to divorce his wife, what is the ruling?
A) The divorce does not take effect because he was coerced
B) The divorce takes effect, and he can claim compensation from the coercer
C) The divorce is valid only if he pronounces it willingly later
D) The wife retains all marital rights

Key Takeaway
Divorce and manumission under coercion are valid; the coerced has recourse against the coercer.

The Hanafi Application of Istihsan in Coercion Cases
Expanded commentary on istihsan vs qiyas in coerced killing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi use of istihsan to override qiyas in cases of coercion to kill and apply the rulings to scenarios involving coerced sale and killing.

Core Idea
Istihsan prevents retaliation when coercion creates doubt, imposing blood money instead.

The passage explores cases where a person is coerced to kill a slave. According to strict analogy (qiyas), the killer should be executed. However, Hanafi jurists prefer istihsan when the permission to kill is tainted by coercion, leading to a doubt that precludes retaliation, and they impose a monetary penalty instead. The commentary then develops complex scenarios involving coerced sale and purchase, demonstrating how istihsan works in multi-party situations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first case
The owner is coerced (by imprisonment or death threat) to kill his slave. He kills him. What is the ruling under qiyas and istihsan?
Qiyas: retaliation. Istihsan: liability for value (blood money).
2
Understand the istihsan rationale
The permission to kill is invalid due to coercion, so there is doubt (shubha) whether the act is purely the killer's or attributed to the coercer. This doubt prevents qisas.
The text says 'فأورث شبهة فلم يجب القصاص'.
3
Apply to the second case
The owner is coerced to sell his slave to a buyer who is also coerced to buy. Then the buyer is coerced to kill the slave. The owner can kill the coercer by qiyas, but the buyer pays value by istihsan.
Complex because of multiple parties.
4
Analyze the final case
The buyer is coerced to buy by imprisonment but is voluntary in possession. When coerced to kill, the coercer becomes the killer and is executed; the buyer is liable for value.
Distinguishes between coercion in contract and coercion in action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى إعتاق وطلاق ففعل وقع ورجع فقتله عمداً يقتل القاتل لأن الإذن بالقتل لم يصح مع الإكراه ولأنه قول لا يؤثر فيه عدم الرضا فيكون التلف مضافاً إلى القتل دون الإذن بخلاف المأمور بالعتق حيث لا يضمن لأن المأمور لا يملك الإعتاق إلا بالإذن فصار المعتق متلقياً بسبب الإذن فيصير التلف محالاً إلى الإذن. ولو أكره المولى بحبس أو قتل فقتله يضمن قيمته استحساناً ويقتص القاتل قياساً. وجه الاستحسان أن الإذن إذا فسد بالإكراه لفوات الرضا معتبر من وجه وفعل المأذون كفعل الآذن فأورث شبهة فلم يجب القصاص فأوجبنا الدية صوناً لدمه عن الهدر، ولو أكره المولى بقتل على بيع عبده وتسليمه والمشتري بالقتل على الشراء والقبض ثم أكره المشتري من على قتله بقتل فللمولى أن يقتل المكره قياساً لأن المشتري مكره على القتل فصار فعله منقولاً إلى المكره، ويضمن قيمته استحساناً لأن العبد مملوك للمشتري وللبائع فيه حق الاسترداد فكان القصاص للبائع من وجه وللمشتري من وجه فكان المستحق للقصاص مجهولاً فلا يكون لأحدهما حق استيفاء القصاص فأوجبنا القيمة على المكره في ماله للبائع لأن للبائع حق الاسترداد وقد أبطل المشتري هذا الحق عليه بالقتل بغير رضاه، فلو أكره أو قيد على بحبس البيع والقبض والمشتري على الشراء بقتل ثم أكره المشتري على قتله بقتل فقتله يضمن قيمته لمولاه ثم يقتل المكره بالعبد قصاصاً لأن المشتري طائع في القبض مكره في الشراء فملك المشتري العبد بعقد فاسد فكان مضموناً عليه بالقيمة وقتله صار منقولاً إلى المكره فصار المكره قاتلاً عبداً عمداً فيجب القصاص. ولو أكره المشتري على الشراء بحبس وللبائع بقتل ثم أكره المشتري على القتل بقتل فقتله فالولي بالخيار إن شاء ضمن المكره قيمة عبده، وإن شاء ضمن لأنه طائع في القبض وقد قتله المكره بقتل المشتري فيجب القصاص اهـ.
And regarding freeing (a slave) and divorce: if he does it, it takes effect, and he (the coerced) has recourse (against the coercer). So if he kills him intentionally, the killer is executed because permission for killing is not valid with coercion, and because it is an utterance in which lack of consent does not affect it; so the destruction is attributed to the killing, not the permission, unlike the one commanded to free (a slave) where he is not liable, because the one commanded does not own the act of freeing except by permission, so the manumitter derives the act from the permission, and thus destruction is referred to the permission. And if the owner is coerced by imprisonment or killing and he kills (his slave), he is liable for its value by istihsan, and the killer is subject to retaliation by qiyas. The reason for istihsan is that if the permission is invalidated by coercion due to loss of consent, which is considered in some sense, and the act of the permitted person is like the act of the permitter, so it creates doubt, and thus retaliation is not required; so we impose blood money to preserve his blood from being wasted. And if the owner is coerced by killing to sell his slave and hand him over, and the buyer is coerced by killing to buy and take possession, then the buyer is coerced to kill him by killing, then the owner may kill the coercer by qiyas because the buyer is coerced to kill, so his act is transferred to the coercer, and he is liable for its value by istihsan because the slave is owned by the buyer and the seller has a right of recovery, so the right to retaliation belongs partly to the seller and partly to the buyer, and thus the one entitled to retaliation is unknown, so neither has the right to execute retaliation, so we impose the value on the coercer from his property for the seller because the seller has the right of recovery and the buyer has nullified this right against him by killing without his consent. If he is coerced or shackled for imprisonment? for sale and possession, and the buyer is coerced by killing to buy, then the buyer is coerced to kill him by killing, and he kills him, he is liable for his value to his owner, then the coercer is killed for the slave as retaliation because the buyer is voluntary in possession and coerced in purchase, so the buyer owns the slave by a defective contract, so he is liable for the value, and his killing is transferred to the coercer, so the coercer becomes a deliberate killer of a slave, so retaliation is obligatory. And if the buyer is coerced to buy by imprisonment, and the seller by killing, then the buyer is coerced to kill by killing, and he kills him, then the guardian has the option: if he wishes he can claim the value of his slave from the coercer, and if he wishes he can claim because he is voluntary in possession and the coercer killed him by killing the buyer, so retaliation is obligatory.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage begins with the matn ruling that if a person is coerced to free a slave or divorce his wife, the act is valid and takes effect, and the coerced person can seek compensation from the coercer. The commentary then discusses ancillary issues: if a person is coerced to kill, the permission is invalid, so the killer is executed. In contrast, if one is commanded to free a slave, the act is valid and the commander is liable. Then cases of coerced sale and purchase are explored, particularly when both seller and buyer are coerced, leading to complex questions of liability for killing the slave. The Hanafi jurists use istihsan to avoid retaliation in cases of doubt and impose blood money instead. The final case involves a buyer coerced by imprisonment, leading to options for the guardian.

Key Terms
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference; departing from qiyas to a more equitable ruling.
قياس (qiyās): Analogical reasoning; applying a ruling from an original case to a new case due to shared effective cause.
شبهة (shubha): Doubt; uncertainty that prevents application of fixed penalties.
دية (diyah): Blood money; financial compensation paid to the heirs of a victim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first case of the passage, why is the owner not executed for killing his slave under coercion?
A) Because killing a slave is always a lesser offense.
B) Because the coercion created doubt about who is truly responsible.
C) Because the owner was merely following orders.
D) Because the slave's value is equivalent to blood money.

Key Takeaway
Istihsan overrides qiyas when coercion creates doubt, substituting blood money for retaliation in killing cases.

Istilad by an Interdicted Person
Ruling on istilad and umm walad by interdicted person

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on istilad by an interdicted person and explain the legal consequences for the umm walad based on the presence or absence of a child.

Core Idea
Istilad by an interdicted person is valid; the child's presence exempts the umm walad from working for value.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a nuanced issue: can an interdicted person (mahjur 'alayh) validly claim paternity of a child born to his slave woman? The ruling answers yes, with important distinctions based on whether a child is born. The reasoning uses qiyas to the case of a sick debtor and highlights the role of the child as a witness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and main ruling
Determine that the act is istilad (claiming paternity of a child from a slave woman) performed by an interdicted person. Note that the child is free, the mother becomes umm walad, and the mother cannot be sold.
This is the default ruling from the text.
2
Trace the qiyas analogy
Understand that the ruling is analogized to the case of a sick debtor (marid madin) who declares istilad. The same effects apply: the slave woman becomes umm walad and is freed upon the master's death from his capital.
The illa (effective cause) is the benefit and need for the child.
3
Distinguish between the two scenarios
Compare the case where there is a child vs. where there is no child. With child: neither mother nor child works for value (sa'y). Without child: the mother works for her full value after the master's death.
The child acts as a witness, making the master's claim stronger.
4
Understand the underlying rationale
Recognize that the child's lineage serves as external proof (shahid) of the master's claim, removing the need for sa'y. Without a child, only the master's word remains, which is insufficient, so sa'y is imposed.
This mirrors the sick debtor case where the same distinction applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن جاءت جاريته بولد فادعاه ثبت نسبه منه وكانت الأمة أم ولد له والولد حر لأنه في إلحاقه بالمصلح في الاستيلاد توفير النظر لاحتياجه إليه ويلحق هذا الحكم بالمريض المديون وتعتق من جميع ماله بموته ولا تسعى ولا ولدها في شيء بخلاف ما إذا أعتقها من غير أن يدعي الولد ولو لم يكن معها ولد فقال المحجور هذه أم ولدي كانت بمنزلة أم الولد لا يقدر على بيعها، فإذا مات المولى سعت في كل قيمتها بمنزلة المريض إذا قال لأمته هذه أم ولدي وليس معها ولد لأنها إذا كانت معها ولد فثبوت نسب الولد بمنزلة الشاهد بخلاف ما إذا لم يكن معها ولد لأنه شاهد معها.
And if his slave woman comes with a child and he claims it, his lineage is established from him, and the slave woman becomes an umm walad for him, and the child is free, because in attributing it to him by [؟] in istilad there is consideration of his benefit due to his need for it. And this ruling is attached to the sick debtor; she becomes free from all his property at his death, and neither she nor her child works for anything, unlike if he freed her without claiming the child. And if she did not have a child and he said, 'This is my umm walad,' she is like an umm walad — he cannot sell her. When the master dies, she works for her entire value, like the case of a sick person who says to his slave woman, 'This is my umm walad,' and she has no child, because when she has a child, the establishment of the child's lineage is like a witness, unlike when there is no child, because he is a witness along with her.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage deals with istilad, the act of claiming paternity of a child born to one's slave woman. If an interdicted person (mahjur 'alayh) claims the child, the child is free and the mother becomes umm walad, meaning she will be freed upon his death and cannot be sold. The reasoning given is that this is in his best interest (tawfir al-nazar) because he needs the child. This ruling is analogized to a sick person in debt (marid madin). In the case where there is a child, the mother does not have to work (sa'y) for her value after the master's death, because the child's lineage serves as proof of his claim. However, if he simply declares her umm walad without a child, she still cannot be sold, but after his death she must work for her full value, because there is no external witness (the child) to confirm his claim — he is the only witness, which is insufficient. This parallels the sick person's case: if he declares a childless slave woman as umm walad, she must work for value.

Key Terms
استيلاد (istilad): The act of a master claiming a child born to his slave woman, thereby making her umm walad.
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave woman who has borne a child to her master, resulting in her freedom upon his death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If an interdicted person's slave woman gives birth and he claims the child, what is the status of the slave woman?
A) She remains a slave and can be sold.
B) She becomes umm walad and is freed immediately.
C) She becomes umm walad but must work for her value after his death.
D) She becomes umm walad and does not work for value.

Key Takeaway
Istilad by an interdicted person is valid; the presence of a child eliminates the mother's obligation to work for value, as the child's lineage serves as a witness to the claim.

Marriage and Mahr by an Interdicted Person
Ruling on validity of marriage and mahr by the interdicted person.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on the validity of marriage by an interdicted person, apply the cap of mahr al-mithl, and calculate the mahr due upon divorce before consummation.

Core Idea
Interdicted person's marriage is valid, but mahr is limited to mahr al-mithl; excess is void.

In Hanafi fiqh, an interdicted person (safih) is one whose financial dealings are restricted due to foolishness. However, marriage, being a solemn contract unaffected by jest, remains valid even if he enters it. This lesson examines the ruling on marriage and dower (mahr) by an interdicted person, focusing on the limitation of mahr to the proper dower (mahr al-mithl) and the consequences of divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Subject and Ruling
The subject is an interdicted person (safih) who marries. The text states: 'وإن تزوج امرأة جاز النكاح' (If he marries a woman, the marriage is valid). Note that this is a straightforward ruling: marriage is valid.
2
Parse the Reasoning: Why Marriage is Valid
The text explains: 'لأنه لا يؤثر فيه الهزل فلا يؤثر فيه السفه' (because jest does not affect it, so foolishness does not affect it). Marriage is not invalidated by hazl (jest) or safah (foolishness). This differentiates marriage from other contracts that may be affected by such factors.
Hazl and safah are not barriers to marriage validity.
3
Parse the Mahr Ruling: Cap at Mahr al-Mithl
If the interdicted person specifies a mahr, only the amount equal to mahr al-mithl (proper dower) is valid; any excess is void. 'فإذا سمى لها مهراً جاز منه مقدار مهر مثلها وبطل الفضل' (If he specifies a dower, from it an amount equal to her proper dower is valid, and the excess is void).
The excess is batil, not the entire mahr.
4
Apply to Pre-Consummation Divorce
If he divorces her before consummation, she is entitled to half of mahr al-mithl from the specified amount. 'وإذا طلقها قبل الدخول وجب نصف مقدار مهر المثل من المسمى' (if he divorces her before consummation, half of the amount of the proper dower from the specified is obligatory).
Half of mahr al-mithl, not half of the stipulated excessive amount.
5
Extend to Multiple Marriages
The same rule applies 'لو تزوج أربع نسوة أو تزوج كل يوم واحدة وطلقها' (if he marries four women, or marries one every day and divorces her). The mahr is always capped at mahr al-mithl for each marriage.
Consistency: the ruling covers serial or simultaneous marriages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن تزوج امرأة جاز النكاح لأنه لا يؤثر فيه الهزل فلا يؤثر فيه السفه، فإذا سمى لها مهراً جاز منه مقدار مهر مثلها وبطل الفضل، وإذا طلقها قبل الدخول وجب نصف مقدار مهر المثل من المسمى . وكذا لو تزوج أربع نسوة أو تزوج كل يوم واحدة وطلقها.
And if he marries a woman, the marriage is valid because jest does not affect it, so foolishness does not affect it. If he specifies a dower for her, an amount equal to her proper dower (mahr al-mithl) is valid from it, and the excess is void. And if he divorces her before consummation, half of the amount of the proper dower from the specified is obligatory. Similarly, if he marries four women, or marries one every day and divorces her.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage states a clear ruling: the marriage (nikaah) contracted by an interdicted person (safih) is valid, because marriage, like other contracts based on mutual consent, is not invalidated by frivolity (hazl) or foolishness (safah). However, the financial aspect is restricted: the mahr (dower) he stipulates is limited to mahr al-mithl (the proper dower of a woman of her status). Any excess is void (batil). If he divorces her before consummation, she is entitled to half of mahr al-mithl from the specified amount, not half of the stated excessive dower. The same rule applies if he marries multiple women or divorces serially; the mahr is always capped at mahr al-mithl. This protects the interdicted person's estate from waste while allowing valid marital relations.

Key Terms
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): The proper dower appropriate for a woman of her status, considering family, age, beauty, etc.
هزل (hazl): Jest or frivolity; a factor that can invalidate certain contracts but not marriage.
سفه (safah): Foolishness or lack of prudence; ground for interdiction (hajr).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
An interdicted man marries a woman and stipulates a mahr of 1000 dirhams, but her mahr al-mithl is 500 dirhams. He then divorces her before consummation. How much is she entitled to?
A) 500 dirhams (half of the mahr al-mithl).
B) 1000 dirhams (the stipulated mahr).
C) 250 dirhams (half of the mahr al-mithl).
D) Nothing, because the marriage is invalid.

Key Takeaway
An interdicted person's marriage is valid, but the mahr is limited to mahr al-mithl; excess is void, and pre-consummation divorce entitles half of mahr al-mithl.

Marriage and Slave Marriage Restrictions for the Ma'dhun
Explains prohibition on ma'dhun marrying or giving slaves in marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the ma'dhun's capacity to marry and give slaves in marriage, and explain the reasoning behind the prohibition.

Core Idea
The ma'dhun's permission is for trade only; marriage is not trade and harms the master.

This lesson examines a key limitation on the ma'dhun (a slave permitted to trade): he cannot marry or arrange marriages for his slaves. The ruling is based on the principle that the ma'dhun's authority is confined to commerce, and marriage imposes financial obligations that harm the master. We will also explore an intra-Hanafi disagreement and correct a common error in al-Hidayah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is prohibited? The ma'dhun is forbidden from marrying (for himself) and from giving his slave (male or female) in marriage. State the ruling as a prohibition (haram/makruh tahrimi).
The source uses 'لا يتزوج' and 'لا يزوج مملوكه'.
2
Analyze the rationale (ta'lil)
Two reasons are given: (1) marriage is not trade, so it falls outside the scope of permission; (2) it imposes mahr and nafaqa on the ma'dhun's liability, harming the master. Identify these reasons in the text.
The 'ضرر على المولى' is a key point.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf (the 'second') differs: he permits giving female slaves in marriage, analogizing it to hiring. Identify his evidence and the majority response.
The text says 'وقال الثاني: يزوج الأمة دون العبد'.
4
Understand the application to similar parties
The same prohibition applies to other limited-capacity agents like the minor and insane who are permitted to trade, the mudarib, and partnership types. Note that the father and executor are excluded from this ruling.
The source corrects an error in al-Hidayah that included the father and executor.
5
Summarize the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon view is the prohibition in all cases. The author sides with the majority, rejecting Abu Yusuf's exception.
The tarjih authority is the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( ولا يتزوج لأنه ليس من باب التجارة ولأنه فيه ضرر على المولى لوجوب المهر والنفقة في رقبته . وفي المحيط : حجر المأذون ولو اشترى المأذون أمة فتسرى بها وولدت له ثبت نسبه منه ولا يخرج الأمة وولدها من التجارة وكذا لو تزوج أمة بغير بينة بإذن المولى لم تخرج الأمة وولدها من التجارة، فإن كان النكاح ببينة خرجت من التجارة. قال الحاكم أبو الفضل : يحتمل أن يكون هذا الجواب في أمة بأجرة .اهـ. قال رحمه الله : (ولا يزوج مملوكه) أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان عليه دين أو لا زوجها من المولى ولا دين عليه أو من غيره . وقال الثاني : يزوج الأمة دون العبد لأن فيه تحصيل النفقة والمهر فأشبه إجارتها ولهذا جاز الأب والأب ولهما أن الإذن يتناول التجارة والتزويج ليس منها ولهذا لا للمكاتب ووصي الأمة يملك تزويج العبد بالاتفاق والأب والوصي تصرفهما بالنظر إلى الصغير وفي تزويج النظر المذكور، وعلى هذا الخلاف الصبي والمعتوه المأذون لهما والمضارب والشريك عناناً ومفاوضة، وما في الهداية من أن الأب والوصي على هذا الخلاف سبق قلم من الكاتب فإنه ذكر المسألة بنفسها في كتاب المكاتب مثل ما ذكرنا ولم يذكر فيهما خلافاً بل جعلهما كالمكاتب، وكذا في عامة كتب أصحابنا كالمبسوط ومختصر الكافي والتتمة. قيدنا بقولنا
He (the author), may Allah have mercy on him, said: (And he does not marry because it is not from the realm of trade, and because therein is harm to the master due to the obligation of the dower and maintenance upon his body. And in al-Muhit: The interdiction of the ma'dhun. And if the ma'dhun bought a slave-girl and had intercourse with her as a concubine and she gave birth, his lineage from him is established, and the slave-girl and her child do not exit from trade. Similarly, if he married a slave-girl without witnesses with the master's permission, the slave-girl and her child do not exit from trade. If the marriage was with witnesses, she exits from trade. Al-Hakim Abu al-Fadl said: It is possible that this answer concerns a slave-girl for hire. End. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And he does not give his slave in marriage) generically, including whether he is in debt or not, whether he marries her to the master and he has no debt, or to another. And the second (i.e., Abu Yusuf?) said: He gives the female slave in marriage but not the male slave, because in it there is acquisition of maintenance and dower, so it resembles hiring her. For this reason, the father and the (guardian?) are permissible. And they have that permission encompasses trade, and marriage is not from it, and for this reason, the mukatib and the executor of the female slave do not have the right to give the male slave in marriage by agreement. And the father and the executor act by consideration of the minor. And upon this difference, the minor and the insane who are permitted, and the mudarib, and the partner of 'inan and mufawada. And what is in al-Hidayah that the father and executor are upon this difference is a slip of the pen of the writer, for he mentioned the issue itself in the Book of the Mukatib as we mentioned, and he did not mention any difference regarding them, but rather made them like the mukatib. Similarly in the general books of our companions like al-Mabsut, Mukhtasar al-Kafi, and al-Tatimma. We restrict with our saying.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the prohibition on the ma'dhun marrying or giving his slave in marriage. The author states that the ma'dhun cannot marry because marriage is not commerce and imposes two financial obligations (mahr and nafaqa) that become a lien on the slave's body, harming the master. Al-Muhit provides additional details: if a ma'dhun buys a slave-girl and takes her as a concubine, she and her child remain part of the trade; similarly, if he marries a slave-girl without witnesses by the master's permission, she remains in trade; but if the marriage is with witnesses, she exits trade. There is a nuance from al-Hakim Abu al-Fadl suggesting this may apply only to a hired slave-girl. Then the text discusses giving slaves in marriage: the ma'dhun is not allowed to give his slave in marriage, whether male or female, regardless of debt. Abu Yusuf (the 'second') reportedly allowed giving female slaves in marriage because it secures maintenance and dower, akin to hiring. The commentary then clarifies that the father and executor (of a minor) have different rules—they act in the minor's interest. The passage corrects a mistake in al-Hidayah that included the father and executor in the same ruling; the author argues the correct view is that they are like the mukatib (who cannot give in marriage). The restriction is based on the principle that the ma'dhun's permission is for trade, not marriage.

Key Terms
تسرى (tasarrā): To take a slave-girl as a concubine (surriyya), which establishes lineage and specific legal effects.
مكاتب (mukātab): A slave who has a contract of manumission (kitaba) with his master, paying installments for freedom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, can a ma'dhun marry?
A) Yes, because he has permission to trade.
B) No, because marriage is not trade and causes financial harm to the master.
C) Only if he has no debt.
D) Only with the master's permission.

Key Takeaway
The ma'dhun's permission is confined to trade; marriage and arranging marriages are outside his authority.

Attending a Wedding Feast with Prohibited Activities
Ruling on walima with prohibited activities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on attending a walima with un-Islamic activities and explain why the sunnah becomes binding once present.

Core Idea
A sunnah becomes binding once you commit; do not abandon it due to later innovations.

You receive an invitation to a wedding feast (walima), but you know or discover that there will be prohibited play and singing. What should you do? The Hanafi school provides a nuanced ruling: the invitation is a confirmed sunnah, but once you arrive, the host's right becomes binding, and you should stay and eat unless you can prevent the wrong. This lesson unpacks the evidence, the objections, and the three distinct scenarios.

Guided Path
1
State the basic ruling
Identify what the matn says: the invitee sits and eats when play and singing occur after arrival. He does not leave.
2
Locate the primary proof
The hadith ‘Whoever does not accept the invitation has disobeyed’ is cited. Understand that this threat elevates the sunnah to the strength of an obligation.
3
Analyse the objection and response
An objector argues that enduring prohibitions for a sunnah is not like doing so for an obligatory duty. The response: the sunnah turns into an obligation once present because the host's right becomes binding, analogous to a nafl prayer becoming obligatory once started.
4
Distinguish the three scenarios
The commentator outlines three cases: (1) no innovation, (2) innovation unknown before arrival, (3) innovation known beforehand. In the first two, attending is sunnah and one should stay; in the third, acceptance is not incumbent.
5
Apply the ruling to a new situation
Use the three-case framework to decide what a person should do when invited to a gathering where there might be music: if known beforehand, he may decline; if discovered after arriving, he stays and advises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ومن دعي إلى وليمة وثمة لعب وغناء يقعد ويأكل) يعني إذا أحدث اللعب والغناء بعد حضوره يقعد ويأكل ولا يترك ولا يخرج ولا يخفى أن قوله وثم إلى آخره» جملة حالية عن نائب فاعل دعي فيفيد وجود ذلك حال الدعوة، فلو قال فحضر لعب لكان أولى فتأمل . وعللوا ذلك بأن إجابة الدعوة سنة لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام من لم يجب الدعوة فقد عصى فلا يتركها لما اقترن بها من البدعة كصلاة الجنازة لأجل النائحة فإن قدر على أبا القاسم (1) ، المنع منع من غيره قال في العناية أخذاً من النهاية : قيل عليه أنه قياس السنة على الفرض وهو غير مستقيم فإنه لا يلزم من تحمل المحذور لأجل الفرض تحمله لأجل السنة. أجيب بأنها سنة في قوة الواجب لورود الوعيد على تركها لقوله فقد عصى أبا القاسم» الحديث. فأورد على أن هذا بأنهم أرادوا بقولهم في قوة الواجب مثل الواجب في الأحكام فهو مشكل لوجوب الفرق بينهما في الأحكام بأن تارك الواجب يستحق العقوبة بالنار وتارك السنة لا يستحقها بل حرمان الشفاعة وإن أرادوا بأنها في قوة الواجب مجرد بيان تأكيد السنة فلا نفعاً. وأجيب بأن إجابة الدعوة وإن كانت سنة عندنا ابتداء إلا أنها تنقلب إلى الواجب بقاء بعد الحضور حيث يلزمه حق الدعوة بالتزامه فصار نظير الصلاة النافلة تنتقل إلى الواجب بل إلى الفرض بالتزامه بالمشروع ؛ أشار إليه صاحب الهداية فيكون قوله «كصلاة الجنازة» قياس واجب على واجب. وبيان تقريب الدليل ببيان الدعوى على ثلاثة أوجه : الأول إذا دعي إلى وليمة أو طعام ولم يكن ثمة شيء من البدع أصلاً، والثاني إذا دعي إلى ذلك ولم يذكر حين الدعوة أن ثمة شيء من البدع أصلاً ولم يعلمه المدعو قبل الحضور ولكن هجم عليه، والثالث إذا ادعى إلى ذلك وذكر إن ثمة شيء من البدع فعلمه المدعوّ قبل الحضور ففي الوجهين الأولين كانت الدعوة على وجه السنة فلا تكون الإجابة لازمة للمدعو ا هـ . وهذا (1) رواه البخاري في كتاب النكاح باب ۷۲ مسلم في كتاب النكاح حديث ۱۰۷، ۱۱۰. أبو داود في كتاب الأطعمة باب .١. الموطأ في كتاب النكاح حديث ٥٠ أحمد في مسنده (٢/ ٦١).
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And whoever is invited to a wedding feast and there is play and singing, he sits and eats.) Meaning if play and singing occur after his presence, he sits and eats, and does not leave nor exit. It is not hidden that his statement 'and there is' etc. is a circumstantial clause about the implicit subject of 'is invited', so it indicates that existence of that at the time of invitation. So if he said 'then he attended and there was play', it would be better, so reflect. And they justified that because accepting the invitation is sunnah, due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'Whoever does not accept the invitation has disobeyed.' So he should not leave it due to the innovation that accompanies it, like the funeral prayer for the sake of the wailing woman. If he is able to prevent, he prevents; otherwise, he does not. He said in al-'Inaya, taking from al-Nihaya: It was said against it that it is analogizing sunnah to obligatory, and it is incorrect, because enduring an objectionable thing for an obligatory act does not entail enduring it for a sunnah. It was answered that it is a sunnah in the strength of obligatory due to the threat on leaving it, because of his saying 'has disobeyed Abu al-Qasim' hadith. Then it was objected that if they meant by 'in the strength of obligatory' like obligatory in rulings, it is problematic because there must be a difference between them in rulings: the one who leaves an obligatory is deserving of punishment by fire, and the one who leaves a sunnah does not deserve it but only deprivation of intercession. If they meant that it is in the strength of obligatory merely as a statement of emphasis of the sunnah, it does not benefit. And it was answered that accepting the invitation, although it is sunnah initially according to us, yet it turns into obligatory after presence, because the right of the invitation becomes binding on him by his commitment; so it becomes like the supererogatory prayer that turns into obligatory, rather obligatory, by committing to the prescribed. Al-Hidayah's author indicated this. So his statement 'like funeral prayer' is a qiyas of obligatory on obligatory. And the clarification of the evidence by explaining the claim is on three aspects: First, if he is invited to a wedding feast or food and there is no innovation at all. Second, if he is invited to that and no innovation is mentioned at the time of invitation, and the invitee did not know before attending, but it came upon him suddenly. Third, if he is invited to that and it is mentioned that there is innovation, so the invitee knows before attending. In the first two aspects, the invitation is on the basis of sunnah, so acceptance is not incumbent on the invitee. And this hadith: narrated by Bukhari in Nikah chapter 72, Muslim in Nikah hadith 107,110, Abu Dawood in At'ima chapter 1, Muwatta in Nikah hadith 50, Ahmad in his Musnad (2/61).
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq discusses the ruling for a person invited to a walima (wedding feast) where there is play and singing (lahw wa ghina). The matn states that such a person should sit and eat, not leave. The commentator explains that this applies when the play and singing occur after the person has already arrived; if they had known beforehand, they might not have attended. The justification is that accepting an invitation to a walima is a sunnah, as evidenced by the hadith: 'Whoever does not accept the invitation has disobeyed.' Therefore, one should not abandon this sunnah due to accompanying innovations, similar to attending a funeral prayer despite the presence of a wailing woman (which is forbidden). If one can prevent the innovation, they should; otherwise, they proceed. A objection is raised: this analogy equates a sunnah (walima) with an obligatory act (funeral prayer is fard kifayah), which is problematic because one is not required to endure harm for a sunnah as for an obligatory act. The answer is that accepting a walima invitation is a sunnah that has the strength of a wajib (obligatory) due to the threat in the hadith. However, this is further critiqued: a wajib and a sunnah have different consequences (punishment vs. loss of intercession). The final resolution is that accepting the invitation is sunnah initially, but once present, it becomes obligatory to fulfill the host's right, similar to a nafl prayer becoming obligatory once one begins it. The Hidayah author supports this. The doctrine is summarized in three cases: (1) no innovation at all – sunnah; (2) innovation not known before but encountered – still attend; (3) innovation known beforehand – acceptance is not incumbent because the invitation was not purely on the sunnah basis. The hadith is referenced from major collections.

Key Terms
وليمة (walīma): A wedding feast, which is a sunnah to accept invitations to.
بدعة (bidʿa): Innovation in religion; here it refers to prohibited acts like play and singing that may accompany the feast.
سنة في قوة الواجب (sunna fī quwwat al-wājib): A sunnah that has the strength of an obligation due to the threat of punishment for leaving it, though technically not wajib.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in which scenario is acceptance of a walima invitation not incumbent on the invitee?
A) When no innovation is present at all.
B) When innovation is present but unknown before arrival.
C) When innovation is known before arrival.
D) In all scenarios it is incumbent.

Key Takeaway
The ruling on attending a walima depends on prior knowledge; once present, the host's right makes fulfilling the invitation binding.
