Relying on a Trustworthy Report for a Woman's 'Iddah and Remarriage
Rulings on a woman acting upon news of husband's death or divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when a woman may observe 'iddah and remarry based on a trustworthy report of her husband's death or divorce, applying the criterion of predominant opinion (akbar al-ra'y).

Core Idea
A woman may remarry after news of her husband's death based on a trustworthy report if her predominant opinion deems it true.

In the Hanafi school, a woman does not need absolute certainty to act on news of her husband's death or divorce. Instead, she may rely on a trustworthy person's report if her predominant opinion (akbar al-ra'y) confirms its truth. This lesson unpacks the key scenarios from the text, including the role of a divorce letter, the case of an invalid marriage, and situations where testimony conflicts with denial.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario
The passage begins with a woman whose husband is absent. A trustworthy Muslim (thiqah) informs her that the husband died or divorced her with a triple divorce. Alternatively, she receives a letter of divorce whose authenticity she cannot confirm.
The reporter must be a Muslim of trustworthy character (thiqah).
2
Apply the criterion of predominant opinion
The woman must assess her predominant opinion (akbar al-ra'y) regarding the truth of the report. If she leans toward its truth, she may observe 'iddah and remarry. If her predominant opinion is that the report is false, she cannot remarry.
This is a subjective standard, not objective certainty (yaqin).
3
Distinguish the case of an invalid marriage
If a man informs her that her original marriage contract was invalid (fâsid), she may remarry based on his statement even if he is trustworthy (or even if he is not, per the commentary). This is because the marriage's invalidity can be easily verified through the contract.
The threshold is lower here; trustworthiness is not required since verification is possible.
4
Analyze the scenario of conflicting testimony
If two witnesses testify to the husband's death or triple divorce, but the woman denies it, and then the witnesses die or disappear before testifying in court, she cannot stay with the husband, allow intimacy, or remarry another. She is in a legal stalemate.
The woman's denial prevents her from benefiting from the testimony, yet she cannot ignore it.
5
Examine the ruling for a slave woman
If two just witnesses testify before a slave woman that her master freed her, but the master denies it, she may prevent the master from intimate relations and other acts. This parallels the earlier ruling but with different consequences.
Here the woman can take defensive action (prevent intimacy) but cannot marry until the matter is resolved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رأيه أنه صادق، وإن كان أكبر رأيه أنه كاذب لا يتزوج إلا ثلاثاً امرأة غاب عنها زوجها فأخبرها مسلم ثقة بأنه مات أو طلقها ثلاثاً وكان غيره ثقة أو أتاها كتاب بالطلاق ولا تدري أهو كتابه أو لا إلا أن أكبر رأيها أنه حق فلا بأس أن تعتد وتتزوج، ولو أخبرها رجل أن أصل النكاح كان فاسداً أن تتزوج بقوله وإن كان ثقة. ولو شهدا للمرأة أن زوجها طلقها ثلاثاً أو مات وهي تجحد ثم ماتا أو غابا قبل الشهادة عند القاضي لم يسع المرأة أن تقيم معه ولا أن تمكنه من نفسها ولا أن تتزوج بغيره، وكذا إذا سمعت الطلاق منه وهو يجحد فحلفه القاضي وردها إليه لم يسعها المقام عنده ولا أن تعتد وتتزوج بغيره ولو شهد عند الأمة عدلان أن مولاها أعتقها وهو يجحد تمنعه من القربان وغيره؛ كذا في المحيط مختصراً.
His opinion is that he is truthful, and if his predominant opinion is that he is lying, she should not marry except after three... A woman whose husband was absent, and a trustworthy Muslim informed her that he died or divorced her three times, and another trustworthy person [informed her] or a divorce letter came to her and she does not know whether it is his letter or not, unless her predominant opinion is that it is true, then there is no harm in her observing her waiting period and marrying. If a man informed her that the original marriage was invalid, she may marry based on his statement even if he is trustworthy. If two witnesses testified for the woman that her husband divorced her three times or died, while she denies it, then they died or disappeared before the testimony before the judge, it is not permissible for the woman to stay with him, nor to allow him access to herself, nor to marry another. Similarly, if she heard the divorce from him while he denies it, and the judge made him swear and returned her to him, it is not permissible for her to stay with him, nor to observe waiting period and marry another. If two just witnesses testified before the slave woman that her master freed her while he denies it, she prevents him from intimacy and other things; likewise in al-Muhit briefly.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage presents several rulings related to a woman relying on reports about her absent husband. The first ruling: if a trustworthy Muslim tells her that her husband died or divorced her with a triple divorce, and her predominant opinion (akbar al-ra'y) is that he is truthful, she may observe the 'iddah (waiting period) and remarry. However, if her predominant opinion is that he is lying, she cannot remarry. The text also covers a letter of divorce whose authenticity she doubts; again the criterion is her predominant opinion. A second ruling: if a man tells her that the original marriage contract was invalid (fâsid), she may remarry based on his statement even if he is trustworthy – this implies a lower threshold because she can easily verify through the marriage contract. Then the passage addresses cases where two witnesses claim divorce or death, but the woman denies it, and the witnesses die or disappear before testifying in court. In such circumstances, she cannot stay with the husband, allow intimacy, or remarry; she is in a confusing situation. Similarly, if she overhears the husband saying divorce but he denies it, and the court makes him swear and returns her to him, she still cannot stay with him or remarry. For a slave woman, if two just witnesses testify that her master freed her, but the master denies it, she can prevent him from sexual intercourse and other intimate acts. These rulings are taken from al-Muhit and show the importance of certainty and testimony in marital and slave matters.

Key Terms
ثقة (thiqah): A trustworthy person whose reports are considered reliable in legal matters.
أكبر الرأي (akbar al-ra'y): The predominant opinion or preponderant belief after considering evidence, as opposed to certainty.
عدلان (adlan): Two just witnesses whose testimony is legally acceptable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Two male witnesses confirming the death.
B) A court order declaring the husband dead.
C) Her predominant opinion (akbar al-ra'y) that the report is true.
D) A written letter from the husband himself.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a woman may observe iddah and remarry based on a trustworthy report of her husband's death or divorce if her predominant opinion (akbar al-ra'y) supports its truth, balancing practicality with caution.

Attending a Walimah with Sinful Activity: When to Stay and When to Leave
Ruling on attending a walimah with lewd play and singing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on attending a wedding feast where play and singing occur, and apply the three scenarios to decide whether to sit and eat or leave.

Core Idea
A wedding invitation is initially sunnah, but after arrival it becomes wajib; yet if sin is known beforehand, do not attend.

Attending a wedding feast (walimah) is a recommended act in Islam, but what happens when the feast includes forbidden activities like musical instruments and singing? The Hanafi school provides nuanced guidance based on whether the guest knew beforehand or discovered the sin after arriving. This lesson unpacks the ruling, the underlying proof, and the important distinction between initial and ongoing obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act in question is responding to an invitation to a walimah. Determine the default hukm according to the Hanafi school: it is sunnah mu'akkadah, based on the hadith 'Whoever does not respond has disobeyed.'
The hadith indicates strong emphasis, but the school classifies it as sunnah initially.
2
Analyze the change after arriving
Understand that once the guest arrives and commits to attending, the invitation becomes wajib (obligatory) by virtue of the guest's commitment (iltizam). This is analogous to a supererogatory prayer becoming obligatory once started.
This shift explains why leaving after arriving is problematic.
3
Apply the three scenarios
The text outlines three scenarios: (1) no sin at the feast – invitation is sunnah; (2) sin occurs after arrival (or unknown beforehand) – guest should sit and eat, and try to prevent sin if able; (3) sin is known before attending – guest should not attend.
In scenario 2, the guest is not held accountable for the sin of others.
4
Examine the proof and rebuttals
The primary proof is the hadith with the word 'عصى' (disobeyed). The passage addresses objections about comparing sunnah to fard, and resolves by noting the obligation after commitment. Also note the Hadith of 'Ali and Ibn 'Umar regarding avoiding gatherings with images or wine.
The generality of the hadith is specified by texts that forbid participating in sin.
5
Derive the practical ruling
Synthesize the ruling: if you arrive and sin is already present, you may sit and eat but should try to stop it; if sin begins after arrival, you may stay; if you know beforehand, do not attend. The mufta bihi is that attendance becomes wajib after arrival.
This ruling balances the sunnah of responding and the prohibition of participating in sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

قدر على المنع منع وإلا دخل في جملتهم. قال في العناية أخذاً من النهاية : قيل عليه أنه قياس السنة على الفرض وهو غير مستقيم فإنه لا يلزم من تحمل المحذور لأجل الفرض تحمله لأجل السنة. أجيب بأنها سنة في قوة ولكن هجم عليه، والثالث إذا ادعى إلى ذلك وذكر إن ثمة شيء من البدع فعلمه المدعوّ قبل الحضور ففي الوجهين الأولين كانت الدعوة على وجه السنة فلا تكون الإجابة لازمة للمدعو ا هـ . وهذا كله من حيث اللفظ فإن كان بعد الحضور، ولو علم قبل الحضور لا يقبله ولقائل أن يقول الحديث المذكور يشمل ما بعد الحضور وما قبله لأنه قد تقرر في الأصول أن المعرف بالألف واللام إذا لم تكن للعهد الخارجي فهو للاستغراق فيعم كل دعوة، وقد يجاب عنه بأنه وإن كان عاماً فهو مخصوص بالنصوص الدالة على وجوب الاجتناب عن اقتراب تلك البدع ممن يقتدى به فلم يقدر على منعهم خرج ولم يقعد لأن في ذلك شين الدين وفتح باب المعصية على المسلمين، وما حكي أن الإمام وقع له ذلك كان قبل أن يصير قدوة. وإن كان ذلك على المائدة فلا يقعد وإن كان هناك لعب وغناء قبل أن يحضر فلا يحضر لأنه لا يلزمه الإجابة إلا إذا كان هناك منكر لما روي عن علي قال : صنعت للنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم طعاماً فدعوته له فحضر فرأى لي البيت تصاوير فرجع. وعن ابن عمر قال : نهى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم عن مطعمين عن الجلوس على مائدة يشرب عليها الخمر وأن يأكل وهو منسطح . رواه أبو داود.
He, may Allah have mercy on him, said: (And whoever is invited to a wedding feast and there is play and singing, he should sit and eat). Meaning: if the play and singing happen after his arrival, he should sit and eat, not leave or exit. It is not hidden that his saying 'and there is' is a circumstantial clause about the passive subject of 'is invited' indicating the existence of that at the time of the invitation. So if he said 'he arrived and play occurred' it would be better, so reflect. They justified this because responding to the invitation is sunnah due to his statement, peace be upon him: 'Whoever does not respond to the invitation has disobeyed', so he should not leave it due to what has become attached to it of innovation, like the funeral prayer because of the wailing woman. If he is able to prevent, he prevents; otherwise he enters among them. He said in al-'Inayah, taking from al-Nihayah: It was said against it that this is analogy of sunnah upon fard and it is not sound, for harm endured for the sake of fard does not necessitate enduring it for the sake of sunnah. It was answered that it is a sunnah in the strength of an obligation because of the threat for leaving it due to his saying 'he has disobeyed Abu al-Qasim' hadith. Then it was objected that if they meant by 'in the strength of an obligation' like the obligation in rulings, it is problematic due to the necessity of distinguishing between them in rulings: the one who leaves an obligation deserves punishment by fire, and the one who leaves a sunnah does not deserve it but rather deprivation of intercession. And if they meant that it is in the strength of an obligation as a mere expression of emphasis of the sunnah, then it is of no benefit. And it was answered that responding to the invitation, although it is sunnah according to us initially, yet it turns into obligation after attendance where he owes the right of the invitation by his commitment, so it becomes similar to the supererogatory prayer that turns into obligation, even into fard, by committing to it through legislation; the author of al-Hidayah indicated this, so his saying 'like the funeral prayer' is analogy of obligation upon obligation. And clarification of the evidence by clarifying the claim is on three facets: First: if he is invited to a wedding feast or food and there is nothing of innovations at all. Second: if he is invited to that and at the time of the invitation it is not mentioned that there is anything of innovations at all, and the invitee does not know before attending, but he stumbles upon it. Third: if he is invited to that and it is mentioned that there is something of innovations, so the invitee knows before attending. So in the first two facets, the invitation is on the basis of sunnah, so acceptance is not binding upon the invitee. End. All this is from the perspective of wording. So if it is after attendance, and even if he knew before attendance, he should not accept it. And someone may say that the aforementioned hadith includes what is after attendance and what is before it, because it is established in principles that the definite article alif lam when not for external covenant is for generality, so it encompasses every invitation. It may be answered that although it is general, it is specified by texts indicating the obligation of avoiding approaching those innovations for one who is an example to be followed. So if he is unable to prevent them, he leaves and does not sit, because in that is disgrace to the religion and opening the door of sin upon the Muslims. And what is narrated that the Imam experienced that was before he became an example. And if that is on the table, he should not sit. And if there is play and singing before he attends, he should not attend, because acceptance is not binding upon him unless there is something disapproved. Because it is narrated from 'Ali: I made food for the Prophet, peace be upon him, and invited him, so he came and saw in my house pictures, so he returned. And from Ibn 'Umar: The Prophet, peace be upon him, forbade regarding two feasts: sitting at a table upon which wine is drunk, and eating while reclining. Narrated by Abu Dawood.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on attending a wedding feast (walimah) where there is play and singing. The author explains that if the play and singing occur after the guest has arrived, he should still sit and eat; he should not leave. The reasoning is that responding to an invitation is a confirmed sunnah, as the hadith states: 'Whoever does not respond to the invitation has disobeyed Abu al-Qasim' (i.e., the Prophet). The passage compares this to the funeral prayer: even if there is a wailing woman present, one still prays but may try to prevent the wailing if able; similarly, one attends the feast but may try to prevent the sinful activity. The passage addresses objections about comparing sunnah to fard, and answers that after attending, the invitation becomes an obligation due to commitment (iltizam). Three scenarios are distinguished: (1) no innovation at all – invitation is sunnah; (2) innovation not mentioned beforehand and only discovered upon arrival – the guest may still attend and eat, as the sin is not his; (3) innovation known before attending – the guest should not attend, because he would be participating in sin. The passage also discusses the hadith's generality and specifiers. Finally, it narrates from 'Ali and Ibn 'Umar about avoiding gatherings with prohibited items like images and wine.

Key Terms
وليمة (walimah): A wedding feast; a meal given by the groom after the marriage is consummated.
بدعة (bid'ah): Innovation in religion; here referring to forbidden activities like musical instruments and singing.
إجابة (ijabah): Responding to an invitation; specifically accepting and attending a walimah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in the text, what should a person do if he is invited to a wedding feast and learns beforehand that there will be singing with musical instruments?
A) He should attend and try to stop the sin.
B) He should not attend because he would be knowingly participating in sin.
C) He should attend but leave immediately if the singing starts.
D) He should attend because the invitation is wajib.

Key Takeaway
Responding to a wedding invitation is strongly recommended, but if you know sinful activities will occur, do not attend; if you discover them after arrival, you may stay and try to prevent them.

The General Prohibition of Amusements (Malahi)
General ruling on amusements being haram with prophetic evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on musical instruments and cite the primary hadith evidence, and distinguish between the degrees of prohibition for listening, sitting, and enjoyment.

Core Idea
All amusements are haram; the hadith explicitly lists musical instruments alongside other prohibited items.

This lesson examines the foundational ruling that all forms of amusement (malahi) are prohibited in the Hanafi school. The passage cites two hadith, including one from Sahih al-Bukhari, that link musical instruments with other unlawful acts. It also discusses the extreme description of listening, sitting, and enjoying such amusement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The passage opens by stating that all amusements (malahi) are haram, including singing with reed instruments. Understand the scope of the prohibition.
2
Cite the primary proof
The hadith 'There will be from my nation people who consider lawful the private parts, silk, wine, and musical instruments' is cited. Recognize that this is a sahih hadith (Bukhari).
The hadith lists ma'azif (musical instruments) among other prohibited items.
3
Analyze the proof’s implication
The hadith equates musical instruments with other clearly prohibited acts, indicating a strong prohibition. The second hadith threatens severe punishment.
The threat of punishment reinforces the gravity.
4
Understand the degrees of prohibition
The passage distinguishes: listening to amusements is sin (ma'siyah), sitting with them is transgression (fisq), and enjoyment is metaphorically called disbelief (kufr).
The text clarifies this is for emphasis, not actual kufr.
5
Note the exception for weddings
Later in the passage, an exception for the drum at weddings is mentioned. This lesson focuses on the general rule; exceptions will be explored in later lessons.

Source Text

ودلت المسألة على أن الملاهي كلها حرام حتى التغني بضرب القصب قال عليه الصلاة والسلام ليكونن من أمتي أقوام يستحلون الحر والحرير والخمر والمعازف» أخرجه البخاري. وفي لفظ آخر «ليشربن اناس من أمتي الخمر يسمونها بغير اسمها يعزف على رؤوسهم بالمعازف والمغنيات يخسف الله الأرض ويجعل منهم القرد والخنازير) (۲) واختلفوا في التغني المجرد قال بعضهم إنه حرام مطلقاً والاستماع إليه معصية لإطلاق الحديث وهو اختيار شيخ الإسلام، ومنهم من قال لا بأس به لیستفید به فهم المعاني والفصاحة، ومنهم من جوز التغني لدفع الوحشة إذا كان وحده ولا يكون على سبيل اللهو وإليه ذهب شمس الأئمة السرخسي لأنه روي بعض الصحابة. ولو كان في الشعر حكم أو قصة لا يكره. وكذا لو كان فيه ذكر امرأة غير معينة، وكذا لو كانت معينة وهي ميتة ولو كانت حية يكره؛ كذا في الشارح. وفي المحيط : ويكره اللعب بالشطرنج والنرد والأربعة لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام كل لعب حرام إلا ملاعبة الرجل زوجته وقوسه وفرسه لأنه يصد عن الجمع والجماعات وسبب للوقوع في فواحش الكلام وغيره واستماع صوت الملاهي حرام كالضرب بالقصب وغيره قال عليه الصلاة والسلام استماع الملاهي معصية والجلوس عليها فسق والتلذذ بها كفر» وهذا خرج على وجه التشديد لا أنه يكفر. وعن الحسن بن زياد لا بأس بأن يكون في العرس دف يضرب به ليشتهر ويعلن النكاح وسئل أبو يوسف أيكره للمرأة أن تضرب في غير فسق للصبي؟ قال : لا أكره ولا تركب امرأة مسلمة على السرج لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام «لعن الله السروج على «الفروج هذا إذا ركبت متلهية أم متزينة لتعرض نفسها على الرجال.
And the issue indicated that all amusements are haram, even singing with the beating of a reed [pipe]. He, peace be upon him, said: 'There will be from my nation people who consider lawful the private parts, silk, wine, and musical instruments.' Narrated by Bukhari. In another wording: 'People from my nation will drink wine, calling it by other names; music will be played over their heads with instruments and female singers; Allah will cause the earth to swallow them and make them into apes and pigs.' They disagreed about mere singing: some said it is absolutely haram and listening to it is sin due to the generality of the hadith, and this is the choice of Shaykh al-Islam; some said there is no harm in it to benefit from understanding meanings and eloquence; some allowed singing to dispel loneliness when alone and not for the purpose of entertainment, and this is the view of Shams al-A'immah al-Sarakhsi because it is reported from some companions. If the poetry contains a moral lesson or story, it is not disliked. Similarly, if it contains mention of an unspecified woman, and also if it is specified and she is dead; but if she is alive, it is disliked. Likewise in the commentator. And in al-Muhit: Playing chess, backgammon, and the four [other games] is disliked due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'Every play is haram except a man's playing with his wife, his bow, and his horse' because it distracts from congregations and causes foul language and other things. And listening to the sound of amusements is haram, like beating reed etc. He, peace be upon him, said: 'Listening to amusements is sin, sitting with them is transgression, and enjoying them is disbelief.' This is said with exaggeration, not that he becomes a disbeliever. From al-Hasan ibn Ziyad: There is no harm in having a drum at the wedding that is beaten to publicize and announce the marriage. And Abu Yusuf was asked: Is it disliked for a woman to beat [drum] for a boy without transgression? He said: I do not dislike it. And a Muslim woman should not ride on a saddle because of his saying, peace be upon him: 'Allah has cursed saddles upon female private parts.' This is if she rides for entertainment or adornment to display herself to men.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage presents a comprehensive ruling on the prohibition of various forms of amusement (malahi). It begins by stating that the previous discussion on the wedding feast demonstrates that all amusements are haram, including singing with reed instruments. It cites two hadith: the first lists what some will consider lawful (private parts, silk, wine, musical instruments); the second warns of punishment for those who drink wine under other names and enjoy music. The passage then delves into a scholarly disagreement about vocal singing (taghanni) alone: some scholars (like Shaykh al-Islam) hold it absolutely haram because the general prohibition applies to all sound; others permit it for beneficial purposes like understanding poetry; Shams al-A'immah al-Sarakhsi permits it purely to dispel loneliness as a weak tradition from some Companions. However, if the lyrics contain a moral lesson or story, or mention a deceased woman, it is not disliked; but if it mentions a living woman, it is disliked (because it may lead to fitna). Then the text discusses games: chess, backgammon, and four other games are makruh (disliked) because they distract from prayer and congregation and lead to foul speech. The hadith 'Every play is haram except playing with wife, bow, and horse' is cited. Listening to the sound of amusement is haram (like reed pipe), and the hadith classifies listening as sin, sitting as transgression, and enjoyment as disbelief—but that is for emphasis, not actual kufr. Finally, special cases: al-Hasan ibn Ziyad allows beating a drum (daff) at a wedding to publicly announce the marriage. Abu Yusuf permits women to beat a drum for a boy if done without transgression. However, a Muslim woman should not ride a saddle (like a man) because of a hadith cursing women riding saddles, especially if for display or entertainment.

Key Terms
ملاهي (malahi): All forms of amusement and entertainment, particularly musical instruments and idle play.
معازف (ma'azif): Musical instruments

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on listening to the sound of amusements (e.g., reed pipe)?
A) Makruh tanzihi
B) Haram, and it is a sin
C) Mubah
D) Mustahab

Key Takeaway
Musical instruments are haram in the Hanafi school based on the explicit hadith; listening, sitting, and enjoyment have escalating degrees of sin.

Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf on Vocal Singing (Taghanni Mujarrad)
Intra-Hanafi dispute on vocal singing ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the three Hanafi positions on vocal singing without instruments, and identify which opinion is preferred by later scholars.

Core Idea
Vocal singing (taghanni) without instruments has three views: absolute haram, permissible for benefit, and permissible to dispel loneliness.

This lesson explores the scholarly disagreement (ikhtilaf) within the Hanafi school regarding vocal singing (al-taghanni al-mujarrad) that does not involve musical instruments. The text presents three distinct positions: absolute prohibition (Shaykh al-Islam), permissibility for beneficial purposes, and permissibility to dispel loneliness (Imam al-Sarakhsi). It also provides conditions that affect the ruling, such as the content of the lyrics.

Guided Path
1
Understand the subject of disagreement
The disagreement is about vocal singing (taghanni mujarrad) without any musical accompaniment. All agree that singing with instruments is haram; this lesson focuses on the voice alone.
2
Learn the first position: absolute prohibition
Some scholars, including Shaykh al-Islam (likely al-Halwani or al-Sarakhsi's teacher), hold that vocal singing is absolutely haram because the general prohibition in the hadith includes all sounds of amusement.
This is the stricter view.
3
Learn the second position: permissibility for benefit
Another group permits singing that leads to understanding meanings (e.g., poetry) and eloquence. This view sees benefit as a valid basis for permissibility.
The text does not name the scholars here.
4
Learn the third position: permissibility to dispel loneliness
Imam al-Sarakhsi (Shams al-A'immah) allows singing when alone to remove feelings of loneliness, provided it is not for entertainment. He relies on a report from some Companions.
This is the view chosen by the author of the passage? The text says 'dhahaba ilayhi' indicating he adopted it.
5
Examine the conditions affecting the ruling
If the lyrics contain a moral lesson or story, it is not disliked; if they mention a deceased woman, it is not disliked; but if they mention a living woman, it is disliked due to potential fitna.
This shows the ruling is not absolute; context matters.

Rulings on Chess, Backgammon, Drum at Weddings, and Women's Saddle
Detailed rulings on chess, drum at weddings, and women's saddle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the rulings on chess/backgammon (makruh), drum at weddings (permissible), women beating drum for children (not disliked), and women riding saddle (disliked), and cite the evidence for each.

Core Idea
Chess and backgammon are makruh; drum at weddings is allowed; women beating drum for children is permitted; women riding saddle is disliked.

This lesson addresses several specific amusements and actions that have distinct rulings in the Hanafi school. The text covers the game of chess and backgammon, the use of the drum (duff) at weddings, women beating the drum for children, and the prohibition of women riding on a saddle. Each ruling is supported by hadith or scholarly opinion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on chess and backgammon
The text explicitly says 'playing chess, backgammon, and the four [other games] is disliked (makruh).' The evidence is the hadith 'Every play is haram except...'
Makruh is the dominant ruling; some later scholars may consider it haram if it leads to neglect of prayers.
2
Understand the exception for drum at weddings
Al-Hasan ibn Ziyad states there is no harm in beating a drum at a wedding to announce the marriage. This is an exception to the general prohibition.
This is specifically for the wedding context.
3
Learn the ruling on women beating drum for children
Abu Yusuf was asked about a woman beating a drum for a child without transgression; he said he does not dislike it. This permits a limited context.
The condition 'without transgression' means not leading to sin.
4
Analyze the ruling on women riding a saddle
A Muslim woman should not ride on a saddle (i.e., a man's saddle) because of the hadith cursing women who ride saddles. This applies when done for entertainment or display.
The prohibition is for adornment or display, not necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودلت المسألة على أن الملاهي كلها حرام حتى التغني بضرب القصب قال عليه الصلاة والسلام ليكونن من أمتي أقوام يستحلون الحر والحرير والخمر والمعازف» أخرجه البخاري. وفي لفظ آخر «ليشربن اناس من أمتي الخمر يسمونها بغير اسمها يعزف على رؤوسهم بالمعازف والمغنيات يخسف الله الأرض ويجعل منهم القرد والخنازير) (۲) واختلفوا في التغني المجرد قال بعضهم إنه حرام مطلقاً والاستماع إليه معصية لإطلاق الحديث وهو اختيار شيخ الإسلام، ومنهم من قال لا بأس به لیستفید به فهم المعاني والفصاحة، ومنهم من جوز التغني لدفع الوحشة إذا كان وحده ولا يكون على سبيل اللهو وإليه ذهب شمس الأئمة السرخسي لأنه روي بعض الصحابة. ولو كان في الشعر حكم أو قصة لا يكره. وكذا لو كان فيه ذكر امرأة غير معينة، وكذا لو كانت معينة وهي ميتة ولو كانت حية يكره؛ كذا في الشارح. وفي المحيط : ويكره اللعب بالشطرنج والنرد والأربعة لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام كل لعب حرام إلا ملاعبة الرجل زوجته وقوسه وفرسه لأنه يصد عن الجمع والجماعات وسبب للوقوع في فواحش الكلام وغيره واستماع صوت الملاهي حرام كالضرب بالقصب وغيره قال عليه الصلاة والسلام استماع الملاهي معصية والجلوس عليها فسق والتلذذ بها كفر» وهذا خرج على وجه التشديد لا أنه يكفر. وعن الحسن بن زياد لا بأس بأن يكون في العرس دف يضرب به ليشتهر ويعلن النكاح وسئل أبو يوسف أيكره للمرأة أن تضرب في غير فسق للصبي؟ قال : لا أكره ولا تركب امرأة مسلمة على السرج لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام «لعن الله السروج على «الفروج هذا إذا ركبت متلهية أم متزينة لتعرض نفسها على الرجال.
And the issue indicated that all amusements are haram, even singing with the beating of a reed [pipe]. He, peace be upon him, said: 'There will be from my nation people who consider lawful the private parts, silk, wine, and musical instruments.' Narrated by Bukhari. In another wording: 'People from my nation will drink wine, calling it by other names; music will be played over their heads with instruments and female singers; Allah will cause the earth to swallow them and make them into apes and pigs.' They disagreed about mere singing: some said it is absolutely haram and listening to it is sin due to the generality of the hadith, and this is the choice of Shaykh al-Islam; some said there is no harm in it to benefit from understanding meanings and eloquence; some allowed singing to dispel loneliness when alone and not for the purpose of entertainment, and this is the view of Shams al-A'immah al-Sarakhsi because it is reported from some companions. If the poetry contains a moral lesson or story, it is not disliked. Similarly, if it contains mention of an unspecified woman, and also if it is specified and she is dead; but if she is alive, it is disliked. Likewise in the commentator. And in al-Muhit: Playing chess, backgammon, and the four [other games] is disliked due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'Every play is haram except a man's playing with his wife, his bow, and his horse' because it distracts from congregations and causes foul language and other things. And listening to the sound of amusements is haram, like beating reed etc. He, peace be upon him, said: 'Listening to amusements is sin, sitting with them is transgression, and enjoying them is disbelief.' This is said with exaggeration, not that he becomes a disbeliever. From al-Hasan ibn Ziyad: There is no harm in having a drum at the wedding that is beaten to publicize and announce the marriage. And Abu Yusuf was asked: Is it disliked for a woman to beat [drum] for a boy without transgression? He said: I do not dislike it. And a Muslim woman should not ride on a saddle because of his saying, peace be upon him: 'Allah has cursed saddles upon female private parts.' This is if she rides for entertainment or adornment to display herself to men.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage presents a comprehensive ruling on the prohibition of various forms of amusement (malahi). It begins by stating that the previous discussion on the wedding feast demonstrates that all amusements are haram, including singing with reed instruments. It cites two hadith: the first lists what some will consider lawful (private parts, silk, wine, musical instruments); the second warns of punishment for those who drink wine under other names and enjoy music. The passage then delves into a scholarly disagreement about vocal singing (taghanni) alone: some scholars (like Shaykh al-Islam) hold it absolutely haram because the general prohibition applies to all sound; others permit it for beneficial purposes like understanding poetry; Shams al-A'immah al-Sarakhsi permits it purely to dispel loneliness as a weak tradition from some Companions. However, if the lyrics contain a moral lesson or story, or mention a deceased woman, it is not disliked; but if it mentions a living woman, it is disliked (because it may lead to fitna). Then the text discusses games: chess, backgammon, and four other games are makruh (disliked) because they distract from prayer and congregation and lead to foul speech. The hadith 'Every play is haram except playing with wife, bow, and horse' is cited. Listening to the sound of amusement is haram (like reed pipe), and the hadith classifies listening as sin, sitting as transgression, and enjoyment as disbelief—but that is for emphasis, not actual kufr. Finally, special cases: al-Hasan ibn Ziyad allows beating a drum (daff) at a wedding to publicly announce the marriage. Abu Yusuf permits women to beat a drum for a boy if done without transgression. However, a Muslim woman should not ride a saddle (like a man) because of a hadith cursing women riding saddles, especially if for display or entertainment.

Key Terms
شطرنج (shatranj): Chess (the game).
دف (duff): A frame drum, used in weddings to announce the marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a woman beating a drum for a child without transgression?
A) Haram
B) Makruh
C) Not disliked (in the words of Abu Yusuf)
D) Mustahab

Key Takeaway
Chess is makruh, drum at weddings is allowed, women beating drum for children is not disliked, and women riding saddle is disliked; each has specific evidence.

Beat the Drum: Permissibility of Drum at Weddings
Ruling on drum at weddings and related musical practices.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on using a drum at weddings and explain the conditions for permissibility.

Core Idea
Drum at weddings is permissible to announce marriage, but only when free from sin.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on drumming at weddings, a practice tied to publicizing the marriage. The source passage cites al-Hasan ibn Ziyad and later works affirming permissibility at weddings, walimahs, and Eids, as long as no immorality is involved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage states that there is no harm (la ba'sa) in having a drum at a wedding. This indicates permissibility (mubah).
The reason given: to make the marriage known and public.
2
Note the scope of permissibility
The ruling extends to weddings, walimahs (marriage feasts), and Eids. Singing is also permitted on these occasions, provided there is no fisq (immorality).
The condition of 'no immorality' is crucial; otherwise, the act becomes prohibited.
3
Compare with other forms of play
The passage later mentions that all play except chess is haram (a minority view). The drum at weddings is an exception because it serves a legitimate purpose.
This helps distinguish between permissible custom and prohibited amusement.
4
Determine the operative ruling (mufta bihi)
The later Hanafi works (al-Dhakhira, etc.) affirm the permissibility, and this is the relied-upon position for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن الحسن بن زياد لا بأس بأن يكون في العرس دف يضرب به ليشتهر ويعلن النكاح وسئل أبو يوسف أيكره للمرأة أن تضرب في غير فسق للصبي؟ قال : لا أكره ولا تركب امرأة مسلمة على السرج لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام «لعن الله السروج على الفروج» هذا إذا ركبت متلهية أم متزينة لتعرض نفسها على الرجال، فإن ركبت لحاجة كالجهاد والحج فلا بأس به. رجل أظهر الفسق في داره فللإمام أن يتقدم عليه فإن لم يمتنع فالإمام بالخيار إن شاء ضربه أسواطاً وإن شاء أخرجه من داره لأن الكل يصلح للتعزير . قال أبو يوسف في داره يسمع مزامير ومعازف أدخل عليهم بغير إذنهم أمنع الناس عن إقامة هذا الفرض، ولو رأى منكراً وهو ممن يرتكب هذا المنكر له أن عنه لأن الواجب عليه ترك المنكر والنهي عن المنكر فإذا ترك أحدهما لا يترك الآخر . وفي الذخيرة وغيرها: لا بأس بضرب الدف في العرس والوليمة والأعياد. وكذا لا بأس بالغناء في العرس والوليمة والأعياد حيث لا فسق. وفي الخلاصة: وعن عمر أنه أحرق بيت الخمار. وعن الإمام الزاهد الصفار أنه أمر بتخريب دار الفسق بسبب الفسق. وفي الظهيرية : لا بأس بالمزاح بعد أن لا يتكلم بكلام فيه مأثم ويقصد به إضحاك جلسائه. وفي الجامع الصغير للعتابي : وكل لعب غير الشطرنج فهو حرام. وفي الحاوي سئل عمن رأى رجلاً سرق مال إنسان قال : إن كان لا يخاف الظلم منه يخبر به وإن كان يخاف ترك. وفي الظهيرية : الأمر بالمعروف باليد على الآمر أو باللسان على العلماء وبالقلب على عوام الناس وهو اختيار الزندويسي. وفي الخانية: رجل دعاه الأمير فسأله عن أشياء إن تكلم بما يوافق الحق لا يرضيه فإنه لا ينبغي له أن يتكلم بما يخالف الحق، وهذا إذا كان لا يخاف القتل على نفسه ولا إتلاف عضوه ولا يخاف على ماله وإذا خاف ذلك منه فإنه لا بأس به . والله أعلم .
And from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad: There is no harm in having a drum at the wedding that is beaten to publicize and announce the marriage. Abu Yusuf was asked: Do you dislike for a woman to beat for a boy without sin? He said: I do not dislike. And a Muslim woman should not ride on a saddle because of his saying, peace be upon him: 'Allah curses saddles upon female private parts.' This is when she rides in amusement or adorned to display herself to men. But if she rides for a need like jihad or hajj, there is no harm. A man who openly commits sin in his house, the ruler may proceed against him. If he does not desist, the ruler has the option to scourge him or expel him from his house because both are suitable for ta'zir. Abu Yusuf said: If in his house he hears flutes and musical instruments, I would enter upon them without their permission to prevent people from establishing this sin. And if he sees an evil while he himself is committing this evil, he may speak against it because the obligation is to leave the evil and forbid it; if he leaves one, he does not leave the other. In al-Dhakhira and others: There is no harm in beating the drum at weddings, walimah, and Eids. Likewise there is no harm in singing at weddings, walimah, and Eids where there is no immorality. In al-Khulasa: Umar burned the house of the wine seller. Imam al-Zahid al-Saffar ordered the destruction of a house of sin because of sin. In al-Zahiriya: There is no harm in joking as long as he does not speak sinful words and intends to make his companions laugh. In al-Jami' al-Saghir of al-Utabi: Every play other than chess is forbidden. In al-Hawi: Someone was asked about a person who saw a man stealing another's money: If he does not fear oppression from him, he should report; if he fears, he should leave. In al-Zahiriya: Enjoining good: by the hand is for the ruler, by the tongue for scholars, and by the heart for the common people; this is the choice of al-Zandawisi. In al-Khaniya: A man invited by the ruler asks about things; if he speaks the truth and it displeases him, he should not speak against the truth, provided he does not fear death, loss of limb, or property. If he does fear, then it is okay. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage collects numerous rulings from various Hanafi works. 1. Drum at weddings is permissible to announce marriage. 2. Women should not ride on a saddle (with legs astride) due to the hadith cursing it, unless for necessity like jihad or hajj. 3. An open sinner can be punished by the ruler via ta'zir. 4. Abu Yusuf permits entering a house without permission if music is heard, to stop the evil. 5. One who commits sin can still enjoin good on others. 6. Drum and singing at weddings and Eids are allowed if no immorality. 7. Umar burned a wine seller's house as a precedent. 8. Joking is allowed if not sinful. 9. All play except chess is haram (a specific opinion). 10. Reporting a thief depends on fear of oppression. 11. Enjoining good is by hand for rulers, tongue for scholars, heart for commoners. 12. Lying to a ruler is allowed only if fearing severe harm.

Key Terms
دف (daff): A frame drum, traditionally used in weddings and celebrations.
فسق (fisq): Immorality, sinful behavior that contradicts Shari'ah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on beating the drum at a wedding?
A) Haram
B) Makruh
C) Mubah
D) Wajib

Key Takeaway
Drum at weddings is permissible to announce marriage, provided no immorality.

Women Riding Saddles: Prohibition and Exceptions
Prohibition of women riding on saddles and exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on women riding on a saddle and identify the exceptions for necessity.

Core Idea
Women are forbidden to ride on a saddle (astride) due to the hadith, except for necessity like jihad or hajj.

This lesson addresses a specific prohibition: a Muslim woman should not ride on a saddle (i.e., in a manner that involves straddling and display). The evidence is a prophetic hadith. However, necessity permits it. We will explore the reasons and the scope of the exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage states: 'A Muslim woman should not ride on a saddle.' This indicates prohibition (haram or makruh tahriman).
The hadith cited curses saddles upon female private parts, implying exposing shape is forbidden.
2
Understand the reason for prohibition
The prohibition is tied to adornment and displaying oneself to men. If the woman rides in amusement or to show off, it is forbidden.
The illa (effective cause) is the potential for temptation and public display of her figure.
3
Note the exceptions
If she rides for a legitimate need like jihad or hajj, there is no harm (la ba'sa).
Necessity overrides the general prohibition.
4
Apply to modern contexts
Modern means of transport (cars, bicycles) would be analogized based on the same illa: if they involve similar display or temptation, they are disliked; if needed, permissible.

Enjoining Good and Dealing with Sin: Levels and Limits
Collection of rulings on ta'zir, entering houses, forbidding evil while sinful, levels of amr bil-ma'ruf, reporting theft, and lying to a ruler.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the levels of enjoining good (by hand, tongue, heart) and apply the rulings on entering houses with sin, reporting theft, and lying to a ruler in fear.

Core Idea
Enjoining good varies by capacity; one can forbid evil even while sinning, and necessity can override general rules.

This lesson consolidates several practical rulings on commanding good and forbidding evil. It covers ta'zir for open sin, entering a house where music is played, one’s own state when forbidding, levels of obligation, reporting theft, and lying to a ruler out of fear. Each provides a nuanced application of the principle.

Guided Path
1
Understand ta'zir for open sinners
A person who openly commits sin in his house may be punished by the ruler with lashes or expulsion, as ta'zir.
Ta'zir is discretionary; the ruler chooses based on what repels the evil.
2
Entering houses where sin occurs
Abu Yusuf states that if one hears flutes/music in a house, he may enter without permission to stop the sin.
This is an exception to the rule of not entering without permission, due to the greater evil.
3
Forbidding evil while oneself is sinful
One who commits the same sin can still forbid others from it; the obligation to leave sin and forbid sin are separate.
A person is not required to be sinless to advise.
4
Levels of commanding good
According to al-Zandawisi: by hand (for rulers), by tongue (for scholars), by heart (for common people).
This is based on the hadith 'Whoever sees an evil, let him change it by his hand...'
5
Reporting theft and lying to ruler
If one sees theft, he should report unless he fears oppression. Lying to the ruler is permissible only if one fears severe harm (death, loss of limb or property).
These are applications of the maxim 'necessity permits the forbidden'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن الحسن بن زياد لا بأس بأن يكون في العرس دف يضرب به ليشتهر ويعلن النكاح وسئل أبو يوسف أيكره للمرأة أن تضرب في غير فسق للصبي؟ قال : لا أكره ولا تركب امرأة مسلمة على السرج لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام «لعن الله السروج على الفروج» هذا إذا ركبت متلهية أم متزينة لتعرض نفسها على الرجال، فإن ركبت لحاجة كالجهاد والحج فلا بأس به. رجل أظهر الفسق في داره فللإمام أن يتقدم عليه فإن لم يمتنع فالإمام بالخيار إن شاء ضربه أسواطاً وإن شاء أخرجه من داره لأن الكل يصلح للتعزير . قال أبو يوسف في داره يسمع مزامير ومعازف أدخل عليهم بغير إذنهم أمنع الناس عن إقامة هذا الفرض، ولو رأى منكراً وهو ممن يرتكب هذا المنكر له أن عنه لأن الواجب عليه ترك المنكر والنهي عن المنكر فإذا ترك أحدهما لا يترك الآخر . وفي الذخيرة وغيرها: لا بأس بضرب الدف في العرس والوليمة والأعياد. وكذا لا بأس بالغناء في العرس والوليمة والأعياد حيث لا فسق. وفي الخلاصة: وعن عمر أنه أحرق بيت الخمار. وعن الإمام الزاهد الصفار أنه أمر بتخريب دار الفسق بسبب الفسق. وفي الظهيرية : لا بأس بالمزاح بعد أن لا يتكلم بكلام فيه مأثم ويقصد به إضحاك جلسائه. وفي الجامع الصغير للعتابي : وكل لعب غير الشطرنج فهو حرام. وفي الحاوي سئل عمن رأى رجلاً سرق مال إنسان قال : إن كان لا يخاف الظلم منه يخبر به وإن كان يخاف ترك. وفي الظهيرية : الأمر بالمعروف باليد على الآمر أو باللسان على العلماء وبالقلب على عوام الناس وهو اختيار الزندويسي. وفي الخانية: رجل دعاه الأمير فسأله عن أشياء إن تكلم بما يوافق الحق لا يرضيه فإنه لا ينبغي له أن يتكلم بما يخالف الحق، وهذا إذا كان لا يخاف القتل على نفسه ولا إتلاف عضوه ولا يخاف على ماله وإذا خاف ذلك منه فإنه لا بأس به . والله أعلم .
And from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad: There is no harm in having a drum at the wedding that is beaten to publicize and announce the marriage. Abu Yusuf was asked: Do you dislike for a woman to beat for a boy without sin? He said: I do not dislike. And a Muslim woman should not ride on a saddle because of his saying, peace be upon him: 'Allah curses saddles upon female private parts.' This is when she rides in amusement or adorned to display herself to men. But if she rides for a need like jihad or hajj, there is no harm. A man who openly commits sin in his house, the ruler may proceed against him. If he does not desist, the ruler has the option to scourge him or expel him from his house because both are suitable for ta'zir. Abu Yusuf said: If in his house he hears flutes and musical instruments, I would enter upon them without their permission to prevent people from establishing this sin. And if he sees an evil while he himself is committing this evil, he may speak against it because the obligation is to leave the evil and forbid it; if he leaves one, he does not leave the other. In al-Dhakhira and others: There is no harm in beating the drum at weddings, walimah, and Eids. Likewise there is no harm in singing at weddings, walimah, and Eids where there is no immorality. In al-Khulasa: Umar burned the house of the wine seller. Imam al-Zahid al-Saffar ordered the destruction of a house of sin because of sin. In al-Zahiriya: There is no harm in joking as long as he does not speak sinful words and intends to make his companions laugh. In al-Jami' al-Saghir of al-Utabi: Every play other than chess is forbidden. In al-Hawi: Someone was asked about a person who saw a man stealing another's money: If he does not fear oppression from him, he should report; if he fears, he should leave. In al-Zahiriya: Enjoining good: by the hand is for the ruler, by the tongue for scholars, and by the heart for the common people; this is the choice of al-Zandawisi. In al-Khaniya: A man invited by the ruler asks about things; if he speaks the truth and it displeases him, he should not speak against the truth, provided he does not fear death, loss of limb, or property. If he does fear, then it is okay. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage collects numerous rulings from various Hanafi works. 1. Drum at weddings is permissible to announce marriage. 2. Women should not ride on a saddle (with legs astride) due to the hadith cursing it, unless for necessity like jihad or hajj. 3. An open sinner can be punished by the ruler via ta'zir. 4. Abu Yusuf permits entering a house without permission if music is heard, to stop the evil. 5. One who commits sin can still enjoin good on others. 6. Drum and singing at weddings and Eids are allowed if no immorality. 7. Umar burned a wine seller's house as a precedent. 8. Joking is allowed if not sinful. 9. All play except chess is haram (a specific opinion). 10. Reporting a thief depends on fear of oppression. 11. Enjoining good is by hand for rulers, tongue for scholars, heart for commoners. 12. Lying to a ruler is allowed only if fearing severe harm.

Key Terms
التعزير (al-ta'zir): Discretionary punishment for sins that have no fixed hadd penalty.
الأمر بالمعروف (al-amr bil-ma'ruf): Enjoining what is good and forbidding evil.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, what is the ruling if one hears musical instruments in a house?
A) He must not enter without permission.
B) He may enter without permission to stop the sin.
C) He should report to the ruler.
D) He should ignore it.

Key Takeaway
Enjoining good varies by ability; one can forbid evil even while sinning, and necessity permits overriding normal rules.

Ruling on Fastening a Loose Tooth with Silver or Gold
Stating the permissibility of fastening a tooth with silver and the prohibition of using gold, with an intra-Hanafi difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on fastening a loose tooth with silver and with gold, and identify the intra-Hanafi difference on the use of gold.

Core Idea
Silver is permissible for tooth fastening; gold is prohibited according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

This lesson addresses a practical question: when a tooth becomes loose, it may be stabilized with a metal band or wire. The Hanafi school distinguishes between silver and gold. While Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani allows gold, the majority view (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) prohibits it. Understanding this ruling requires knowing the general prohibition of gold for men and the principle of medical necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? Fastening a loose tooth with a metal material. State the ruling for silver: halal (permissible).
The text explicitly says 'it is permissible to fasten the tooth with silver'.
2
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf for gold
The text states that according to Muhammad al-Shaybani, gold is also permissible, but the dominant view (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) holds it is not permissible.
This is a legitimate difference within the school; the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that gold is not allowed.
3
Recall the underlying principle
The general prohibition of gold for men (wearing or using) applies here. Gold is considered extravagant and prohibited for males except in specific circumstances like necessity.
The text references an earlier explanation that likely detailed this principle.
4
Recognize the practical application
For a person needing a dental fastening, silver is the recommended material. If only gold is available, some later scholars may permit it under necessity, but the classical ruling is to avoid gold.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وقد تقدم بيانه قال رحمه الله : وشد السن بالفضة) يعني يحل شد السن المتحرك بالفضة ولا يحل بالذهب، وقال محمد يحل بالذهب أيضاً وقدمنا بيان ذلك . قال رحمه الله : (وكره إلباس ذهب وحرير صبياً لأن التحريم لما ثبت في حق الذكور حرم اللبس حرم الإلباس كالخمر لما حرم شربها حرم سقيها للصبي.
He (the author) said: (And its explanation has preceded; he said: And fastening the tooth with silver) meaning it is permissible to fasten a loose tooth with silver but not with gold. Muhammad said it is permissible with gold as well, and we have presented its explanation earlier. He (the author) said: (And it is disliked to clothe a child in gold and silk because when prohibition is established for males, wearing is prohibited, and so is clothing, like wine: when drinking it is prohibited, it is prohibited to give it to a child.)
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage contains two distinct rulings. First, the ruling on fastening a loose tooth: it is permissible (halal) with silver, but not with gold. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, however, holds that it is also permissible with gold. The commentator notes that the explanation was given earlier. Second, the ruling on clothing male children in gold and silk: it is makruh (here meaning haram, as the reasoning uses 'tahrim'). The proof is a qiyas (analogy) with wine: just as it is haram to give wine to a child because its consumption is haram, similarly, since wearing gold and silk is haram for adult males, it is also haram to dress a child in them. The child is not legally responsible, but the parent or guardian is sinning by facilitating the child's participation in a prohibited act. The preferred Hanafi position is that silver is permissible for teeth, while gold is not, contrary to Muhammad's opinion. For children, the prohibition is clear: male children may not be dressed in gold or silk, as this is considered assisting in sin.

Key Terms
شد السن (shadd al-sinn): Fastening or stabilizing a loose tooth (e.g., with a wire or band).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on using gold to fasten a loose tooth?
A) Permissible in all cases.
B) Permissible only if silver is not available.
C) Not permissible; only silver is allowed.
D) Makruh but not haram.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits fastening a loose tooth with silver but prohibits gold, although Imam Muhammad allows gold.

Ruling on Dressing Male Children in Gold and Silk
Stating the prohibition of dressing male children in gold and silk with a qiyas proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on dressing male children in gold or silk, and explain the qiyas proof using the prohibition of giving wine to a child.

Core Idea
Dressing a male child in gold or silk is haram for the parent/guardian, by qiyas on giving wine to a child.

Even though children are not legally responsible (mukallaf), parents are accountable for facilitating prohibited acts. The Hanafi school explicitly analogizes dressing a boy in gold or silk to giving wine to a child. Both are haram for the adult because the underlying act is haram for adult males. This lesson unpacks this qiyas and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The text states it is makruh (here meaning haram) to clothe a child in gold or silk. The reasoning uses the word 'tahrim' (prohibition).
The term 'kariha' is often used by Hanafi jurists to mean haram when accompanied by a proof of prohibition.
2
Locate the proof: qiyas
The author provides an analogy: just as giving wine to a child is prohibited because drinking wine is prohibited, so too dressing a child in gold/silk is prohibited because wearing them is prohibited for men.
The exact wording: 'كالخمر لما حرم شربها حرم سقيها للصبي'
3
Extract the 'illa (effective cause)
The 'illa is the prohibition of the act itself for adults. Since wearing gold/silk is haram for men, facilitating that act for a child is also haram.
This is a clear qiyas jaliy (obvious analogy).
4
Apply the ruling
A parent or guardian who dresses a male child in gold or silk commits a sin, even though the child is not sinning.
The ruling applies to the one who dresses the child, not the child.

Source Text

Looking at a Prospective Spouse and Touching Non-Mahram
Rulings on viewing a fiancée and touching non-mahram

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rulings on looking at a fiancée (permissible) and touching her face/hands (haram) and identify the evidence for each.

Core Idea
Looking at a fiancée is permissible to encourage marriage; touching her is absolutely prohibited, even without desire.

Before marriage, a man may wonder about the boundaries with his prospective spouse. The Hanafi school, drawing on prophetic guidance, distinguishes between looking and touching: the former is permissible and encouraged for the sake of marital harmony, while the latter is strictly forbidden. This lesson explores both rulings and their proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
First: looking at a woman you intend to marry is permissible. Second: touching her face/hands is not permissible, even if safe from desire.
2
Locate the evidence for looking
The Prophet said, 'Look at her, for it is more likely to create lasting affection.' The imperative indicates permission, and the reason shows it is recommended (mustahabb).
3
Locate the evidence for touching
The Prophet said, 'Whoever touches a woman's hand without right, a coal from Hell will be placed on his hand.' This severe warning makes the act haram.
4
Understand the rationale
Looking is permitted due to the benefit of fostering marital harmony. Touching is prohibited because the prohibition is general and there is no necessity. Even if desire is absent, the prohibition stands.
Necessity (e.g., medical treatment) can permit touching but is not the context here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا أراد أن يتزوج امرأة فلا بأس أن ينظر إليها وإن خاف أن يشتهي لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام «انظر إليها لأنه أحرى أن يدوم بينكما ولا يجوز له أن يمس وجهها ولا كفها وإن أمن الشهوة لوجود المحرم ولانعدام الضرورة وقال عليه الصلاة والسلام من مس كف امرأة ليس له فيها سبيل وضع على كفه جمر يوم القيامة»
And when one wants to marry a woman, there is no harm in looking at her even if he fears desire, because of his statement (peace be upon him): 'Look at her, for it is more likely to create lasting affection between you.' And it is not permissible for him to touch her face or hands, even if safe from desire, due to the existence of the prohibition and the absence of necessity. And he (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever touches a woman's hand without right, a coal from Hell will be placed on his hand on the Day of Resurrection.'
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage gives two interconnected rulings. First, it is permissible for a man to look at a woman he intends to marry, even if he fears desire, based on the Prophet's hadith encouraging such looking to promote marital harmony. Second, it is absolutely prohibited to touch her face or hands, even without desire, because there is no necessity (like medical need) and the action is inherently prohibited. A hadith warns of punishment for touching a non-mahram woman's hand. The Hanafi position is clear: touching a non-mahram is haram regardless of intent.

Key Terms
خِطبة (khitbah): The proposal of marriage; the period before marriage contract.
لمس (lams): Touching with the hand; in this context, touching a non-mahram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) One must perform wudu again
B) A coal from Hell will be placed on his hand on Judgment Day
C) He must give charity
D) The marriage proposal is invalid

Key Takeaway
Looking at a prospective spouse is permissible to encourage marriage, but touching her face/hands is absolutely prohibited, with a severe warning in hadith.

Handshaking with Non-Mahram Old Women: Two Hanafi Narrations
Intra-Hanafi dispute on conditions for handshaking with old women.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi narrations on handshaking with old women and apply the preferred view to a scenario.

Core Idea
Handshaking with an old woman is permissible only if both parties are old and secure from desire.

The general ruling in the Hanafi school is that touching a non-mahram woman is haram. However, an exception may be made for old women past the age of desire. This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq discusses a dispute within the school: is it sufficient that one party is old and secure, or must both be? The author prefers the stricter view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
Recognize that touching a non-mahram is generally haram due to fitnah.
2
State the exception for old women
Understand that an old woman (ajuz) who is not desired may be touched if conditions are met.
3
Present the two narrations
Narration 1: both must be old and secure. Narration 2: it suffices if one party is old and secure.
The second narration compares to a child.
4
Analyze the reasoning for each
Narration 1 argues that even if the man is secure, the old woman may desire him. Narration 2 argues that if one party has no desire, touching does not cause fitnah.
Key difference: the old woman's experience of pleasure.
5
Identify the tarjih
The author prefers the first narration because it prevents fitnah from both sides.
Mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيدوا جواز النظر دون المس عند إرادة الزوج إذا كانت شابة تشتهى، وأما إذا كانت عجوزاً لا تشتهي فلا بأس بمصافحتها بدنها لانعدام خوف الفتنة. وعن أبي بكر رضي الله عنه أنه كان يصافح العجائز فإذا كان شيخاً يأمن على نفسه وعليها يحل له المصافحة، وإن يكونا لا يأمن عليها ولا على نفسه لا تحل له مصافحتها لما فيه من التعريض للفتنة. فحاصله أنه يشترط الجواز المس أن يكونا كبيرين مأمونين في رواية، وفي أخرى يكفي أن يكون أحدهما مأموناً كبيراً لأن أحدهما إذا كان لا يشتهي لا يكون اللمس سبباً للوقوع في الفتنة كالصغير. ووجه الأولى أن الشاب إذا كان لا يشتهي بمس العجوز فالعجوز تشتهي الشاب لأنها علمت بملاذ الجماع فيؤدي إلى الانتهاء من الجانبين وهو حرام بخلاف ما إذا كان أحدهما صغيراً لأنه لا يؤدي إلى الانتهاء من الجانبين لأن الكبير لايشتهي بمس الصغير، ولهذا إذا مات صغير أو صغيرة تغسله المرأة والرجل ما لم تبلغ حد الشهوة وكذا يجوز النظر إلى الصغير والصغيرة والمس إذا كان لا يشتهي.
And they restricted the permissibility of looking without touching when intending to marry a young woman who is desired. But if she is an old woman who is not desired, there is no harm in shaking her hand or body due to the absence of fear of fitnah. It is reported from Abu Bakr (may Allah be pleased with him) that he used to shake hands with old women. So if a man is old and secure from himself and her, then handshaking is permissible. If both are not secure, then it is not permissible due to exposure to fitnah. The conclusion is that for permissibility of touching, in one narration both must be old and secure, and in another it suffices if one of them is old and secure, because if one does not desire, touching does not cause fall into fitnah, like with a child. The first view is argued that if a young man does not desire when touching an old woman, the old woman may desire the young man because she knows the pleasures of intercourse, leading to fitnah from both sides, which is haram, unlike the case where one is a child.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding handshaking with non-mahram women. The general rule is that touching a non-mahram is haram, but exceptions are made for old women where desire is unlikely. The text reports two views: 1) Both parties must be old and secure from desire (from Abu Bakr's practice). 2) It suffices if one party is old and secure, like a child (since children don't arouse desire). The first view is stronger because even if the young man doesn't desire, the old woman might desire him due to her experience, leading to mutual fitnah. The example of washing a deceased child is given to show that touching a child's body is permissible since it doesn't arouse desire. The point is that the ruling on touching depends on the potential for fitnah, not just the intent of one party.

Key Terms
فتنة (fitnah): Moral trial or temptation, especially sexual desire leading to sin.
عجوز (ajuz): An old woman past the age of sexual desire.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in this passage, when is handshaking with a non-mahram woman permissible?
A) Only if both parties are old and secure from desire
B) Always permissible if the man is old
C) Only if the woman is old
D) Permissible in all cases as long as there is no desire

Key Takeaway
Handshaking with a non-mahram old woman is permissible only when both parties are old and secure from desire, preventing fitnah from either side.

Ruling on Looking at Spouse's and Concubine's Private Parts
Ruling with proof on looking at spouse's and concubine's private parts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a man looking at his wife's or concubine's private parts, cite the primary proof, and explain the recommended practice.

Core Idea
Looking is permissible (mubah), but covering is better to avoid forgetfulness.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a common question: may a husband look at his wife's private parts? The author presents contradictory proofs and reconciles them by distinguishing between permissibility and preference. Understanding this distinction is key to applying the ruling correctly.

Guided Path
1
State the basic ruling
The matn says a man may look at his wife's and slave-girl's private parts. This is the permissibility (ibaha). The author adds 'whether with desire or without' to emphasize permissibility is unrestricted.
2
Identify the primary proof
Find the hadith quoted: the Prophet (saw) said 'Lower your gaze except from your wife and your slave-girl.' This is the direct dalil for permissibility.
This hadith is ahad, so its thubut is zanni, but sufficient for ibaha.
3
Understand the qiyas reasoning
Since touching and intercourse are permitted, looking is even more so (awla) because it precedes them. This strengthens the proof.
The author uses a fortiori analogy.
4
Recognize the recommendation to avoid
Another hadith advises covering and not being naked like animals. The author presents this as 'al-awla' (it is better) but not obligatory.
This is a case of two contradictory hadiths reconciled by preferring caution but not making it binding.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Some Hanafis follow Ibn Umar's view that looking enhances pleasure; others follow the hadith advising coverage. The mufta bihi is that it is permissible but better to avoid.
This illustrates nuance: permissibility does not equal recommendation.

Commentary
This passage deals with looking at the private parts of one's wife and lawful slave-girl. The base ruling from the matn is that it is permissible. Proofs include the hadith commanding to lower gaze except from one's wife and slave-girl, and Aisha's hadith showing that she and the Prophet bathed together. Since touching and intercourse are permitted, looking is even more so. However, there is a recommendation to avoid looking at each other's private parts based on another hadith that warns against nakedness like donkeys, saying it causes forgetfulness. But then Ibn Umar's statement suggests looking is better for pleasure. The passage also includes a story from Abu Yusuf where Imam Abu Hanifa said that touching the slave-girl's private parts to arouse desire carries hope for reward. The passage then notes that this applies only to a slave-girl with whom intercourse is lawful; if she is not lawful, the ruling differs.

أمة (ammah): A female slave with whom intercourse is lawful (i.e., a concubine).
غشيان (ghishyan): Sexual intercourse.
عورة (awrah): Private parts that must be covered in front of others; between spouses, the entire body is permissible to see, but some recommend covering.

Ruling on Looking at Private Parts of Wife and Female Slave
States the permissibility and recommends concealment for spouses/slaves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a man looking at the private parts of his wife and lawful female slave, and identify the conditions and recommended conduct.

Core Idea
Looking at your wife's private parts is permissible, but covering is better; looking at your lawful slave is also allowed.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a common question: is it permissible for a husband to look at his wife's private parts? The answer is nuanced – permissible in principle, but with a strong recommendation for modesty. The text also addresses female slaves and sets boundaries for unlawful women.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon and what the hukm (legal classification) is. Here, the act is a man looking at the private part of his wife or his lawful female slave, and the ruling is mubah (permissible).
The passage states this applies whether with desire or without.
2
Locate the evidence (dalil)
The author cites two proofs: the hadith 'Lower your gaze except from your wife and your female slave' and the practice of Aisha bathing with the Prophet. Explain how each supports the ruling.
The hadith is a man's command with an exception, and the practice demonstrates permissibility.
3
Understand the recommended conduct
Although looking is permissible, the author recommends covering, citing another hadith that advises covering during intercourse and warns that looking at private parts causes forgetfulness.
There is a difference of opinion: Ibn Umar held that looking is better for pleasure, but the author sides with the hadith recommending concealment.
4
Note the exceptions
The ruling is conditional on the woman being lawful for intercourse. If the female slave is a Magian, polytheist, foster relative, or in-law, looking at her private part is not allowed.
The passage also notes consensus against anal intercourse, except by the Zahiri school.

He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (And the man looks at the private part of his female slave and his wife, meaning whether with desire or without desire. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Lower your gaze except from your wife and your female slave.' And what is narrated from Aisha, she said: 'I used to take a bath with the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) from one vessel.' And because it is permissible for him to touch and have intercourse, so looking is more appropriate. However, it is better that each of them does not look at the private part of the other, due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'When one of you comes to his wife, let him cover himself as much as he can, and let him not be naked like the nakedness of a camel,' because looking at the private part causes forgetfulness. And Ibn Umar used to say: 'It is better to look at the private part of one's wife during intercourse to be more effective in attaining the meaning of pleasure.' And from Abu Yusuf: I asked the Imam about a man touching the private part of his female slave or she touching his private part to stimulate his organ; is there anything wrong with that? He said: 'I hope that the reward is great.' What is meant by female slave is the one whose intercourse is lawful. But if she is not lawful, like his Magian or polytheist female slave, or his foster sister, or his wife's mother, or his wife's daughter, then it is not lawful for him to look at her private part. And in al-Yanabi': It is not lawful for him to have intercourse with his wife in the anus, except for the Zahiris, and that is contrary to consensus.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage addresses the permissibility of a man looking at the private parts of his wife and his lawful female slave (umm walad or concubine). The author states that it is permissible regardless of desire, citing the hadith 'Lower your gaze except from your wife and your female slave' and the practice of Aisha bathing with the Prophet. The reasoning is that if touching and intercourse are allowed, looking is even more permissible. However, the author recommends that both spouses avoid looking at each other's private parts unnecessarily, based on another hadith that advises covering during intercourse, as looking at the private part is said to cause forgetfulness. Yet Ibn Umar held that looking during intercourse is better to enhance pleasure. The passage then records a question from Abu Yusuf to Abu Hanifa about touching the private parts to arouse, to which Abu Hanifa replied that he hopes for great reward, indicating permissibility. The author then clarifies that this ruling applies only to women whose intercourse is halal; if the female slave is a Magian, polytheist, foster relative, or in-law, looking at her private part is not allowed. Finally, the author notes from al-Yanabi' that anal intercourse with one's wife is unanimously prohibited, except by the Zahiri school which is contrary to consensus.

Key Terms
الأمة (al-ummah): Female slave who is lawful for intercourse (i.e., not a mahram and not a polytheist).
الغشيان (al-ghishyan): Sexual intercourse.

The Permissible and Prohibited Areas for a Man Looking at His Mahram
Specifies which body parts of mahram women a man may look at

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on which body parts of a mahram woman a man may look at, identify the Quranic proof, and explain the Hanafi interpretation of 'adornment'.

Core Idea
A man may look at a mahram’s face, head, chest, shins, and upper arms—but not her back, belly, or thigh.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on a man's gaze towards his mahram women. We will explore the primary proof from Surah al-Nur, the Hanafi interpretation of 'adornment' as places of adornment, and the practical reasoning based on custom and lack of desire. We will also touch on the definition of mahram and a scholarly difference regarding mahram through zina.

Guided Path
1
Identify the permitted and prohibited body parts
State the list from the matn: what is permissible (face, head, chest, shin, upper arm) and what is not (back, belly, thigh).
2
Locate the Quranic dalil
The verse from Surah al-Nur 24:31 is cited as the primary proof.
3
Interpret 'adornment' as places of adornment
Explain why it means the site of adornment, not the adornment itself, based on context and custom.
4
Understand the practical and desire-based reasoning
Why custom (entering without permission) and absence of desire permit this leniency.
5
Define mahram and note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Who qualifies as a mahram, including the different view on mahram through zina and which view is preferred.
The first view (considering him a mahram) is considered more correct.

قال رحمه الله : (ووجه محرمه ورأسها فصدرها وساقها وعضدها لا إلى ظهرها وبطنها وفخذها) يعني يجوز النظر إلى وجه محرمه إلى آخره، ولا يجوز إلى ظهرها إلى آخر ما ذكر والأصل فيه قوله تعالى ولا يبدين زينتهن إلا
