He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (And the face of his mahram, her head, her chest, her shin, and her upper arm, but not her back, belly, and thigh.) This means it is permissible to look at the face of his mahram, etc., and not permissible to look at her back, etc. The basis for this is His saying (the Most High): 'And let them not display their adornment except to their husbands or their fathers' (Surah al-Nur 24:31) – the verse. It does not mean the adornment itself, because looking at the adornment itself is permissible, but rather the place of adornment. So the head is the place of the crown and hair, the face is the place of kohl, the neck and chest are the place of the necklace, the ear is the place of the earring, the upper arm is the place of the bracelet, the forearm is the place of the bangle, the hand is the place of the ring and henna, the shin is the place of the anklet, and the foot is the place of henna. Unlike the back, belly, and thigh, because they are not places of adornment, and because some enter upon others without permission or formality, and a woman in her house wears casual clothes and is not usually covered. If she were commanded to cover from her mahrams, it would cause great hardship. And desire is absent among mahrams, unlike an ajnabi (non-mahram). The mahram is one whose marriage is permanently forbidden due to blood relation or cause, like fosterage and in-law relations, even if through zina. And it is said if the prohibition of in-law is established through zina, it is not permissible for him to look at what was mentioned, like an ajnabi, because the prohibition for him is by way of punishment, not blessing, so it does not apply in what we mentioned. The first is more correct, considering the reality. And you may say: It is more appropriate not to mention the thigh here, because it is known that it is not permissible for a mahram to look at what a man is not allowed to look at from a man, by a fortiori argument, because looking at the opposite gender is more severe. If you say: The purpose of mentioning the thigh is to clarify the reality and to explicitly state what is known from what preceded necessarily, I say: If that is the intent, then it is more appropriate to mention the knee instead of the thigh, because the ruling of 'awrah for the knee is lighter than for the thigh, and for the thigh it is lighter than for the private parts. By mentioning the thigh, the ruling for the knee being lighter is not known. But by mentioning the knee, the ruling for the thigh and private parts is known a fortiori, because they are stronger in prohibition of looking. The commentator and the author of al-Nihayah and al-Mujtaba used the implication of the verse as evidence for permissibility and prohibition. It was objected that the verse only indicates permissibility, not prohibition. And the better view, as in al-Bada'i', is to use as evidence His saying: 'Say to the believing men to lower their gazes' (al-Nur 24:30), except that He permitted mahrams to look at apparent and hidden places of adornment by His saying: 'And let them not display their adornment...' Some later scholars objected to the rational proof, which is our saying: 'They enter without seeking permission,' because what is mentioned in al-Bada'i' is that mahrams do not enter upon them without permission, lest they be exposed and his gaze fall on her private parts, and that would be disliked. But this is inattentiveness, because what is meant is that seeking permission is not obligatory, not that it is not recommended. He said in al-Bada'i': It is not permissible for a man to enter another's house without permission, even if it is his mahram; so he should not enter without permission, except that the matter of permission for mahrams is easier and simpler. So it is concluded from his statement that entering is...
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the ruling on a man looking at his mahram women. The matn states that a man may look at his mahram's face, head, chest, shins, and upper arms, but not her back, belly, or thighs. The author provides the Quranic evidence from Surah al-Nur (24:31) where Allah commands women not to display their adornment except to their husbands and certain relatives. He explains that 'adornment' here means the places where adornment is worn, not the adornment itself. Thus, the places of adornment (like head for crown, face for kohl, etc.) are permissible to show, while areas that are not adornment sites (back, belly, thigh) are not. Additionally, there is a practical reasoning: mahrams often enter without formal permission and women are in casual dress at home, so strict covering would cause hardship. Furthermore, desire among mahrams is usually absent, unlike with ajnabi (non-mahrams). The author then discusses who qualifies as a mahram: someone permanently forbidden to marry due to blood, fosterage, or in-law relations. There is a difference of opinion if the in-law relationship is established through zina: some say such a person is treated as an ajnabi because the prohibition is a punishment. The author prefers the first view (considering him a mahram). The passage then engages in a subtle discussion about why the thigh is mentioned. Some argue it was unnecessary because it is already known that a man cannot look at another man's thigh, and looking at a mahram's thigh is even more severe. But the author explains that mentioning the thigh helps clarify the gradation of 'awrah: the knee is lighter than the thigh, and the thigh is lighter than the private parts. Therefore, it would have been better to mention the knee to indicate the lighter ruling, but mentioning the thigh is still informative. Finally, the author critiques the use of the verse as evidence for both permissibility and prohibition, stating that the better approach is to combine the command to lower gaze with the exception for mahrams. He also responds to an objection about the rational proof that mahrams enter without permission, clarifying that the Hanafi position is that seeking permission is not obligatory but still recommended, and entering without permission is not allowed if there is fear of seeing exposed 'awrah.

Key Terms
المحرم (al-mahram): A relative whom one is permanently forbidden to marry due to blood, fosterage, or in-law relationship.
الزينة (al-zinah): Adornment; in this context, it refers to the places of adornment (e.g., head for crown, face for kohl), not the adornment itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Her head
C) Her thigh
D) Her shin

Key Takeaway
A man may look at a mahram's face, head, chest, shins, and upper arms; the prohibition includes back, belly, and thigh, based on Quran and custom.

intermediate
8 min
Ruling on Looking at Mahram Women: Permissible and Impermissible Parts
States the ruling on permissible and impermissible parts of a mahram woman to look at

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which parts of a mahram woman are permissible to look at according to the Hanafi school, derive the ruling from Surah al-Nur:31, and identify the basis for the prohibition of other parts.

Core Idea
Looking at a mahram is limited to places of adornment; back, belly, and thigh are not permitted.

This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the Hanafi position on looking at mahram women. While the Quran (24:31) commands women not to reveal their adornment except to certain relatives, the school interprets 'adornment' as the places where adornment is worn, not the adornment itself. This yields a specific list of permissible body parts and a rational basis rooted in everyday practice and absence of desire.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal category
The act is 'looking at a mahram woman'. This is a social interaction with a ruling on permissibility. The text distinguishes between permissible parts (face, head, chest, shin, upper arm) and impermissible parts (back, belly, thigh). The overall hukm for permissible parts is mubah; for the others, it is haram or makruh depending on context.
The ruling is not about obligation but about boundaries of permissible sight.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The Quranic verse Surah al-Nur:31 is cited: 'And let them not reveal their adornment except to their husbands or their fathers...' The Hanafi school uses this as the primary proof, interpreting 'zina' (adornment) as the places of adornment.
The verse is qati al-thubut but zanni al-dalala, allowing interpretive scope.
3
Analyse the wajh al-dalalah
The word 'zina' in the verse does not refer to the adornment itself (e.g., jewelry) but to the body parts where adornments are worn. The head is the place of the crown, the face of kohl, the neck of the necklace, etc. Back, belly, and thigh are not places of adornment, hence not included in the exception.
This interpretation is key: it limits permissible exposure to specific areas.
4
Consider the rational basis (ta'lil)
Two rational arguments support the ruling: (1) Hardship (mashaqqa) – women at home are in casual dress and would face great difficulty if required to cover fully from mahrams. (2) Absence of desire (shahwa) – mahrams are not expected to feel sexual desire towards each other, so the normal strictness of hijab is relaxed.
These reasons also explain why back, belly, and thigh are excluded: they are not typical adornment places and are more sensitive.
5
Note critical discussions within the school
The passage includes a discussion on mentioning the thigh versus the knee, and a dispute on whether mahram by zina (illegitimate relation) is treated differently. Also, the permissibility of entering without permission is debated: some say not obligatory to seek permission, others say makruh. These nuances help apply the ruling precisely.
The text also responds to an objection that the verse indicates permissibility only, not prohibition. The Hanafi response uses Q. 24:30 (lowering gaze) as the general rule, with the exception for mahrams.

Source Text

كتاب الكراهية
وساقها وعضدها لا إلى ظهرها وبطنها وفخذها ويمس ما يحل له النظر إليه وأمة غيره مطلقا بنتها فلا يحل له النظر إلى فرجها. وفي الينابيع : ولا يحل له أن يأتي زوجته في الدبر إلا عند أصحاب الظاهر وهو خلاف الإجماع قال رحمه الله : (ووجه محرمه ورأسها فصدرها وساقها وعضدها لا إلى ظهرها وبطنها وفخذها) يعني يجوز النظر إلى وجه محرمه إلى آخره، ولا يجوز إلى ظهرها إلى آخر ما ذكر والأصل فيه قوله تعالى ولا يبدين زينتهن إلا لبعولتهن أو أبائهن [النور: ۳۱] الآية. ولم يرد به نفس الزينة لأن النظر إلى عين الزينة مباح ولكن المراد موضع الزينة، فالرأس موضع التاج والشعور، والوجه موضع الكحل، والعنق والصدر موضع القلادة، والأذن موضع القرط، والعضد موضع الدملج، والساعد موضع السوار، والكف موضع الخاتم والخضاب والساق موضع الخلخال، والقدم موضع الخضاب بخلاف الظهر والبطن والفخذ لأنها ليست بمواضع الزينة ولأن البعض يدخل على البعض من غير استئذان ولا احتشام والمرأة تكون في بيتها في ثياب بذلة ولا تكون مستورة عادة، فلو أمرت بالستر من محارمها لحرجت حرجاً عظيماً والشهوة فيهن منعدمة من المحارم بخلاف الأجنبي والمحرم من لا يحل نكاحها على التأييد بنسب ولا سبب كالرضاع والمصاهرة وإن كان بالزنا، وقيل إن كانت حرمة المصاهرة ثابتة بالزنا لا يجوز له النظر إلى ما ذكر كالأجنبي لأن الحرمة في حقه بطريق العقوبة لا بطريق النعمة فلا يظهر فيما ذكرنا، والأول أصح اعتباراً للحقيقة. ولك أن تقول : الأنسب أن لا يذكر الفخذ هنا لأنه علم عدم جواز نظر المحرم إلى من عدم جواز نظر الرجل إلى الرجل فيه بطريق الأولى لأن نظر الجنس إلى خلاف الجنس هذا فيه أغلظ . فإن قلت : المقصود من ذكر الفخذ بيان الواقع والتصريح بما علم مما تقدم التزاماً قلت : إن كان هذا هو المراد فالأنسب أن يذكر الركبة بدل الفخذ لأن حكم العورة في الركبة أخف منه في الفخذ وفي الفخذ أخف منه في السوأة فبذكر الفخذ لا يعلم حكم الركبة بكونها أخف، وأما بذكر الركبة فيعلم حكم الفخذ والسوأة بالأولى لأنهما أقوى منها في حرمه النظر. واستدل الشارح وصاحب النهاية والمجتبى على الحل والحرمة لملابسة الآية التقدير، واعترض بأن الآية إنما تدل على الحل لا الحرمة والأولى كما في البدائع الاستدلال بقوله تعالى قل للمؤمنين يغضوا من أبصارهم [النور: ٣٠] إلا أنه رخص للمحارم النظر إلى موضع الزينة الظاهرة والباطنة بقوله تعالى ولا يبدين زينتهن الآية. واعترض بعض المتأخرين على الدليل العقلي وهو قولنا يدخل من غير استئذان لما ذكر في البدائع أن المحارم لا تدخل عليهن من غير استئذان ربما كانت مكشوفة العورة فيقع بصره عليها فيكره له ذلك وهذا غفلة منه لأن المراد أن لا يجب عليه الاستئذان لا الندب قال في البدائع : لا يحل للرجل أن يدخل بيت غيره من غير استئذان وإن كان من محارمه فلا يدخل من غير استئذان إلا أن الأمر في الاستئذان على المحارم أيسر وأسهل فتلخص من عبارته أن الدخول في بيت الأجنبي من غير استئذان حرام وفي بيت محارمه من غير استئذان مكروه والله الموفق. ثم قال تاج الشريعة فإن قلت : إذا جاز الدخول من غير استئذان فعلى هذا ينبغي أن لا يقطع إذا سرق من بيت أمه من الرضاع الجواز ما ذكرنا لنقصان الحرز في حقه قلت : لا يقطع عند البعض، وأما جواز الدخول عليها من غير استئذان ممنوع ؛ ذكره خواهر زاده أن المحارم من حيث الرضاع لا يكون لهم الدخول عليها من غير استئذان ولهذا يقطعون بعض اهـ كلامه ولك أن تقول : ليس هذا الجواب بتام أما كونه لا يقطع عند البعض فهو قول أبي يوسف، وعلى قولهما يقطع وهو المختار بظاهر الرواية، وقد تقدم السارق في باب السرقة لأن الحرز في حقهم كامل اهـ.
Book of Disliked Matters. And her shin and upper arm not to her back and belly and thigh, and he may touch what it is permissible for him to look at, and the slave-girl of another absolutely; her daughter, so it is not permissible for him to look at her private part. And in al-Yanabi': It is not permissible for him to come to his wife in the anus except by the Zahiri school, and that is contrary to consensus. He (the author) said: (And the face of his mahram, her head, her chest, her shin, and her upper arm, not to her back, belly, and thigh) meaning it is permissible to look at the face of his mahram etc., and it is not permissible to look at her back etc. The basis for this is the saying of Allah Most High: 'And let them not reveal their adornment except to their husbands or their fathers' [al-Nur: 31] etc. He did not mean the adornment itself because looking at the actual adornment is permissible, but what is intended is the place of adornment. So the head is the place of the crown and hair, the face is the place of kohl, the neck and chest are the place of the necklace, the ear is the place of the earring, the upper arm is the place of the bracelet, the forearm is the place of the bangle, the hand is the place of the ring and henna, the shin is the place of the anklet, and the foot is the place of henna, unlike the back, belly, and thigh because they are not places of adornment. And because one enters upon another without permission and without shyness, and the woman is in her house in casual clothes and is not usually covered, so if she were commanded to cover from her mahrams, she would be in great hardship. And desire regarding them is absent from mahrams, unlike a non-mahram. And a mahram is one whose marriage is permanently prohibited by lineage or cause such as rada' and musahara, even if through zina. And it is said that if the prohibition of musahara is established through zina, it is not permissible for him to look at what was mentioned like a non-mahram, because the prohibition in his case is by way of punishment not favor, so it does not apply to what we mentioned. And the first is more correct considering the reality. And you may say: It is more appropriate not to mention the thigh here because it is known from the lack of permissibility of a mahram looking at what a man is not permitted to look at a man, by primary reasoning, because looking at the opposite gender is more severe. If you say: The purpose of mentioning the thigh is to state the reality and to clarify what is known from what preceded by implication. I say: If this is intended, then it is more appropriate to mention the knee instead of the thigh because the ruling of the 'awra of the knee is lighter than the thigh, and the thigh is lighter than the private parts. So by mentioning the thigh, the ruling of the knee being lighter is not known, whereas by mentioning the knee, the ruling of the thigh and private parts is known by primary reasoning because they are stronger in prohibition. And the commentator and the author of al-Nihaya and al-Mujtaba deduced permissibility and prohibition from the apparent meaning of the verse, and it was objected that the verse only indicates permissibility not prohibition. And it is better, as in al-Bada'i, to deduce from His saying 'Say to the believing men that they lower their gaze' [al-Nur: 30], except that He permitted mahrams to look at the places of outward and inward adornment by His saying 'and let them not reveal their adornment' etc. And some later scholars objected to the rational proof, which is our saying 'he enters without permission', because it is mentioned in al-Bada'i that mahrams do not enter upon them without permission perhaps she might be uncovered of the 'awra and his gaze would fall upon it, and that is disliked for him. This is negligence from him because what is meant is that it is not obligatory upon him to seek permission, not recommended. He said in al-Bada'i: It is not permissible for a man to enter the house of another without permission, even if he is one of her mahrams, so he should not enter without permission, except that the matter of seeking permission for mahrams is easier and simpler. So from his expression it becomes clear that entering the house of a non-mahram without permission is haram, and entering the house of his mahram without permission is makruh. And Allah is the Provider of success. Then Taj al-Shari'ah said: If you say: If entering without permission is permissible, then accordingly a person should not be cut off if he steals from the house of his mother from rada' due to the deficiency of the protection in his right. I say: He is not cut off according to some. As for the permissibility of entering upon her without permission, it is denied; Khahar Zadeh mentioned that mahrams by rada' do not have the right to enter upon her without permission, and for that reason they are cut off? End of his speech. And you may say: This answer is not complete. As for his not being cut off according to some, that is the saying of Abu Yusuf, and according to the two of them he is cut off, and that is the chosen view in the apparent transmission. And the thief has already been discussed in the chapter of theft because the protection in their right is complete. End.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi school concerning the limits of looking at mahram women. The matn states that a man may look at the face, head, chest, shin, and upper arm of his mahram, but not at the back, belly, or thigh. The commentator explains that the basis is the Quranic verse 24:31, which commands women not to reveal their adornment except to certain relatives. He clarifies that 'adornment' here means the places of adornment, not the adornment itself. Thus, the head is where a crown is worn, the face where kohl is applied, etc. The back, belly, and thigh are not places of adornment and thus are not permitted. The commentator also addresses the point that mahrams enter without permission, so requiring full covering would cause hardship. There is a discussion of whether the thigh should be included, with some saying mentioning the knee would be more appropriate. The passage also includes a dispute with the Zahiri school regarding anal intercourse, which is rejected by consensus. A later objection about the proof is addressed, clarifying that the verse indeed indicates permission, not prohibition, and the stronger proof is the command to lower the gaze with an exception for mahrams. The passage concludes with a discussion on entering without permission, distinguishing between non-mahram (haram) and mahram (makruh).

Key Terms
محرم (mahram): A person whom one is permanently forbidden to marry due to lineage, rada' (fosterage), or musahara (marriage-based affinity).
زينة (zīnah): In the verse, it means the places where adornment is worn, not the adornment itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following parts of a mahram woman is a man NOT permitted to look at?
A) Her face
B) Her chest
C) Her thigh
D) Her shin

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits looking at a mahram's face, head, chest, shin, and upper arm based on the Quran's mention of adornment places, prohibiting back, belly, and thigh.

Ruling on Touching Mahram and Related Matters
Provides the ruling on touching mahram women, kissing, embracing, and traveling with them

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on physical contact with mahram and non-mahram, and identify the conditions that affect permissibility.

Core Idea
Physical contact with a mahram is permissible if safe from desire, but becomes prohibited if desire is feared.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on touching, kissing, embracing, and secluding with mahram relatives. The author cites hadith evidence to distinguish between permissible interaction and prohibited intimacy. Understanding these rulings is essential for proper social conduct within Islamic guidelines.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their default ruling
List the acts discussed: touching mahram, kissing mahram's head, seclusion with mahram, traveling with mahram, and general greetings like shaking hands, kissing, and embracing non-mahram.
Default ruling for mahram: permissible (mubah) unless fear of desire.
2
Locate the primary dalil for each act
The Prophet kissed Fatima's head, showing permissibility. For seclusion, the hadith 'No man secludes with a woman...' is interpreted to exclude mahram. For traveling, the hadith about not traveling without a mahram implies permission with one.
The hadith about limbs committing zina is used to emphasize avoiding all forms when desire is feared.
3
Analyze the condition that changes the ruling
Fear of desire (shahwa) for oneself or the mahram transforms the permissibility into prohibition. The text says to avoid touch, look, and seclusion when desire is feared.
This condition applies to all acts, including shaking hands with non-mahram.
4
Apply the principle to non-mahram interactions
For non-mahram, shaking hands (musafaha) is permissible if safe from desire and intended for honor. Kissing and embracing are disliked (makruh) as the Prophet refused them. The hadith about shaking hands is interpreted with this condition.
The text cites 'our scholars' (mashayikhuna) for this interpretation.
5
Note the special ruling for shaking hands and other forms
Shaking hands is a sunnah practice, while kissing hands of scholars and just rulers is mentioned in al-Nawadir as permissible. The overall principle is that any physical contact that may lead to desire must be avoided.
This balance between need and precaution is characteristic of Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ويمس ما يحل له النظر إليه) يعني يجوز ما حل له النظر إليه من محارمه ومن الرجل لا من الأجنبية لتحقق الحاجة إلى ذلك من المسافرة والمخالطة، وكان عليه الصلاة والسلام يقبل رأس فاطمة ويقول أجد منها ريح الجنة وقال «من قبل رأس أمه فكأنما قبل عتبه الجنة ولا بأس بالخلوة معها لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام «لا يخلون رجل بامرأة ليس منها سبيل فإن ثالثهما الشيطان والمراد إذا لم تكن محرماً لأن المحرم بسبيل منها إلا إذا خاف على نفسه أو عليها الشهوة فحينئذ لا يمسها ولا ينظر إليها ولا يخلو بها لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام العينان يزنيان وزناهما النظر واليدان يزنيان وزناهما البطش، والرجلان يزنيان وزناهما المشي، والفرج يصدق ذلك أو يكذبه فكان في كل واحد منها زنا والزنا محرم أنواعه بجميع وحرمه الزنا بالمحارم أشد وأغلظ فيجتنب الكل ولا بأس بالمسافرة بهن لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام لا تسافر المرأة فوق ثلاثة أيام إلا بزوج أو محرم» وإن احتاجت إلى الإركاب والإنزال فلا بأس أن يمسها من وراء ثيابها ويأخذ ظهرها وبطنها من وراء إذا أمنا الشهوة، وإن خاف عليها أو على نفسه أو ظناً أو شكاً فليجتنب ذلك بجهده، فإن أمكنها الركوب بنفسها تمتنع من ذلك أصلاً، وإن لم يمكنها تتلفف بالثياب كي لا تصل حرارة عضوها إلى عضوه، وإن لم تجد الثياب فليدفع عن نفسه بقدر الإمكان. ولا بأس بأن يدخل على الزوجين محارمهما وهما في الفراش من غير وطء باستئذان وكذا الخادم حين يخلو الرجل بأهله، وكذا الأمة ويكره أن يأخذها بيده ويدخلها ويعلم الناس أنه يريدها اهـ. (فروع) قال في الجامع الصغير ويكره تقبيل غيره ومعانقته ولا بأس بالمصافحة لما أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام سئل أيقبل بعضنا بعضاً ؟ قال : لا. قالوا: ويعانق بعضنا بعضاً؟ قال: لا. قالوا : أيصافح بعضنا بعضاً؟ قال : نعم . قال مشايخنا: إن كان يأمن على من الشهوة وقصد البر والإكرام وتعظيم المسلم فلا بأس به والحديث محمول على هذا التفصيل المصافحة سنة قديمة متوارثة وفي النوادر : وتقبيل يد العالم والسلطان العادل لا
He (the author) said: (And he may touch what it is permissible for him to look at) meaning it is permissible for him to touch what it is permissible for him to look at of his mahrams and of a man, not of a non-mahram woman due to the confirmed need for that in travel and mixing. And the Prophet (peace be upon him) used to kiss the head of Fatima and say 'I smell the scent of Paradise from her.' And he said: 'Whoever kisses the head of his mother, it is as if he kissed the threshold of Paradise.' And there is no harm in seclusion with her due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'A man should not seclude himself with a woman from whom he is not of the way, for indeed the third of them is Satan.' And the meaning is when she is not a mahram, because the mahram is of her way, except when he fears desire for himself or for her, then he should not touch her, nor look at her, nor seclude with her due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'The two eyes commit zina and their zina is the look, the two hands commit zina and their zina is the striking, the two feet commit zina and their zina is walking, and the private part confirms that or denies it.' So each of them has zina, and zina is haram in all its types, and the prohibition of zina with mahrams is more severe and more grave. So one should avoid all. And there is no harm in traveling with them due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'A woman should not travel more than three days except with a husband or a mahram.' And if she needs to be mounted and dismounted, there is no harm in touching her from behind her garments and taking her back and belly from behind if they are safe from desire. But if he fears for her or for himself, or thinks or doubts, then he should avoid that with all his effort. If she can mount by herself, he should refrain from that entirely. And if she cannot, she should wrap herself in clothes so that the heat of her limb does not reach his limb. And if she cannot find clothes, then he should ward off as much as possible. And there is no harm in mahrams entering upon the spouses while they are in bed without intercourse with permission, and likewise the servant when the man is alone with his wife, and likewise the slave-girl. And it is disliked that he takes her by the hand and enters her and people know that he wants her. End. (Branches) He said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: It is disliked to kiss another and embrace him, but there is no harm in shaking hands because the Prophet (peace be upon him) was asked: 'Should we kiss one another?' He said: 'No.' They said: 'Should we embrace one another?' He said: 'No.' They said: 'Should we shake hands with one another?' He said: 'Yes.' Our scholars said: If he is safe from desire and intends piety and honor and respect for the Muslim, then there is no harm in it. And the hadith is interpreted with this detail. Shaking hands is an ancient inherited sunnah. And in al-Nawadir: kissing the hand of a scholar and a just ruler is not...
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage provides the Hanafi ruling on touching mahram women and related interactions. The author states that it is permissible to touch a mahram in areas where it is permissible to look, due to need in travel and daily interaction. The Prophet's kissing of Fatima's head is cited as evidence. Seclusion with a mahram is generally permissible unless there is fear of desire, in which case it becomes prohibited. The hadith about the limbs committing zina is referenced to emphasize avoiding all forms of improper interaction. Traveling with a mahram is permissible up to three days without a mahram? Actually the hadith says a woman should not travel without a mahram. For mounting and dismounting, contact through clothes is allowed if safe from desire. The passage also discusses entering upon spouses, and rules for servants and slave-girls. Then it branches to general rules: kissing and embracing non-mahram are disliked, but shaking hands is permissible with conditions. The text ends abruptly, possibly discussing kissing a scholar's hand.

Key Terms
خلوة (khalwah): Seclusion with a member of the opposite gender. Permissible with mahram unless fear of desire.
مصافحة (musafaha): Handshake between men; permissible if safe from desire and intended for honor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on shaking hands with a non-mahram woman?
A) It is always permissible because the Prophet shook hands with women
B) It is never permissible because it leads to zina
C) It is permissible if the man is safe from desire and intends respect
D) It is permissible only through a barrier

Key Takeaway
Physical interaction with mahram and non-mahram in the Hanafi school is governed by the condition of safety from desire; where desire is absent, permissible contact is allowed for genuine need and respect.

Rulings on a Female Slave's Own Male Slave: Foreign Man or Mahram?
This passage clarifies that a female slave's own male slave is treated as a foreign man.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a female slave's male slave, explain the Hanafi proof, and compare it with the Shafi'i/Maliki position.

Core Idea
A male slave owned by a female slave is a foreign man, not a mahram, because desire is present and need is minimal.

This passage addresses a specific question: is a male slave owned by a female slave considered a mahram, like a brother or father, or a foreign man? Understanding this ruling clarifies the boundaries of hijab between a female slave and her own male slave. The Hanafi school takes a firm position based on a careful reading of the primary sources, contrary to the Shafi'i and Maliki schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
The act in question is a female slave's own male slave looking at her. The ruling: he is like a foreign man, so looking is restricted.
The ruling applies to both the female slave's obligation to cover and the male slave's restriction of gaze.
2
Understand the Hanafi Position and Its Evidence
The Hanafi position rests on the absence of a mahram relationship and the presence of desire; they cite Quran 4:3 but interpret it as referring to female slaves only. Note the reliance on statements of early scholars like Sa'id ibn Jubayr.
The key phrase is 'the verse is about female slaves, not male slaves.'
3
Recognize the Opposing School's Argument
Imam Malik and Imam al-Shafi'i consider the same verse to include male slaves owned by a female slave, making them mahrams. Understand their basis: the general wording 'what your right hands possess.'
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf in tafsir.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi Response
The Hanafis respond by pointing to the practical context and the early scholars' interpretation. The verse in Surah al-Nur is specifically about female slaves. The male slave works outside the home, so need for seeing her is minimal, and desire is present.
This is a classic example of specification of a general text.
5
Note the Practical Consequence
The practical consequence: under Hanafi law, a female slave must observe hijab from her own male slave as from any foreign man. In the Shafi'i/Maliki view, she may appear before him without hijab.
This difference matters in daily practice.

قال رحمه الله : (وعبدها كالأجنبي من الرجال) حتى لا يجوز لها أن تبدي زينتها له إلا ما يجوز أن تبديه للأجنبي، ولا يحل له أن ينظر من سيدته إلا ما يجوز له أن ينظر إليه من الأجنبية. قال الإمام مالك والشافعي : نظره إليها كنظر الرجل إلى محارمه لقوله تعالى أو ما مكلت أيمانكم [النساء: ٤] ولنا أنه محل غير محرم ولا زوج والشهوة متحققة والحاجة قاصرة لأنه يعمل خارج البيت والآية واردة في الإماء. قال سعيد بن جبير وسعيد بن المسيب والحسن لا يغرنكم سورة النور فإنها واردة في الإناث لا في الذكور ولهذا لا يجوز لها أن تسافر معه لأنه أجنبي عنها. وفي المحيط والعبد في النظر إلى سيدته التي لا قرابة بينه وبينها بمنزلة الرجل الأجنبي سواء كان العبد خصياً أو مجبوباً أو فحلاً. وفي قاضيخان : وللعبد أن يدخل على سيدته بغير إذنها بالإجماع. قال رحمه الله : (ويعزل عن أمته بلا إذنها وعن زوجته بإذنها) يعني لو وطئ أمته فله إذا أراد الإنزال أن ينزل خارج فرجها بغير إذنها، أما الزوجة فليس له ذلك إلا بإذنها لأنه عليه الصلاة والسلام نهى عن العزل عن الحرة إلا بإذنها، ولأن الحرة لها حق في الوطء حتى كان لها المطالبة به قضاء لشهوتها وتحصيلاً للولد ولهذا تخير في الجب والعنة ولا حق للأمة في الوطء والعزل لما ذكرنا. ولو كانت تحته أمة غيره فقد ذكرنا حكمه في النكاح لا يقال هذه مكررة مع قوله في النكاح والاذن في العزل لسيد الأمة لأنا نقول : ذاك في الأمة المتزوجة وهذا في الأمة المطوءة بملك
He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'And her slave is like a foreign man from among men.' So it is not permissible for her to display her adornment to him except what she may display to a foreign man, and it is not permissible for him to look at his mistress except what he may look at from a foreign woman. Imam Malik and al-Shafi'i said: His looking at her is like a man's looking at his mahrams due to His statement 'or what your right hands possess' [4:3]. But for us (Hanafis), he is not a mahram nor a husband, and desire is present, and need is minimal because he works outside the house, and the verse is about female slaves. Sa'id ibn Jubayr, Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib, and al-Hasan said: Do not let Surah al-Nur deceive you; it was revealed concerning females, not males. For this reason, it is not permissible for her to travel with him because he is a foreign man. In al-Muhit: The slave in looking at his mistress with whom there is no kinship is like a foreign man, whether the slave is a eunuch, impotent, or a stallion. In Qadikhan: The slave may enter upon his mistress without her permission by consensus. He said: 'And he may perform 'azl (coitus interruptus) with his slave woman without her permission, and with his wife only with her permission.' Meaning if he has intercourse with his slave woman, he may, when he wants to ejaculate, ejaculate outside her vagina without her permission. As for the wife, he may not do that except with her permission, because the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade 'azl with a free woman except with her permission. And because the free woman has a right to intercourse, such that she may demand it in court for her desire and to obtain a child, and for that reason, he is given a choice in cases of impotence and incapacity. The slave woman has no right to intercourse or 'azl for what we mentioned. If he has a slave woman owned by another under him, we have mentioned its ruling in marriage. It should not be said that this is a repetition with his saying in marriage about permission for 'azl from the slave's master, because we say: That is about a married slave woman, this is about a slave woman possessed by ownership.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage covers two rulings. First, the ruling that a female slave's own male slave (i.e., a slave owned by a female slave) is legally like a foreign man (ajnabi) in terms of hijab. This means the female slave must observe hijab in front of him as she would with any non-mahram male. The Hanafi proof is that he is not a mahram (no prohibitive kinship) nor a husband, and desire is present. They reject the analogy to 'what your right hands possess' from Quran 4:3 (which the Shafi'is and Malikis use to say such a slave is like a mahram) because that verse refers to female slaves, not male slaves. The correct understanding is that a male slave is a foreign man, and thus a female slave may not show adornment to him beyond what is allowed for a foreign man, and he may not look at her except what a foreign man may look at. The second ruling is about 'azl (coitus interruptus): It is permissible for a man to perform 'azl with his own slave woman (umm walad or regular slave) without her permission. However, with his free wife, he must obtain her permission to perform 'azl. This is based on a hadith that the Prophet (SAW) forbade 'azl with a free woman without her permission. The reasoning is that a free wife has a right to intercourse and to bear children, whereas a slave woman has no such right. The passage distinguishes between a married slave woman (where 'azl permission is from her master) and a slave woman owned by the man himself.

Key Terms
عبدها ('abduha): A male slave owned by a female slave.
أجنبي (ajnabi): A foreign man with no marital or blood relationship that makes him a mahram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following statements is true about a female slave's male slave?
A) He is a mahram, so she does not need to cover from him.
B) He is like a foreign man, so she must cover from him.
C) He is like a foreign man, but she may travel with him.
D) He is a mahram, but only if he is not a eunuch.

Key Takeaway
A male slave owned by a female slave is not a mahram; hijab must be observed as with any foreign man.
